How To Do It Right? Exploring Crowdsourcing 
From A Management Perspective 
A Quantitative Analysis of Crowdsourcing Platform Data 
Master Thesis – M.Sc. Management & Technology (TUM & DTU 1:1) 
Technical University of Denmark. Technical University Munich. 
Supervised by 
1st Professor Jason Li-Ying. DTU Entrepreneurship. Centre for Technology 
Entrepreneurship.                              
2nd Professor Joachim Henkel. TUM School of Management. Dr. Theo Schöller-
Stiftungslehrstuhl für Technologie- und Innovationsmanagement.   
Submitted by 
Tobias Fudickar 
Tåsingegade 29 
2100 København 
t_fudickar@web.de 
 
Submitted on 
23.06.2023 in Copenhagen 
 
 
==PAGE:2==
II 
 
Abstract 
Crowdsourcing is a method of open innovation that combines the advantages of outsourcing 
and crowd involvement. Even though crowdsourcing is broadly established, many companies 
still struggle to reap its benefits. Thus, the first aim of this study was to investigate success 
factors in crowdsourcing. I used data from 1254 contests on two crowdsourcing platforms. I 
applied multivariate linear regressions to assess the relationships between crowdsourcing 
contest characteristics and the number of solvers as a surrogate parameter of crowdsourcing 
success. I found that an association with the ideation phase is positively and significantly related 
to the number of solvers. Some application fields are significantly related to the number of 
solvers based on the field characteristics. A disclosed seeker identity is positively and 
significantly related to the number of solvers. Further, identity disclosure moderates the 
relationship between some application fields and the number of solvers. The task text length is 
negatively and significantly related to the number of solvers. Finally, the award size is 
logarithmically and significantly related to the number of solvers. Further, the relationship 
between the award size and the number of solvers is moderated by application fields and time. 
The evaluation of crowdsourced ideas can be very costly. Thus, the second aim of this study 
was to investigate the potential of solver evaluations to reduce evaluation costs. I used data 
from 9329 contributions on a crowdsourcing platform. I applied multivariate logistical 
regressions to assess the relationships between solver evaluations and the contribution`s 
winning chance. I found that the solver evaluation scores, the number of likes, and the number 
of comments are positively and significantly related to the contribution`s winning chance. 
Further, I found that seekers might reduce their evaluation workload by 50% while maintaining 
90% accuracy by using solver evaluations. Finally, I conducted three interviews with industry 
professionals to estimate the real-world applicability of the findings. Although the interviews 
could not be statistically evaluated due to the small number, their results qualitatively confirmed 
this study`s relevance for crowdsourcing management.

1 
 
1 Introduction  
Crowdsourcing is an increasingly popular form of open innovation, outsourcing, and 
community management; the worldwide market size for crowdsourcing is rapidly growing 
(Industry Global News 24, 2022; Absolute Markets Insights, 2022), and scholars are 
increasingly interested in researching its mechanisms. In crowdsourcing, organizations post 
tasks on digital platforms that they either cannot solve internally; or that can be cheaper or more 
efficiently solved by amateurs than by employees or machines. The amateurs use their spare 
time to solve these tasks and get rewarded by the organizations for completing them 
successfully. Many established companies tried to profit from the potential of crowdsourcing. 
Some only once or a few times (e.g., BP, see Goldenberg, 2011), some multiple times (e.g., 
AstraZeneca, see Wazoku, 2023), and some made crowdsourcing a substantial part of their 
innovation and business strategy (e.g., LEGO, see PD, 2018).  
The term crowdsourcing, a combination of crowd and outsourcing, inherits the idea that one 
can combine the advantages of both approaches to create an efficient tool. It also illustrates the 
broad range of applications that the implementation of crowdsourcing principles has, from 
outsourcing to crowd, which has a loose transition to communities. Handing out simple work 
for micro-payments to a group of unidentified workers that take on these tasks, following first-
come-first-serve principles, is crowdsourcing. Encouraging a self-organizing community to 
discuss and develop solutions to specific problems is crowdsourcing. But, the most intuitive, 
the hosting of open idea contests by companies is also crowdsourcing. While the terminology`s 
broad range displays the flexibility and potential of crowdsourcing, it hampers the 
generalization of findings in the field.  
Thus, this study will focus on crowdsourcing for innovation as one specific type of 
crowdsourcing to ensure generalizability in a field. The specific research interest is the 
implementation of crowdsourcing competitions in the form of digital idea contests. Thus, it 
investigates the embedment of crowdsourcing in open innovation practices. The use of the term 
crowdsourcing in this paper, unless noted otherwise, always refers to crowdsourcing for 
innovation in the form of competitions.  
The appearance of crowdsourcing competitions can take multiple forms. But at its core, they 
are definable, and their implications for the crowdsourcing organization, and therefore for 
management (research), are very similar. Organizations initialize crowdsourcing competitions 
that seek a solution to a specific problem. Organizations of different forms (e.g., commercial, 
not-for-profit, and non-government) engage in crowdsourcing. In the following, I will refer to 
==PAGE:11==
2 
 
those organizations that set a task and search for its solution as seekers. Seekers post their tasks 
on a digital platform; some seekers use their proprietary crowdsourcing platforms (e.g., LEGO) 
or social media channels (e.g., IKEA), while others use so-called innovation intermediaries 
(e.g., Innocentive). These platforms handle the logistics of open innovation. In this case, they 
facilitate crowdsourcing competitions. The terms platform and innovation intermediary are 
interchangeably used in this study unless noted otherwise. Seekers use them to reach a crowd, 
as such platforms have usually built an active community. Organizations that could not build a 
community around their products before the competition or lack the reach needed to attract a 
sufficiently big crowd might prefer such platforms. A crowd consists of many potential 
contributors to a problem`s solving. Naturally, only a fraction of all crowd members become 
active contributors to a specific crowdsourcing competition. I further refer to the members that 
actively participate by contributing a solution to the problem as solvers. 
Seekers search for solutions to different problems, which call for rather specific outcomes (e.g., 
a method to make food more durable) or more open outcomes (e.g., an idea to improve a city 
park). In the following, I will refer to the problems seekers post on the platforms as tasks. 
Complementary to the task description, seekers often also provide information and data on the 
given task and evaluation criteria for the solutions. After the seeker publishes the task, the 
solvers have a, by the seeker ex-ante defined, timeframe to submit a contribution. I further refer 
to this period as the contest period. Usually, the solvers can complete the submission via the 
platform on which the seeker posted the task. Solutions can take on different forms depending 
on the requested solution type. Many crowdsourcing competitions focus on the ideation stage 
of innovation. Thus, a theoretical submission (e.g., in text form) is sufficient. However, some 
seekers demand more advanced solutions, proof-of-concept (e.g., via providing data), or even 
early prototypes. I further refer to all types of solution submissions as contributions. One should 
note that a solver can usually contribute multiple times in a crowdsourcing competition.  
After the contest period, the seeker takes a, by the seeker ex-ante defined, timeframe to evaluate 
the contributions and selects a winner or multiple winners. I further refer to this period as the 
evaluation period. Seekers can also crowdsource the evaluations or adopt a hybrid approach by 
providing the crowd with a voting function. Some seekers clearly define and publish ex-ante, 
which places they will give which prize. Other seekers only set a price pool and base the specific 
distribution on the contributions ex-post. Monetary rewards are the most common form of 
awards, but they can also be trips, vouchers, items, and more. Other prices entitle the winners 
to train at the seekers` facilities or funding for the solvers` projects. I further refer to the prices 
==PAGE:12==
3 
 
distributed by the seekers to the solvers as awards. After the evaluation period ends, the seeker 
announces the winners and distributes the awards to them. In return, the winning solvers mostly 
surrender their intellectual property rights on the winning contributions to the seeker or must 
provide them with a free usage license. The intellectual property rights for the losing 
contributions are not affected. I further refer to the crowdsourcing competitions defined above 
as contests.  
Crowdsourcing platforms choose different approaches to competition in contests. Some keep 
the submitted contributions secret, promote competition, and focus on protecting the intellectual 
property of the solvers. Other platforms seek a cooperative crowd structure by making 
submitted contributions public and providing the crowd with the functions to evaluate the 
contributions and, most importantly, to give feedback to the solvers. They might also promote 
a cooperative crowd structure by rewarding the Most Valuable Participant (MVP). The MVP is 
a participant that advanced other solvers` contributions by providing constructive feedback. An 
example of a tendential competitive platform is Innocentive (www.innocentive.com), and a 
tendential cooperative platform is HYVECrowd (www.hyvecrowd.com).  
The boundaries to engage in more crowdsourcing seem low. Seekers can start crowdsourcing 
as simply as using social media to post tasks and might substantially reduce the costs of 
evaluating the crowdsourced ideas by involving the crowd in the evaluation process. Moreover, 
the advantages of crowdsourcing are manifold. Among others, the seekers can gather many 
different solutions, develop their brands, and might reduce the costs of innovation. However, 
many companies failed in their crowdsourcing initiatives and did not pursue their ambitions 
further (Zetlin, 2016), while some managed to build broad crowds and organizational 
infrastructure to profit from ongoing crowdsourcing activities (PD, 2018). Such anecdotal data 
indicate that the management of crowdsourcing for innovation is by no means trivial. Some 
companies excel at it, while others fail drastically. 
Crowdsourcing can be a source of substantial competitive advantage, and companies that refuse 
to use it might forego a massive potential. However, if they want to be successful at 
crowdsourcing, they must be able to manage to crowdsource effectively. Not every company 
naturally possesses the capabilities to engage in successful crowdsourcing campaigns. Further, 
it is vague which capabilities are needed to be successful at crowdsourcing and which tactics 
are appropriate in what kind of situations. To summarize, many companies want (or need) to 
improve or establish crowdsourcing practices. Thus, management research on crowdsourcing 
must address the companies` challenges.  
==PAGE:13==
4 
 
Crowdsourcing research has focused mainly on qualitative and theoretical work and mostly 
failed to gather quantitative support for their hypotheses. That is, among others, a result of the 
shortage of overarching, comparable data, as crowdsourcing is still not performed very 
frequently. Only a few intermediaries have aggregated enough contests to obtain statistical 
relevance over time. Thus, one objective of this study is to fill various research gaps in the field 
of crowdsourcing through quantitative analysis. 
The first research gap, in the fields of application for crowdsourcing tasks, was suggested by 
Ghezzi et al. (2018) and was not adequately addressed since. They suggested that it is 
inadequately understood which fields of application suit crowdsourcing better than others. 
However, when seekers confront the choice to engage in crowdsourcing, they might have to 
consider their task`s application fields, as they might inhibit or facilitate the potential of 
crowdsourcing. An intuitive example of a field of application that might be inadequately suited 
for crowdsourcing might be rocket science because only a limited number of solvers might have 
capabilities that would suffice to solve a challenge in that area compared to, for example, 
business tasks. The second research gap, namely the practices to evoke the seekers` intrinsic 
and extrinsic motivation, was also indicated by Ghezzi et al. (2018) and has also not been 
adequately addressed since. Consistent with the general lack of quantitative studies in the field, 
they suggest that crowdsourcing research lacks appropriate investigations into the measures that 
seekers can use to motivate solvers to participate. Prior research established that solvers engage 
out of different motivations (Acar, 2019), but it is unclear how exactly seekers should design 
their contests to maximize the seekers` motivation to contribute. The third and final research 
gap that I am addressing focuses on how user involvement could reduce the costs of the 
evaluation phase. Prior research indicated that the crowd has the potential to evaluate the 
contributions very efficiently (Dahlander et al., 2019; Berg, 2016) but could not provide broad 
quantitative and generalizable evidence for the broader crowdsourcing context. Evaluating 
many contributions is very costly (Goldenberg, 2011), and usually, only a few are helpful 
(Archak and Sundararajan, 2009; Goldenberg, 2011). Therefore, the involvement of users in 
the evaluation process to reduce the number of contributions that a jury has to evaluate might 
substantially reduce the costs of crowdsourcing while maintaining a reasonably accurate 
evaluation. 
Thus, I am investigating the following research questions in this study: 
(1) In which fields of application and under which conditions do seekers gain the greatest 
advantages from using crowdsourcing? (Ghezzi et al., 2018) 
==PAGE:14==
5 
 
(2) Which practices allow seekers to draw on the solver`s intrinsic and extrinsic 
motivation to increase their participation in crowdsourcing sessions? (Ghezzi et al., 
2018) 
(3) To what extent could solver involvement affect the workload and accuracy of the 
evaluation? 
As crowdsourcing involves multiple theoretical fields, I will address a variety of theories in this 
investigation, e.g., the motivational theory, the concept of openness, and the wisdom of crowds. 
I will develop arguments for hypotheses based on the theories and the results from prior 
crowdsourcing and open innovation research, which are mostly qualitative. I will test the 
different hypotheses on multiple data sets. For the first two research questions, I will use data 
from 1174 contests from the arguably most popular crowdsourcing platform Innocentive and 
data which included 80 contests from a smaller, more open crowdsourcing platform 
(HYVECrowd). Based on these data, I analyzed the relationships between contest 
characteristics and the number of solvers. Consistent with prior research (DiPalantino and 
Vojnovic, 2009), I will use the number of solvers as an independent variable to indicate 
crowdsourcing potential and solver attraction. For the third research question, I will use data 
from 9329 contributions on the crowdsourcing platform HYVECrowd. Based on these data, I 
will analyze the relationships between solver evaluations and the contribution`s winning chance 
and the potential to predict the winning solutions using different types of solver evaluations. 
This investigation contributes to the existing crowdsourcing literature with many significant 
findings which also extend the quantification of crowdsourcing literature in general. First, it 
empirically supports the assumption that early innovation phases may be more suitable for 
crowdsourcing. Second, it finds significant differences between the potentials of crowdsourcing 
in different fields of application. Third, it finds that if seekers disclose their identity, they might 
attract more solvers under specific circumstances. Fourth, it supports the findings of 
DiPalantino and Vojnovic (2009) on the logarithmic relationship between the award size and 
the number of solvers and shows that this relationship is moderated by some application fields. 
Fifth, it finds a negative and highly significant relationship between the task overview length 
and the number of solvers. Sixth, it empirically supports the assumption that the seeker can 
substantially reduce their workload in the evaluation phase while maintaining reasonable 
evaluation accuracy if the solvers are involved in the evaluation process. Seventh, it provides 
compelling insights into crowds in different application fields and development stages. Eighth, 
==PAGE:15==
6 
 
it presents potential differences between the number of solvers and the number of contributions 
as proxies for crowdsourcing success in management research.  
Finally, the findings have important managerial implications. They suggest that managers must 
consider the innovation phase and the field of application while assessing the potential of using 
crowdsourcing in innovation processes. After deciding to engage in crowdsourcing, they must 
set their award size properly, should consider disclosing their identity, and try to shorten their 
task overview to attract more solvers. During the evaluation phase, they should consider using 
solvers` evaluations to shorten their evaluation phase and reduce costs. Finally, they should use 
the number of solvers on their tasks instead of the number of contributions to efficiently 
measure crowdsourcing participation.  
The remainder of this thesis is structured as follows. First, I will briefly summarize relevant 
literature on prior research. Second, I will present the theory and hypothesis development. 
Third, I will introduce the data and research methodology. Fourth, I will present the results. 
Fifth, I will discuss the findings and methods. Finally, I will present managerial implications, 
suggestions for further research, and conclusions. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:16==
7 
 
2 Prior Research  
One can address crowdsourcing research from multiple perspectives, e.g., the participant, the 
organization, and the system (Zhao and Zhu, 2014) or the task, the crowd, the platform, and the 
seeker (Karachiwalla and Pinkow, 2021). I will focus on the latter as it is more specific than the 
first. The goal of the following short literature review is not to be an exhaustive overview of 
crowdsourcing research but rather to provide a basis and context for the quantitative 
investigation. 
2.1 Crowdsourcing Definition and Embedment in the Management Literature 
Crowdsourcing is a broadly defined term that describes many forms of crowd involvement. 
Researchers came up with elaborate but lengthy and awkward definitions (Estellés-Arolas and 
González-Ladrón-de-Guevara, 2012).  However, most researchers use the white paper version 
definition of Howe (2006): "Crowdsourcing is the act of taking a job traditionally performed 
by a designated agent (usually an employee) and outsourcing it to an undefined, generally large 
group of people in the form of an open call.". Therefore, I will also follow this definition. I must 
note that Howe (2006) also provided a sound-byte version definition, which states that 
crowdsourcing is "the application of open-source principles to fields outside of software".  
Building on the sound-byte version definition, one can regard crowdsourcing as an 
improvement to open source (Brabham, 2008; Howe, 2006). Among others, the improvement 
stems from the fact that both the seekers and the solvers can reap monetary gains from 
crowdsourcing, which is mostly not the case in open-source projects (Brabham, 2008). 
Moreover, seekers can use crowdsourcing to apply advantages from open-source to non-digital 
products, as seekers can keep the intellectual property of crowdsourced ideas, which is essential 
for the production and development of these products (Brabham, 2008).  
Crowdsourcing can also be described as a hybrid of an open community approach and closed, 
commercialized approaches to innovation (Trompette et al., 2008). Seekers post the tasks 
openly to an uncontrolled community that approaches tasks as it likes. On the other hand, the 
seeker organization usually fully controls the development and commercialization of the 
invention after the crowdsourcing process is over. 
The emergence of crowdsourcing and its usage constituted a shift from formal, secure 
organizations focused on protection to embracing openness and uncertainty, highly comparable 
to the change to open innovation between companies (Powell, 2017; Chesbrough, 2006).  
==PAGE:17==
8 
 
I will focus on crowdsourcing in the context of open innovation, but as mentioned above, 
crowdsourcing is a broad term. The types of tasks are mainly two-folded, because seekers post 
very sophisticated, high-value (e.g., developing new chemicals) or small, repetitive, and easy 
tasks (e.g., counting birds) (Whitla, 2009). The first is linked closer to innovation initiatives. 
However, both task types are instances of a new and uncertain form of outsourcing labor to 
mostly amateurs (Corney et al., 2009; Ågerfalk and Fitzgerald, 2008; Howe, 2006).  
2.2 Why Seekers Engage in Crowdsourcing  
Crowdsourcing can be a helpful method for many purposes, e.g., to tackle a global crisis like 
the Covid-19 pandemic (Vermicelli et al., 2021), to improve the economic and social 
sustainability of cultural organizations (Cappa et al., 2020), to increase citizens` participation 
in public planning projects (Brabham, 2009), to improve and reduce costs of research activities 
(Borgo et al., 2018; Behrend et al., 2011), or to improve the effectiveness of disaster relief (Gao 
et al., 2011). However, I will focus on the use and benefits of crowdsourcing in the context of 
open innovation (Howe, 2006; 2008).  
The identification of lead users can be a notable side-effect of crowdsourcing. Research 
suggests that it might be even better than traditional lead user methods, as the lead users engage 
in self-selection ex-ante (Piller and Walcher, 2006). Lead users share similar needs with normal 
users but experience them earlier and thus profit more from innovation (Von Hippel, 1986). 
The same holds for the identification of potential future employees. Solvers can present their 
capabilities during crowdsourcing, and seekers can identify potential employees by their 
performance (Ebner et al., 2009; Ågerfalk and Fitzgerald, 2008).  
Crowdsourcing offers further broad potential beyond its obvious function of outsourcing labor 
to amateurs e.g., spill-overs into different areas of the seeker organization might occur. 
Crowdsourcing initiatives can lead to outcomes, such as building a community, increasing 
website traffic, and gaining attention (Murray et al., 2012; Sakas and Giannakopoulos, 2021). 
Also, investors assume that crowdsourcing initiatives of companies with high brand value and 
investment opportunities are most likely successful and, in turn, increase the company value 
(Cappa et al., 2019).  
However, the main goal of crowdsourcing is to solve tasks by using the capabilities of the 
crowd. The mainly targeted capabilities of the crowd in crowdsourcing for innovation (vs. other 
crowdsourcing forms) are not the often-mentioned wisdom of crowds that describes the 
potential of profiting from the average outcome of its contributions (e.g., at political elections) 
==PAGE:18==
9 
 
(Surowiecki, 2005), but the capabilities of the best individuals in the crowd (Dahlander and 
Piezunka, 2020; Archak and Sundararajan, 2009). 
The strengths of the crowd are mainly two-folded. On the one hand, crowdsourcing attracts 
amateurs with professional skills; on the other hand, it attracts a variety of backgrounds among 
the participants, profiting from the diversity trumps ability theorem (Howe, 2006; 2008). 
Especially as coming up with innovations is getting harder, companies seem to be increasingly 
dependent on external ideas (Jones, 2009).  Solvers from different fields do not just have diverse 
knowledge, but they have also different approaches to finding solutions (Fernandes and Simon, 
1999). Crowdsourcing can substantially decrease the costs of distant searches and, in turn, 
enable firms to reap its benefits more efficiently (Afuah and Tucci, 2012). Research showed 
even a positive relationship between the distance to the task`s field and the probability of 
winning. It is intuitive as the employees of the solver are experts in their field and could not 
solve the task sufficiently and therefore tried crowdsourcing (Jeppesen and Lakhani, 2010). 
Thus, solvers from the same field are also unlikely to find a solution. Seeker organizations 
seldom achieve sufficient diversity internally and might, in turn, miss solutions from distant 
fields.  
Ideas from crowdsourcing competitions are significantly more novel and have a higher 
customer benefit while being slightly less feasible than internally developed ideas (Poetz and 
Schreier, 2012). These observations do not just occur on average but also extend to the instance 
that crowdsourced ideas manage to be regularly among the best ideas in terms of novelty and 
customer benefits (Poetz and Schreier, 2012).  
Finally, seekers must consider what kind of submissions they want to provoke and how the 
quality of such contributions should be (Karachiwalla and Pinkow, 2021). But it is equally 
important to consider ways to attract capable solvers to provoke such contributions. Thus, the 
following parts will summarize crucial findings on the type of solvers and their motivations.  
2.3 Why Solvers Engage in Crowdsourcing  
Different factors motivate solvers to contribute to crowdsourcing contests, e.g., community 
affiliation, monetary gains, development of skills, and potential freelancing opportunities 
(Brabham, 2010).  
Kaufmann et al. (2011) showed that extrinsic motivation (e.g., monetary awards) influences the 
time spent on the platform significantly. Other research could strengthen this finding by 
showing that the number of contributions is positively related to the existence of monetary 
==PAGE:19==
10 
 
awards because it increases the perceived fairness; however, it is negatively related to the size 
of the monetary awards, as less confident solvers might be intimidated (Cappa et al., 2019). 
DiPalantino and Vojnovic (2009) found that participation increases logarithmically with 
increasing award size; however, they did not consider awards for multiple suggestions in their 
analysis. Mixed findings on the influence of monetary awards on motivation might be due to 
the differences in the analyzed crowdsourcing platforms and tasks. However, in line with most 
motivation research, intrinsic motivation (e.g., reputation, development, and further) prevails 
as the more important motivating factor (Ryan and Deci, 2000; Leimeister et al., 2009; Murray 
et al., 2012; Moser and Nicholas, 2013). Next to capability and monetary awards, recognition 
seems to be the most important motivating factor to continue working on innovations (Moser 
and Nicholas, 2013).  
Seekers must also consider perceived fairness in the context of intellectual property. As 
mentioned above, solvers transfer their intellectual property to the seeker in most 
crowdsourcing contests. The solver receives an ex-ante-specified award. However, one could 
argue that the definition of intellectual property in crowdsourcing should shift from a purely 
legal and monetary perspective to an ethical and social view, e.g., offering solvers public 
recognition for their ideas (Liu and Sheshtak, 2021). It would fit the most influential motivation 
factors of participants that they might not prioritize the legal intellectual property but would 
highly profit from the recognition by the seeker and the public (Moser and Nicholas, 2013). 
Further, the presence of a positive social goal in the crowdsourcing initiative is positively 
related to the number of contributions; therefore, intrinsic motivation can also be increased by 
presenting a greater purpose of the contest (Cappa et al., 2019). The same is true for the 
relationship between providing social information about other solvers (e.g., how much they 
contributed) and the number of contributions (Chen, 2010).  
Contradicting, Ullah, et al. (2021) found that extrinsic motivation might be more dominant, 
especially if solvers can generate long-term profits by keeping control of their intellectual 
property. While protection of the intellectual property of the solvers might be motivating, it is 
not in the interest of the seekers, as they want to commercialize the crowdsourced inventions. 
Therefore, most crowdsourcing contests have only one-time payments as their reward. Thus, 
this finding has less practical relevance than others. However, for some crowds (e.g., Amazon 
Mechanical Turk), a shift from mainly amateur solvers to solvers largely from developing 
countries (e.g., India) that consider the platform as their primary means to generate income can 
==PAGE:20==
11 
 
be observed (Ross et al., 2010), which substantially increases the importance of external 
motivation in crowdsourcing contests.    
While most users of crowdsourcing platforms become inactive after a few submissions, the 
group of active users tries to optimize its expected outcome by participating in challenges with 
fewer anticipated participants, even though this often leads to a decrease in their actual expected 
outcome (Yang et al., 2008).  
Finally, most research and companies consider the motivating factors for solvers (e.g., monetary 
rewards and feedback); however, it is equally important to consider the costs of the solvers 
(e.g., expenses for materials and time spent) ex-ante (Karachiwalla and Pinkow, 2021). 
2.4 Crowd Composition and Contest Design  
A comparably small group of solvers generates the most beneficial contributions in 
crowdsourcing contests. Thus, seekers should only reward a small fraction of them (Archak and 
Sundararajan, 2009; Yang et al., 2008). But, as solvers are risk averse, seekers must reward 
multiple of the best contributions to increase the expected outcome and the motivation of the 
solvers (Archak and Sundararajan, 2009).  
The domination of a small number of solvers in a contest can also be generalized to whole 
platforms. A few solvers participating in multiple contests generate the most productive outputs 
and win the most crowdsourcing contests (Yang et al., 2008).  
Crowds can differ in their internal dynamics, e.g., competitive vs. collaborative. Collaboration 
among the crowd is favorable if seekers aim for high-quality contributions to complex, ill-
defined tasks; however, competitive crowds are faster and equally effective for modular tasks, 
no matter how well-defined they are (Natalicchio et al., 2017). Moreover, many crowdsourcing 
methods exist (e.g., open vs. closed), and some might be fitting for tasks with specific 
characteristics (Vermicelli et al., 2021). Seekers must also consider the potential of social media 
channels (e.g., to increase reach and build a community) to facilitate their crowdsourcing 
initiatives (Gao et al., 2011).  
Finally, crowds can be different in their nature, and it matters to which crowd the problem is 
presented; but it is an inherent problem in crowdsourcing that the attracted crowd cannot be 
precisely specified ex-ante (Natalicchio et al., 2017; Howe, 2006). After trying to address the 
crowd with the most potential, seekers must appropriately design and set their tasks to provoke 
the desired contributions. Thus, the next part summarizes crucial findings on the task design.  
==PAGE:21==
12 
 
2.5 Task Design  
To attract more contributions from external solvers, seekers should consider proactive attention 
(sharing internally developed ideas with external participants) and reactive attention (reacting 
to the suggestions of the external participants) (Dahlander and Piezunka, 2014). The findings 
on proactive attention are somewhat counterintuitive for innovation research, as it assumes that 
exposure to an original idea decreases the solver`s fluency and increases only the originality of 
submitted ideas (Wang et al., 2018). This contrast in findings might show that the fluency of 
individual solvers does not matter for the overall number of contributions, as most solvers 
submit a number of ideas that is far lower than their fluency would allow them to, and a 
significant number of solvers might be inspired to contribute after receiving the proactive 
attention.  
Task instructions that are unbounded or prohibitive prevail over suggestive ones (Gillier et al., 
2018). They evoke more original and valuable ideas. Therefore, seekers should either not 
restrict the ideation or actively ask for contributions that differ from existing solutions (Gillier 
et al., 2018). These findings complement the research regarding the value of the diversity of 
crowds (Howe, 2006; 2008).   
Formulating a modular task can be more efficient than a complex, integrated task (Baumann 
and Siggelkow, 2013). One can reduce the number of modules by formulating bigger ones at 
the beginning and smaller specialized tasks later in the process; however, under severe time 
pressure, companies should prefer integrated tasks (Baumann and Siggelkow, 2013).  
Finally, Dahlander et al. (2019) argue that a sequential model (e.g., multiple rounds of ideation 
with in-between feedback) can streamline the solutions to achieve a good fit with the seeker`s 
capabilities and has the advantage that solvers can receive feedback. Feedback might be crucial 
to receive valuable contributions (Dahlander and Piezunka, 2014). However, a one-off model 
(e.g., one round of ideation) might evoke more radical innovations, as the company does not 
intervene, which might be beneficial to prevent an increase in the distant search, narrow 
attention phenomenon, see below (Dahlander and Piezunka, 2020). 
While seekers must design contests and tasks in line with the crowds they want to address, it 
might be equally important to be internally prepared for crowdsourcing. Thus, the next part 
summarizes crucial findings in the internal management of crowdsourcing.  
 
==PAGE:22==
13 
 
2.6 Internal Crowdsourcing Management  
If companies do not plan according to the different crowdsourcing stages, which one can divide 
into define, broadcast, attract and select, they often fail to embrace the opportunities that 
crowdsourcing provides (Dahlander et al., 2019; Dahlander and Piezunka, 2020). Other similar 
frameworks for crowdsourcing projects have been developed from an information system 
perspective (Bhatti et al., 2020; Modaresnezhad et al., 2020); however, I will follow the 
innovation literature. It is crucial to carefully consider ex-ante how many and which kind of 
resources the company wants to use to hold a successful crowdsourcing initiative, as the 
resource allocation for innovation often fails due to indivisibilities, in-appropriability, and 
uncertainty (Karachiwalla and Pinkow, 2021; Arrow, 1962).  
Critical problems in open innovation management also influence crowdsourcing, such as 
having the right personnel to collaborate with external sources, selecting the appropriate tasks 
to take outside the organizational boundaries, and formulating problems that lead to novel 
solutions (Sieg et al., 2010).  
Seekers that want to profit from crowdsourcing must, as with other open innovation initiatives, 
restructure the organization of their New Product Development (NPD) accordingly (Piller and 
Walcher, 2006). Especially the involvement of senior management beyond the NPD department 
seems crucial for company-wide open innovation and internal corporate venturing success 
(Huston and Sakkab, 2006; Burgelman, 1983). Beyond that, seekers must embed the integration 
and commercialization of crowdsourced ideas in the overall organization strategy. While 
crowdsourcing, as with other modes of openness, does not fit each organization, the ones that 
will successfully integrate themselves into the relevant crowdsourcing communities will prevail 
with a competitive advantage (Pisano and Verganti, 2008; Snow, 2011). Seekers do not need to 
rely exclusively on closed or open innovation, as they can also be complementary if the tasks 
are decomposable (Lakhani et al., 2013). Further, industries in which secrecy is less dominant 
(e.g., if patents work well) might reap more benefits from crowdsourcing, as revealing 
proprietary information to external players is crucial for open innovation and might be too risky 
in industries dominated by secrecy (Chesbrough, 2006). Finally, decisions to adopt 
crowdsourcing initiatives do not just depend on external circumstances but result mainly from 
an interpretation of the external circumstances based on an ex-ante established company culture 
that could prevent companies from considering crowdsourcing as a beneficial innovation 
method (Fayard et al., 2016).   
==PAGE:23==
14 
 
Transitions from closed to open innovation strategies lead to a drastic change in the roles of 
R&D professionals (Tushman, 1977). They must actively adapt and embrace these new roles 
to successfully change to an open system (Lifshitz-Assaf, 2018). This change (management) 
has to be carefully considered by innovation managers. Seekers need to identify the boundaries 
of their organization before engaging in open innovation (Carlile, 2004). After clearly defining 
them, they must establish boundary roles for R&D professionals (Tushman, 1977). The 
professionals that perform such positions should be well connected internally and externally to 
ensure efficient integration of outside knowledge and inside capability (Tushman and Scanlan, 
1981). Such boundary work is part of new work (Nelson and Lawrence, 2012). In practice, 
organizations often establish a connection to a crowdsourcing community by using paid and 
controlled users (Dahlander and Wallin, 2006).  
In general, there are two successful approaches for individual professionals in companies to 
generate innovations: focusing on either internal sources of innovation or external sources of 
innovation (Dahlander et al., 2016). However, one should also consider that the degree of 
openness that R&D professionals practice is curvilinearly related to ideation performance 
because it helps to broaden the horizon and to keep up with competitive industries, but the costs 
of integration exceed the gains in ideation performance at some point (Salter et al., 2015). The 
time horizon, the connection to senior management, and the variety of the professional`s 
knowledge moderate these effects (Salter et al., 2015). One can also generalize the trade-off 
between external ideas and integration costs and the resulting curvilinear relationship between 
openness and ideation performance to the organizational level (Laursen and Salter, 2006).   
Critical problems for seekers during the change to open innovation are the not invented here 
(e.g., organizations prefer internally developed ideas over externally developed ideas) and the 
not sold here (e.g., organizations tend to stick to their product line and prevent the occurrence 
of more radical, external innovation) syndromes (Chesbrough, 2006; Katz and Allen, 1982), 
which are not just embedded in the organization`s culture but emerge on the level of individual 
personalities (Elsbach and Flynn, 2013).  
Further, seekers need to increase their absorptive capacity to obtain the ability to take the best 
crowdsourced solutions and turn them into successful innovations. Absorptive capacity is the 
ability of companies to gather, select and commercialize the correct invention and is a crucial 
driver of long-term success that is especially important when considering the integration of 
ideas from open innovation (Cohen and Levinthal, 1990; Zahra and George, 2002). While 
improving the absorptive capacity, the internal stickiness (e.g., inefficient knowledge transfer 
==PAGE:24==
15 
 
in the company), causal ambiguity, and complicated internal relationships must be managed 
(Szulanski, 1996). A helpful method to improve absorptive capacity and reduce internal 
stickiness is the creation of innovation narratives, thereby generating a generative memory, 
which helps translate innovations throughout the organization (Bartel and Garud, 2009). 
Crowdsourcing initiatives should focus on distant solutions (Howe, 2006; 2008). But many 
seekers wrongly select ideas close to what they know, leading to the distant search, narrow 
attention phenomenon (Dahlander and Piezunka, 2020). It is no surprise that this well-known 
human bias that influences professionals to choose practicality over creativity and impedes the 
ability to recognize creative ideas spreads to the organizational level (Mueller et al., 2012).   
Finally, seekers need to deal with the paradox of openness which describes the conflict between 
openness and appropriation (Laursen and Salter, 2014). While seekers might create more value 
using an open innovation approach, they most likely will capture less of that value compared to 
a closed innovation approach. While organizations, which make use of open innovation, are 
well aware of this phenomenon, they have to consider that the same preconditions hold for the 
solvers in crowdsourcing, as they might also increase their profit by keeping their solution to 
the posted task proprietary (Foege et al., 2019). However, under many real-world 
circumstances, the solver`s openness is the right strategy from a game-theoretical point of view 
(Harhoff et al., 2003). Finally, seekers must choose appropriate evaluation processes to reap the 
benefits from the contributions. Thus, the final literature part will summarize insights about the 
evaluation phase.  
2.7 Evaluation Methods 
To improve the efficiency of their evaluation phase, seekers should consider options, such as 
using a metric scale or a judgment call, including the crowd in the evaluation process, and 
spanning the contest over multiple innovation rounds (Dahlander et al., 2019).  
Following the argumentation of Dahlander et al. (2019), the solutions to complex problems 
should be evaluated with judgment calls, as metric scales might be too restricting and biased 
towards narrow solutions (Dahlander and Piezunka, 2020; Murray et al., 2012). Developing the 
right metrics ex-ante can be very costly, and sometimes the nature of the task does not allow 
the use of a metric scale (Murray et al., 2012, Dahlander et al., 2019). If companies expect many 
solutions and can define the problem very well, a metric evaluation becomes more efficient, as 
its need for resources per solution decreases rapidly with the number of solutions. Further, 
==PAGE:25==
16 
 
seekers face a crucial information overload problem (O'Reilly III, 1980), which they might 
prevent by using automatic evaluations.  
Participants tend to have better predictions of future demands than managers, and their 
participation in decision-making increases future demand (Berg, 2016; Fuchs et al., 2010). The 
crowd´s involvement in the evaluation process can save many resources and might be 
particularly efficient if the crowd consists of (future) customers and there is no fierce 
competition between the contributors (Dahlander et al., 2019). Involving solvers in the 
evaluation might also increase a crucial motivator, namely perceived fairness (Franke et al., 
2013). 
2.8 Prior Research Conclusion  
The research in open innovation developed broadly for half a century, and crowdsourcing 
investigations went on for about two decades while aggregating many theoretical and 
qualitative works in different areas of crowdsourcing. However, many of the presented 
suggestions have not been tested empirically, and the research gaps in the crowdsourcing 
literature are considerable (Ghezzi et al., 2018; Nevo and Kotlarsky, 2020). Among others, it is 
still, by and large, unclear which fields are the most promising for crowdsourcing initiatives, 
how seekers can design their tasks to draw on the solver`s motivations, and how organizations 
can conduct the evaluation phase efficiently (Ghezzi et al., 2018). Thus, in the next part, I extend 
and develop theories in these areas to derive hypotheses relating to the research questions 
presented above.    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:26==
17 
 
3 Theory 
3.1 The Task`s Application Field and Innovation Phase 
3.1.1 The Task`s Field of Application  
Some application fields might be better suited for crowdsourcing than others depending on their 
field characteristics. First, crowdsourcing might be an appropriate innovation approach in 
industries less dependent on tight intellectual property strategies, especially secrecy. Similar to 
other open innovation approaches, an inevitable part of crowdsourcing is to reveal proprietary 
information. The seekers can choose how much information they want to share, but a lack of 
shared information can substantially hinder a successful crowdsourcing campaign. To achieve 
a much-needed feasibility level and develop contributions that fit the seeker`s capabilities, the 
solvers need specific information to solve the task accordingly. A lack of information might 
considerably decrease the chance of crowdsourcing an efficient and feasible solution. However, 
seekers are mostly also interested in keeping proprietary information secret to exclude, among 
others, the competition from obtaining specific knowledge. After all, information and data are 
important sources of competitive advantage, and increasingly so. The paradox of openness 
describes this trade-off (Laursen and Salter, 2014).  
One can assume that different industries and fields have distinctive claims to the tightness and 
composition of their intellectual property strategies. Patents can easily protect some innovations 
(e.g., pharmaceutical innovations). Others must be protected by trade secrets (e.g., recipes), as 
a patent disclosure would cause more harm to the seeker than protecting it, and some 
innovations are developed and improved openly (e.g., open-source software). Providing the 
solvers with information on patent-protected innovations does not cause further harm, as 
seekers have already disclosed them in the patent. It is different with innovations protected by 
trade secrets. The seeker is, per definition, obliged to keep the information a secret. Otherwise, 
they would not be protected any further.  
Similar argumentation holds for seekers that follow no traditional intellectual property 
strategies but try to beat their competition in time-to-market and to profit from path dependency 
and causal ambiguity. These seekers might gain more from crowdsourcing, as they obtained the 
complementary capabilities to implement and commercialize the crowdsourced contributions 
ex-ante. That way, they can always stay ahead of their competition, even while disclosing 
proprietary information and contributions from the crowdsourcing contest. Thus, from an 
intellectual property perspective, I assume that industries with secrecy as their intellectual 
==PAGE:27==
18 
 
property strategy are less represented in crowdsourcing competitions and attract fewer solvers 
than other industries. 
Second, the competitive structure of the seeker`s industry might also influence the decision to 
start a crowdsourcing contest. Fiercely competitive industries (e.g., airlines) might be less 
attractive for open innovation, as minor advantages might lead to significant differences in the 
competition. Publicly disclosing information could come with too many risks. It is different for 
less competitive industries (e.g., fast-growing markets) and especially in industries where open 
innovation is already implemented commonly (e.g., software). For them, initiating 
crowdsourcing might be advantageous, as the seeker can frame the task and the direction of the 
innovative efforts in the industry. Thus, I assume that seekers from fiercely competitive fields 
attract fewer solvers in crowdsourcing competitions and are under-represented on 
crowdsourcing platforms. 
While competition and intellectual property might affect the seeker`s ability to attract solvers 
indirectly, I assume that the following theories might be more crucial to describe the 
relationship between the application fields and the number of solvers. First, seeker`s industries 
differ in terms of their complexity. Tasks from some industries require a variety of highly 
specialized and complex knowledge obtained in years of education to be solved. This 
phenomenon is independent of the type of education the solvers had, either self-taught or 
obtained through a degree. Some capabilities need significantly more time and effort to develop 
than others. While almost every member of a crowd can usefully contribute to ideation around 
new Oktoberfest Munich souvenirs, a substantially different picture might emerge for tasks 
concerning the propulsion of rockets. The self-selection (e.g., crowd members can decide to 
contribute) might also strengthen the relationship between complexity and the number of 
solvers. For seemingly easy tasks, crowd members might be more confident to contribute 
usefully and to have a chance at winning, even if they might be less qualified than other crowd 
members. For seemingly challenging tasks, crowd members might be less confident about their 
capabilities and reluctant the contribute. However, straightforward tasks might be too dull to 
address the intrinsic motivation of capable crowd members and too competitive to address their 
extrinsic motivation, as more users might contribute. Thus, I assume that complex application 
fields attract fewer solvers on crowdsourcing platforms, while application fields with medium 
complexity attract many solvers.  
Second, one might assume that the same holds for industries with less overlap with others, as 
the crowd member might be less likely to have obtained industry-specific knowledge. However, 
==PAGE:28==
19 
 
users from other fields distant from the task`s field are usually more successful in 
crowdsourcing contests (Jeppesen and Lakhani, 2010). Thus, a very specialized industry might 
be more interesting for crowdsourcing. If it is somewhat isolated, it has less diversity internally; 
and the benefits of diversity that derive from crowdsourcing can be easier reached. On the other 
hand, the implementation and commercialization might be worse for seekers from such 
industries, as the lack of diversity results most likely in a lack of absorptive capacity. However, 
as fewer solvers are addressed in specific industries, I assume that very isolated industries 
attract fewer solvers on crowdsourcing platforms. 
Third, as described above, solvers are motivated by a social cause and reputation gains. Crowd 
members that assume a meaningful purpose behind a task might be more willing to contribute. 
Further, seekers that do not have profit maximization as their primary objective but search 
mainly for a solution to a problem care less about intellectual property concerns. Thus, I assume 
that social industries attract more solvers on crowdsourcing platforms. 
On the other hand, seekers could turn to crowdsourcing contests for different reasons than 
expecting their task to be particularly suitable for crowdsourcing. Crowdsourcing might be a 
resource used out of desperation if the seeker attempted other innovation approaches and failed 
to find a solution. It can also be an option for seekers that do not control sufficient resources to 
solve the task internally and use crowdsourcing as a cheap yet possibly inefficient method. 
Finally, combined with the disclosure of the seeker`s identity, seekers from unsuitable 
industries might use crowdsourcing to achieve secondary goals (e.g., marketing, recruiting, etc.) 
rather than focusing on the input of the contests. 
To summarize, various application field characteristics should fit crowdsourcing contests 
better, others worse. Thus, I assume that the number of solvers in their contests should be 
affected by such characteristics. However, I also expect that specific exceptions exist based on 
different motivations.  
Hypothesis 1.1: Some application fields should have positive/negative relationships to the 
number of attracted solvers, depending on specific field characteristics. 
3.1.2 Innovation Phase  
Early innovation phases could be more suitable for crowdsourcing than later phases. There is a 
variety of reasons why this might be true. Early and late innovation phases differ, among others, 
in the number of investment costs, flexibility, and need for information. Prototyping and 
consumer testing are usually parts of later innovation stages and need a lot of investment, which 
==PAGE:29==
20 
 
an amateur does not possess. Moreover, innovation is usually path dependent. If one wants to 
perform innovation in a later stage, it is crucial to know what happened before. Thus, the longer 
the innovation process is already going on, the less flexible it becomes, and the more 
information has to be shared. Decreasing the flexibility decreases the potential of 
crowdsourcing, as it thrives on diverse perspectives and approaches. While restricting them by 
the existing innovation path, one might exclude the best ideas ex-ante. The increase in 
information sharing might escalate the paradox of openness. Companies might become 
reluctant to engage in crowdsourcing after it exceeds a critical information load. The 
aggregation of information might also complicate the task formulation and the ability of the 
crowd to understand the task. Therefore, one reason why early innovation phases are fitting 
better into crowdsourcing is that late innovation phases are not fitting. As presented, one can 
intuitively argue against crowdsourcing during later innovation stages, but one should also note 
positively why crowdsourcing is fitting for early phases of innovation and especially for 
ideation.  
One can regard some crowdsourcing contests as similar to brainstorming. As in brainstorming, 
the seeker searches for many different points of view and ideas. The more heterogeneous the 
group, the better the brainstorming might be. Having the broadest technological knowledge is 
less crucial, but a creative idea might lead others to find the appropriate solution. Everyone with 
some background knowledge is welcome to participate and could help. Therefore, many 
potential solvers can participate and feel confident to contribute. Further, solvers often do not 
need to invest heavily during early innovation (e.g., ideation). It lowers the barrier to 
participation for potential solvers. Also, early innovation phases are arguably more enjoyable 
than later phases, as they provide a lot of room for creativity, which makes the contests more 
compelling and engaging. After all, the solvers participate to a high degree for their enjoyment 
during their free time. Thus, I assume that contests located in early innovation phases attract a 
higher number of solvers than contests located in later phases. 
Hypothesis 1.2: An association with an early innovation phase is positively related to the 
number of solvers. 
3.2 Practices to Draw on Seeker`s Motivation 
3.2.1 Identity Disclosure 
Another important decision for seekers might be if they want to disclose their identity. A 
disclosure increases intellectual property concerns, as competitors can allocate the task to the 
==PAGE:30==
21 
 
seeker and derive their research efforts from it. This could have substantial consequences on 
the competitive environment. However, there are close links between crowdsourcing and the 
open innovation environment, which, in its nature, focuses on the advantages of openness rather 
than the disadvantages of secrecy. One might argue that crowdsourcing without disclosing the 
identity could be a fruitful hybrid approach to open innovation, as it combines the wisdom of 
the crowd with less intellectual property concerns. But, crucial mechanisms from open 
innovation (e.g., reputation seeking and brand identification) fail in such a hybrid approach, 
which might result in the fact that such an (as "best of both worlds" perceived) approach misses 
out on the true potential of open innovation. Brand identity might be a critical mechanism for 
participation in crowdsourcing contests. An often-used example of how brand identity can be a 
basis for crowdsourcing is the LEGO crowdsourcing platform (ideas.lego.com), which attracts 
many users and profits hugely from the seeker`s brand identity. If emotional connections 
between the potential solver and the seeker`s brand exist, solvers should be more likely to help 
the seeker by contributing to their innovation efforts. Brand identity also plays a critical role in 
reputation-seeking behavior. Reputation-seeking behavior influences the solver`s contribution 
decision (Moser and Nicholas, 2013). Solvers want to increase their reputation for different 
reasons. Especially influential, large high-reputation companies can significantly increase a 
solver`s reputation by publicly appreciating their work. If potential solvers hope to enhance 
their reputation through seekers, they are more willing to contribute to their contests. The 
motivational effects of brand identity and reputation seeking cannot occur if seekers do not 
disclose their identity. However, if the seeker has a bad reputation (e.g., low CSR and ESG 
ratings) or is unknown, it might be better to crowdsource anonymously. Potential solvers might 
be less willing to contribute if they do not agree with the seeker`s values and actions or think 
that the seeker cannot provide the reputation gains the potential solver seeks. However, as noted 
before, such secrecy of identity is not consistent with the spirit of open innovation and might 
not be appreciated by an open innovation community. Further, it could harm the solver`s ability 
to solve the tasks if information about the seeker is kept secret; and the innovation efforts lack 
the correct context. Thus, I assume that the number of seekers is higher for contests that provide 
information about their seeker`s identity than for contests that do not.  
Hypothesis 2.1: Identity disclosure is positively related to the number of attracted solvers. 
For the managerial implications of this study, it is critical to note that seekers could rather 
follow mixed or different agendas while engaging in crowdsourcing than purely improve 
innovative efforts. An important one might be the reputation building by the seeker. Seekers 
==PAGE:31==
22 
 
present themself as transparent, modern, and inclusive when asking the crowd for help. To 
increase that effect, seekers can also create tasks related to high-reputation fields (e.g., 
sustainability and social efforts). In such cases, the number of solvers or even the quality of 
solutions might not matter for the seeker, but they solely or partly want to signalize to the world 
that they focus on meaningful tasks and, thereby, increase their reputation. Further, other 
secondary goals such as marketing, branding, and community interactions might matter. Thus, 
one should consider the true motivation of crowdsourcing while reasoning on secrecy and 
openness.  
3.2.2 Award Size 
As noted, prior research indicated an ambiguous relation between the award size and the 
number of solvers or contributions. This might be especially true if one cannot control for the 
task`s complexity separately, as solvers expect the awards to be higher if the tasks increase in 
difficulty. However, complexity should also influence the number of solvers and contributions. 
Different theoretical concepts, which could explain the relationship between the award size and 
the number of solvers, are presented in the following. One should not assume that one 
explanation is exclusive but that they coexist and interact to describe the whole relation. A 
monetary award can be motivating, either directly as the value of the award or indirectly, as the 
solvers might assess the size of the award as the strength of appreciation for their efforts.  
Some solvers could be solely motivated by money and consider participating in the contest a 
job (Ross et al., 2010). The award should reach a specific size (e.g., a significant amount of 
money) to successfully attract such solvers. Otherwise, it would not be worth the effort for 
monetarily motivated solvers. Thus, small awards up to this threshold would not attract them. 
However, after the award size reaches this level, it attracts semi-professional solvers, and 
increasing the award size would not matter if one disregards complexity. This argument 
suggests an s-shaped curve with a small number of solvers for lower awards, a rapidly 
increasing number of solvers at a significantly high award, and an almost stagnating number of 
solvers afterward. One could also assume a second s-curve after the first one if the award is 
high enough so that potential solvers that would not usually participate in crowdsourcing 
contests would be attracted. However, such contests (e.g., with a million US dollars reward) 
occur rarely. Therefore, an empirical assessment of such contests is challenging.  
Another part of solvers might be internally motivated and perceive the award only as a means 
of appreciation. They might appreciate lower awards more than the first group. Thus, they might 
have a smaller threshold to participate. However, as such indirect value depends more on a 
==PAGE:32==
23 
 
variety of motivations of the solver, one could assume that this group is more heterogenous 
than the second one. For some, any award might be motivating enough. Others only perceive 
appreciation through a higher one. The award size will reach a threshold that maximizes the 
value of appreciation. Afterward, an increase cannot attract any additional solvers. Therefore, 
one might picture the relationship between the award size and the number of solvers as an S-
shaped curve that is substantially less steep (almost linear) than that of the first group and should 
also increase earlier.  
The size of the award can also be a proxy for complexity. One can assume that seekers might 
offer higher awards for more complex tasks. Thus, one should consider the influence of 
complexity on the number of solvers if one cannot control it, as is the case in this study and I 
will discuss this later. First, the more complex a task, the fewer people can solve it. This 
decrease should happen quite rapidly, as anyone could solve straightforward tasks, but a linear 
increase in complexity should result in an exponential decrease in the number of people that 
can solve the task. If solvers can efficiently self-select tasks, this simplified explanation would 
suggest a negative relation between the award, as a proxy for complexity, and the number of 
solvers. 
Second, an increase in complexity can be motivating, at first, for the internally motivated 
solvers. The challenge increases and gets more interesting. This increase in motivation lasts as 
long as the tasks are not too complex and overwhelming. Afterward, it would decrease the 
willingness to participate. Thus, the relation between the award, as a proxy for complexity, and 
the number of solvers would follow an inverted U-shape.  
Third, the award-to-complexity ratio might matter for monetarily motivated solvers as long as 
the solver can solve the task. However, as the award is, among others, considered a proxy for 
complexity in this research, I cannot control for this relation and will discuss it later.  
Finally, the award might not be just a proxy for complexity in a study like this, but also a signal 
of complexity to potential solvers. As solvers self-select the tasks they want to participate in, 
they might be reluctant to participate in contests with high awards as they perceive themselves 
as underqualified or assume that a high award would attract solvers with better qualifications. 
Therefore, such an effect of perceived complexity might even exist beyond the effect of factual 
task complexity.   
To summarize, especially as this study does not control for complexity separately, one can 
hypothesize the relationship between the award size and the number of solvers based on 
==PAGE:33==
24 
 
multiple theories. One could assume either an S-shape relation based on the award value and 
motivational value from the award, an inverted U-shaped relation based on complexity and 
motivation, or a logarithmic relation based on complexity and capability. As noted, the 
measured effect will likely combine all of the effects above. However, one can assume that the 
complexity effects will outweigh the motivational effects for higher awards. Thus, I assume 
that the size of the award has an inverted U-shape relation with the number of solvers.  
Hypothesis 2.2: The relation between the size of the award and the number of solvers follows 
an inverted U-shape. 
Further, the seekers can decide if they provide a fixed monetary award (e.g., an amount of 
money) or a flexible one (e.g., a share of the resulting revenue). While risk-averse solvers might 
prefer the first option, less risk-averse solvers might see more potential in the second one. Many 
humans tend to be risk-averse. Thus, the first option should attract more solvers. Especially for 
semi-professional solvers, a fixed award should be more appealing. Further, it takes an extra 
effort to find out how much revenue might result from the contribution and compare it to a fixed 
award. Thus, I assume that the number of solvers is higher for contests with fixed monetary 
awards. 
The seekers could provide non-monetary awards (e.g., vouchers, tablets, etc.) instead of 
monetary ones. It might be less expensive for the seeker if they could get the non-monetary 
awards below market price for consumers. But, the perceived value of non-monetary awards 
might differ hugely between the solvers, and a resale of such an award usually imposes 
transaction costs on the solver. Thus, I assume that the number of solvers is higher for contests 
with monetary awards.   
3.2.3 Task Specificity  
Seekers must consider how specific their task should be and how distinct they want to explain 
it. Based on the limitations of the outcome, tasks can be either general or more detailed. A 
solution to a more specified task has a more straightforward use for the seeker, and its value is 
often quantifiable. However, the more specified the task, the fewer people might be qualified 
to solve it. It could also limit the crowd`s creative effort (Gillier et al., 2018). Solvers from 
distant fields won many successful crowdsourcing contests. If the task is too specific, seekers 
might exclude them, or they might not feel addressed during self-selection. The advantage of 
general tasks is that they might attract a big crowd, as many members might be able and willing 
to contribute. Further, if a task is lengthy specified, the pure volume of information might create 
==PAGE:34==
25 
 
a barrier to contribute for potential solvers, as the initial effort to get familiar with the task might 
be too high. However, an increase in its specificity should, at first, be helpful for better 
understanding the task. If the task is too unspecified, potential solvers might be unsure how to 
address it. Thus, I assume that increasing the task specificity has an inverted U-shape relation 
with the number of solvers.  
Hypothesis 2.3: The relationship between the task specificity and the number of attracted 
solvers is an inverted U-shape. 
3.3 Potential of Solver Evaluations 
Seekers might use solvers to decrease their costs of selection. They might integrate solvers by 
providing an option to vote or evaluate other solvers` contributions. This is only possible in 
crowdsourcing contests with open contributions because the solvers need to access them. 
However, the seeker could also publish them after the submission deadline and keep them secret 
before. This hybrid approach would inhibit collaborative innovation but ease possible 
plagiarism concerns during the contest. Otherwise, solvers might be reluctant to share their 
ideas early in the process, as other solvers could copy their contributions or seek inspiration 
from them. Other typical plagiarism concerns in open crowdsourcing contests would remain. 
Third parties could still easily access the contributions, which poses property concerns beyond 
the use in the competition. 
An additional risk of solvers` involvement in the selection is that highly competitive, devious 
solvers might try to improve their chance of winning by negatively evaluating other 
contributions or by using fake accounts to give positive evaluations of themselves. Thus, the 
success of solvers` involvement in the evaluation process might depend on the frequency, 
detection, and regulation of devious solvers.  
Further influencing factors are the complexity and the diversity of the contributions. If they are 
too complex and diverse, solvers might lack the knowledge to understand and adequately 
evaluate other solvers` contributions.  
However, evaluations by solvers could be significantly faster and cheaper than evaluations by 
the NPD team, as the number of solvers is substantially higher than the number of evaluators 
in a jury. It also prevents an information overload on the development team. It could focus on 
the fraction of excellent contributions and their ability to implement them. Therefore, at least a 
pre-evaluation by the solvers seems natural in rather general topics. These topics might also 
==PAGE:35==
26 
 
tend to attract more contributions, see above, and, in turn, substantially increase the costs of 
evaluation and information overload (O'Reilly III, 1980). 
Two indispensable preconditions for solvers` involvement in the evaluation process are that 
solvers have the ability and willingness to evaluate other solvers` contributions appropriately 
and that the seekers can measure the solvers` evaluations efficiently.  
As described above, the literature indicates that solvers can evaluate contributions adequately. 
Their evaluations might be even better than the ones of the seekers` jury (Berg, 2016; Fuchs et 
al., 2010). This results from the solvers` characteristics, which I will theorize in the following.  
Solvers self-select contests according to their abilities and interests, as they want to maximize 
their winning chances (Yang et al., 2008), and they are partly motivated by internal motivation 
(Kaufmann et al., 2011). This self-selection sorts out many potential solvers, a priori, that are 
less qualified to address the contest`s task accordingly. During the contribution phase, the 
solvers deal comprehensively with the corresponding task. They learn to understand its nature 
and identify its crucial challenges by trying to understand and solve the task. Finally, solvers 
are often also end-users and profiteers of innovative contributions to the contest (Dahlander et 
al., 2019). Summarizing, many solvers understand the task, invest the time to solve it, and show 
interest in a good solution. One might assume that this combination is a sufficient foundation 
for the solvers to give appropriate evaluations. As they understood the task and tried to solve it, 
they can appropriately evaluate how another solver`s contribution dealt with the task`s 
challenges and how well it solved it overall.  
Further, solvers participate in the contest and are possible future users. They are interested in 
innovative contributions and fair evaluations. Thus, they want to evaluate the contributions 
faithfully according to their perceived contribution value. Solver evaluation might even increase 
the perceived fairness of the evaluation process (Franke et al., 2013). Thus, this imposes less of 
a risk but rather a potential to attract solvers.  
If seekers want to use the solvers` evaluations in their evaluation process, they must design and 
choose appropriate evaluation tools. Further, they must provide them to the solvers and decide 
on a system for using the solver evaluations in their evaluation processes. Tools should be easy 
to use. Otherwise, they might decrease the willingness to evaluate and more evaluation mistakes 
might occur. The results from the evaluation should also be differentiable between 
contributions. Otherwise, the evaluations are hard to use (e.g., a 5-point scale can be better 
differentiated than a 2-point scale). Standard evaluation methods are likes and evaluation scales.  
==PAGE:36==
27 
 
Likes can be a simple method of direct evaluations by solvers and enjoy broad popularity 
through social media platforms. With a like, solvers can indicate when they value a contribution 
as good. The seeker can take the number of likes on a contribution as a proxy for its quality. It 
would be a typical practical application of the wisdom of the crowd theory (Surowiecki, 2005). 
The number of likes has the advantage that everyone is familiar with likes and that the 
evaluation is effortless (e.g., it only takes one click). However, likes might oversimplify the 
process, and there might be a significant ambiguity behind why solvers gave a like. For 
example, a contribution could get many likes because it provides good sketches of the solution. 
Then, the like would not evaluate the value of the contribution but appreciate its representation. 
Further, the number of likes on a contribution also depends on the number of views. One could 
address the latter by introducing dislikes (e.g., solvers can also signal that they do not like the 
contribution). However, one commonly perceives dislikes as 
demotivating and 
counterproductive, and the willingness to contribute likes or dislikes might differ. Therefore, 
platforms across the border abandoned the concept of dislikes. Finally, seekers can barely 
differentiate between evaluations if the total number of likes is low.  
Evaluation scales (e.g., an X out of 5 stars system) provide more possibilities to differentiate 
between the quality of contributions, even with a small number of evaluations. While the 
problem of negative evaluations might also occur with scales, users tend to evaluate quite 
positively on scales. Seekers can also provide multiple scales for different contribution 
characteristics (e.g., feasibility, novelty, and solution fit). This would prevent the wrong focus 
of evaluation described for the likes above. The evaluation criteria facilitate an efficient 
evaluation process. Seekers can calculate an average value from the different characteristics to 
achieve an overall rating. Weights for specific characteristics can be applied. Thus, even a 
limited number of evaluators could provide a clear ranking of the contributions. However, 
scales might also attract fewer evaluations than likes due to their higher complexity.   
Further, seekers might also consider indirect solver evaluations in their evaluation or control 
for their influences on solver likes and evaluations. The number of views, comments, and 
evaluations may be such influential factors. As described above, the number of views influences 
the number of likes. The more solvers read a contribution, the more likely that someone gives 
it a like. However, the number of views could also indicate a contribution`s quality. The more 
innovative the contribution, the more solvers investigate it out of interest. Further, the better the 
contribution, the more solvers might study it to understand it and seek inspiration for their 
contributions. Therefore, high-quality contributions might attract more views than low-quality 
==PAGE:37==
28 
 
contributions. Seekers could use the number of views as a proxy for a contribution`s quality or 
might at least control for the number of views when using the number of likes as a proxy. The 
number of comments might follow a similar argumentation as the number of views; thus, more 
innovative contributions might receive more comments. Further, comments might contain 
valuable feedback to improve the contributions; thus, more comments might lead to more 
improvements in contributions. Therefore, seekers might also use the number of comments as 
a proxy for contribution quality. Finally, following the wisdom of the crowd argumentation 
(Surowiecki, 2005), the value of the evaluation as a proxy for a contribution`s quality increases 
with the number of evaluations. While a limited number of solvers might evaluate a contribution 
not according to its quality, a higher number of solvers is less likely to do so. Therefore, seekers 
might control for the moderating effect of the number of evaluations, as it increases the relative 
value of the evaluation.  
To summarize, I assume that, if seekers choose an open crowdsourcing contest, solvers might 
be efficient evaluators, and seekers should have the right tools to use the solvers` evaluation 
capabilities. 
Hypothesis 3.1: Solvers are able and willing to evaluate other solvers` contributions 
appropriately. 
Hypothesis 3.2: Seekers can use solver evaluations efficiently to reduce their workload while 
maintaining reasonable evaluation accuracy. 
I acquired new data from two crowdsourcing platforms to address the hypotheses and reduce 
the lack of data in the crowdsourcing field. I used data from Innocentive to investigate the first 
and second research questions and data from HYVECrowd to strengthen the findings from 
Innocentive and mainly to investigate the third research question. Thus, the next chapter 
clarifies the data acquisition, handling, and analysis. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:38==
29 
 
4 Data and Methodology  
4.1 Data - Innocentive   
4.1.1 Research Object – Innocentive 
The first research object was the Wazoku Crowd, also known as Innocentive, hosted by Wazoku 
(https://www.wazoku.com/challenges). Innocentive provides seekers with a platform to publish 
their crowdsourcing campaigns and solvers with the functionalities to submit their contributions 
and interact with the community. However, compared to HYVECrowd, which I introduce in 
4.2, the contributions of other solvers are usually not visible to the solvers, even though this 
function seems to exist. Therefore, one might assume that the seekers actively decide against 
the publication of the contributions and feedback functions. Innocentive is considerably bigger 
than HYVECrowd and, therefore, also has more ways of organizing contests on the platform. 
Contests do not just have tags for their application field but are also grouped based on the
