innovation phase (e.g., ideation and reduction to practice). The platform categorized numerous 
contests into so-called showcases. These showcases have a specific focus (e.g., Covid-19 or 
emerging economies) or are company specific (e.g., Astra Zeneca or NASA). Solvers can join 
these showcases to get notifications about them (WazokuCrowd, 2023b). Innocentive is the 
biggest platform for crowdsourcing contests and hosted almost 1700 contests.  
4.1.2 Data Acquisition - Innocentive 
I used the programming language Python (Python Software Foundation, 2023), more precisely, 
the selenium package for Python (Selenium, 2023), to scrape the information of each contest 
task specification. Selenium`s web driver function empowers users to control a browser with 
Python commands. I used the Chrome browser (Google, 2023). First, I extracted the Uniform 
Resource Locator (URL) links for each contest from the platform`s contest overview page. I 
opened the Chrome browser with Selenium`s web driver and inserted the page`s URL using 
Selenium`s get function. I used the driver`s execute script function to scroll to the end of the 
page and load all contests. I used the sleep function of Python`s time package (Python Software 
Foundation, 2023b) to delay the code`s proceeding until the browser loaded the pages. Then, I 
manually searched for the Hypertext Markup Language (HTML) paths to the sought data. I 
extracted the URL links for each contest using the HTML paths, Selenium`s find elements 
function, and the By. XPATH method. Finally, I saved the URL links in a comma-separated 
values (CSV) file. The general scraping approach with Selenium follows Analytics Vidhya 
(2020). I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 1.1.  
==PAGE:39==
30 
 
I addressed each contest overview page by first importing the scraped URL links from the CSV 
file into a Panda's data frame (McKinney et al., 2010). Then, I opened the Chrome browser, 
using Selenium`s web driver, created an empty list to store the scraped data, and iterated 
through the data frame with the links. For each instance (each link), I constituted a separate list 
to store the scraped data and used the get function of the web driver to search for the link in the 
browser. I used the execute script function of the web driver to scroll down on the web page 
and the sleep function of Python`s time package (Python Software Foundation, 2023(2)) to 
ensure that the browser gets enough time to load the page.  
In the following, I located and scraped the title, the description (e.g., award, number of solvers, 
tags, and keywords), and the main text from each page by finding the texts in the HTML 
document using the find element function and the By.XPATH method of the web driver. I saved 
all the scraped texts by appending them to the list for the specific link. I ended each loop by 
appending the link`s list to a list of all texts that I created at the beginning. Finally, I converted 
the list with all scraped texts to a Panda`s data frame (McKinney et al., 2010) and saved it in a 
CSV data file. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 1.2. 
4.1.3 Data Cleaning and Conversion - Innocentive 
To clean and convert the data, I imported the CSV file with the scraped texts into a panda`s data 
frame (McKinney et al., 2010).  
First, I assigned names to each data column (e.g., title, award, and solvers). I dropped empty 
rows, as some links did not contain any data. They might be blank because of possibly 
disallowed accesses. I identified the empty rows using Panda`s is null function to find the 
instances without any value at the head of their list (e.g., the title column). Afterward, I 
realigned the indices using the reset index function. Then, I corrected the misaligned values. 
Some instances had missing values leading to misalignments in the data frame, as the values 
moved up in the list if the value above was missing. Therefore, I iterated through each row and 
controlled each column (e.g., columns title to keywords) that contained misalignments if it 
contained the value for the respective next column in the row. If a column had the wrong value, 
I appended this value to a specific list for each next column and added this list to the data frame. 
I used a new list, as I would have overwritten other misaligned values if I had directly assigned 
the values to the correct column. I iterated through the rows and checked the generated columns. 
If they were not empty (e.g., “NA”), I assigned their values to the right column and deleted the 
value from the false column. Finally, I dropped the generated columns after reassigning all their 
values.  
==PAGE:40==
31 
 
Second, I used Python`s length function to calculate the length of each title and appended them 
in a new column. For each instance, I deleted the title (e.g., "tags:") and unnecessary spaces in 
the tags column using the replace and strip functions and inserted an empty string if the cell 
was blank to facilitate the data handling in the later process.  
Third, to generate binary variables for each contest type, I manually gathered a list of all used 
contest types: Electronic Requests for Partners, Requests for Partners and Suppliers (I combined 
the requests in one column), Theoretical IP Transfer, Theoretical – Licensing, Reduction to 
Practice, and Ideation. I controlled for the comprehensiveness of the list later. I created a list 
and iterated through all instances for each type in the list. If the code found the contest type in 
the tags string, it appended 1 to the list and 0 else. I converted the list into a data frame and 
appended it to the overall data frame. Finally, I controlled for the case that no contest type was 
found by iterating through all instances. It created a list with binary values, as explained before. 
I could not find a contest type in 0 instances. To facilitate further work with the tags column, I 
applied the re package`s sub and compile functions (Python Software Foundation, 2023(3)) to 
separate the (during the scraping) connected words and used the replace function for minor 
corrections.  
Fourth, I manually gathered all showcases (n=28) in a list to generate binary variables for each 
showcase. I used the same approach described for the contest types to add a new column for 
each showcase with the values 1 if the contest was part of the specific showcase and 0 if the 
contest was not. I provided a list of the showcases in Appendix 1.6. Further, I added another 
binary variable. It was 1 if all showcase values for a contest were 0 to obtain a control variable 
for the case that no showcase is assigned.  
Fifth, I manually gathered a list of all fields (n=10) to generate a binary variable for each 
application field. I used the same approach described for the contest types to add a new column 
for each application field with the values 1 if the contest was part of the specific field and 0 if 
the contest was not. One can find a list of all application fields in Appendix 1.7. Finally, I 
controlled for the case that the platform indicated none of the fields by iterating through all 
instances and changing all application field values to “NA” if none of the fields had 1 as their 
value. I will discuss this interpretation and its theoretical implications later. 
Sixth, each time I generated a binary variable from the tags column, I deleted the string of the 
variable from the tags string. As the tags column consisted of the showcases, the contest types, 
and the fields, the tags should be empty afterward. To control for that, I iterated through all 
instances and printed the respective tag`s string, if it was not an empty one. 
==PAGE:41==
32 
 
Seventh, some texts were in the tags column, and I realigned them to the texts column by 
selecting the strings from the tags column with an unusually high length. Further, I iterated 
through all instances and checked if a value in the text column existed by controlling for the 
type of the value in the text (e.g., if the column contains a float, otherwise a string). If they were 
empty, I changed the value to “NA”. If the text values were not in the text or the tags column, 
they were misaligned to one of the columns with the names SOL, SUB, or SBL columns, which 
were highly inconsistent and irrelevant for this study, as they contained the same values for 
each instance. I iterated through all instances, controlled each of the three columns for the text 
value, and assigned that value to the text column if found. I did not generate a new list for the 
text column, as it was the last column. Therefore, it was impossible to overwrite relevant values, 
which might have been misaligned in the process. Finally, I used the same method as for the 
titles (see above) to generate the text lengths.  
Eighth, I followed a similar approach to define a variable for the contest`s end date. I found the 
pattern that mainly new descriptions include the formulation "Submissions to this Challenge 
must be received by 11:59…". Therefore, I controlled the text column for this formulation and 
created a list of the dates and a binary list for the existence of the date (1 if it was found). 
Finally, I also used the parse function from Python`s dateutil parser package (Dateutil 
Development Team, 2023), which can identify dates in many forms in texts. This function 
found further dates. However, in 1082 instances, I could not find any date. I appended the lists 
to the data frame. I cleaned two problems that occurred during the automatic extraction of the 
dates and that I found manually. Finally, I used the parse function again to align the format of 
all dates, as they were different (e.g., DD/MM/YYYY or YYYY/DD/MM).  
Ninth, I removed the descriptions from all integer values to facilitate the data analysis in a 
statistics program. First, I iterated through all instances and replaced the title and signs in the 
award column with empty strings. I should note that I also replaced "up to" and "in total" in a 
few instances. I will discuss its theoretical implications later. During this process, I found some 
overlooked misalignments, as some contests did not have titles. I reassigned the values from 
the "title" column into the award column for the affected instances, which was empty due to the 
prior realignments. Finally, the award column contained a numeric value, “NA”, or a string if 
the award was a collaboration.  
Tenth, I repeated this process for the solvers, deadline, and keywords columns. The solver’s 
column either contains a numeric value or “NA”. Some deadline values contained a date, 
however, mostly they were a string with the value “awarded”, “closed”, “under evaluation”, or 
==PAGE:42==
33 
 
“canceled”. The keyword column contains numeric values. I converted all the numeric values 
to integer types using Python`s int function to facilitate the transaction to any statistical 
software.  
Finally, I dropped the rows I generated while combining the CSV files, the (now empty or 
unnecessary) tags, SUB, SBL, and SOL columns, and two columns with indices. Further, I 
dropped all rows that did not contain a numeric value for the number of solvers, as they 
constitute the dependent variable. These were 90 rows. I will later discuss the theoretical 
implications. I saved the data frame as a CSV data file to prepare it for import into a statistic 
program. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 1.3. 
I should note that I mostly iterated only through parts of the URLs at once if the scraping was 
very time-consuming and combined them later. This approach prevented significant time and 
data loss if external problems or missing values stopped the program. I used the copy function 
in the open command prompt (CMD) of Windows 11 (Microsoft Corporation, 2023) to combine 
the partial datasets.   
As I could not extract any time variable for most of the contests, I used the index of the URL 
links list to generate a new variable for the time. The list was ordered chronologically. Thus, 
the index might be useful as a proxy. I will discuss its implications later. I used the contest titles 
to connect the indices to the correct contests and provided the corresponding Python code in 
Appendix 1.4.  
Finally, I created a data set that included only seekers who disclosed their identities. I built it 
by loading the initial data set, identifying the correct contests using the binary identity 
disclosure variable, dropping the secret identity contests, and saving the new data set in a CSV 
file. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 1.5.  
4.2 Data - HYVECrowd   
4.2.1 Research Object – HYVECrowd 
The second research object was the platform HYVECrowd (www.hyvecrowd.com), a 
crowdsourcing platform hosted by HYVE AG. Its homepage describes it as a "platform where 
professional talents and experts jointly solve previously unsolvable challenges" (HYVECrowd, 
2023). HYVECrowd provides seekers with a platform to publish their crowdsourcing 
campaigns and solvers with the functionalities to submit their solutions and interact with the 
community. The submissions by all solvers are visible; users can evaluate and give feedback. 
==PAGE:43==
34 
 
It also enables seekers to provide instant feedback and help the solvers to improve their 
contributions during the contest phase. HYVE also provides services regarding the 
crowdsourcing process to seekers if they are inexperienced. These services stretch from setup, 
over campaigning to evaluation and selection (HYVE, 2023). The setup concerns topic framing, 
content creation, and coach involvement. The campaigning interests recruiting and 
communication, community management, and continuous improvement, and the evaluation and 
selection concern an idea assessment center, a jury meeting, and an implementation boot camp 
(HYVE, 2023).  
The platform HYVECrowd is structured as follows. It provides one tab for attracting new 
seekers, one for attracting new solvers, and another for the overview of all the contests 
(HYVECrowd, 2023). One can divide the contests (based on their status) into current, finished, 
and upcoming. Further, one can sort them into categories. The website presents 24 categories 
(e.g., IT, social, sports) representing multiple application fields (HYVECrowd, 2023b). A 
contest can be in one, two, or multiple fields at a time (e.g., automotive and logistics), but it can 
only have one status. The platform accumulated 140 challenges at the point of analysis. 
However, only 83 were directly on the platform. The rest was only marketed on it but held at 
the seeker`s proprietary platforms.  
In the challenge tab, HYVECrowd presents the contests with a picture, the logo of the seeker, 
a title, a subtitle, the current phase of the contest and its left runtime (e.g., contest, evaluation, 
and announcement), and the price for winning it (HYVECrowd, 2023b). Finished contests also 
show the number of participants, submissions, and views in this overview of all contests. 
Every contest has an overview page with a description, the number of contributions, 
participants, comments, evaluations, and views, an overview of the phases of the contest, and a 
presentation of the latest six contributions (HYVECrowd, 2023c). Every contest also has a tab 
for all contributions to the topic. One can filter out the winning contributions after the evaluation 
was published (HYVECrowd, 2023d). The contributions show the solver`s name (or synonym), 
the contribution date, a picture, a title, a subtitle, the number of upvotes, the number of 
comments, the average evaluation by other solvers, and the number of views. 
 
The rest of the tabs of the contests lack consistency, but, in most cases, they present the task, 
provide data and insights relevant to the problem, list the types of awards, present the jury, and 
explain the evaluation criteria (HYVECrowd, 2023e). In some contests, this information is 
aggregated in fewer tabs (see HYVECrowd, 2023c). In others, they each have their own tab 
(see HYVECrowd, 2023e). 
==PAGE:44==
35 
 
4.2.2 Data Acquisition - HYVECrowd Contests 
I used the programming language Python (Python Software Foundation, 2023), more precisely, 
the Python selenium package (Selenium, 2023), to scrape the contests from HYVECrowd. 
Again, I used the Chrome browser (Google, 2023). To find the contest identity document (ID) 
numbers to address the correct URLs, I manually searched for the ID of the latest contest (201). 
The contest IDs follow a sequential numbering system, but not every number between 1 and 
201 leads to a contest. The platform might have deleted some contests after they ended. To 
control which IDs had a corresponding contest, I iterated through the URLs using the IDs and 
used Selenium`s find elements function and the By.XPATH method of the driver to check if the 
website returned values. If it did, I appended the ID to a list. The corresponding contest IDs 
range from 1 to 201. I found 83 contests. I provided the corresponding Python code and contest 
IDs in Appendix 2.1. 
To address the overview page of each contest, I iterated through all contest overview URLs 
(e.g., https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contest/{CONTEST ID}/overview) with the identification 
numbers in the ID list. I selected the texts for extraction by manually identifying their HTML 
paths and classes in the Chrome browser. Then, I used selenium`s find element function and 
By.XPATH method to address their web element they are part of. Using Selenium`s get attribute 
function, I extracted the texts of the selected web elements. The chosen texts included the 
contest ID, the title, the subtitle, the overview text, the contest-specific tab names, and the end 
dates of the phases. They also included the activities: number of contributions, number of 
participants, number of comments, number of evaluations, and number of views. I saved the 
texts of each overview in a list. Using pandas (McKinney et al., 2010), I created a data frame 
from all overview lists and saved it in a CSV file. To extract the ID, I selected the websites` 
links and stripped them down to a three-figure ID format using Python`s replace function. I 
used the replace function to delete any paragraph breaks in the other texts. I provided the 
corresponding Python code and contest IDs in Appendix 2.2. 
I used a similar approach with selenium (Selenium, 2023) to identify the award for the winners 
of each contest. As the awards were only directly shown on the page with all contests, I 
manually searched for the number of these overview pages (9) and iterated through the 
corresponding URLs (e.g.,https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contests?page={PAGENUMBER} 
&per_page=18&tags=&statuses=&sort=published&direction=desc). I extracted the title, the 
ID, and the award as described above, combined and saved them to a CSV data file. I extracted 
==PAGE:45==
36 
 
the title and the ID to connect the award values to their contests later. I provided the 
corresponding Python code in Appendix 2.3. 
I added the awards to the HYVECrowd data set by adding an award column to it and assigning 
the award value from the award data file to the correct row by controlling for the contest IDs. 
Further, I stripped all strings from the award values (e.g., "Win up to" and currencies). This 
data cleaning has theoretical consequences, which I will discuss later. I provided the 
corresponding Python code and the strings in Appendix 2.4. 
I used a similar approach with selenium to identify which contests are part of which category 
(Selenium, 2023). First, I manually determined the highest URL identification number for a 
category (24). Then, I manually identified the highest number of pages in one category (6), as 
one page only shows nine contests. To get all the contests in each, I iterated through all category 
URLs using the identification numbers between 1 and 24. For each of the categories, I also 
iterated through the page numbers from 1 to 6 to include all pages (e.g., 
https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contests?page={PAGENUMBER}&per_page=9&tags={CATE
GORY}&statuses=&sort=published&direction=desc). With the same method as described 
above, I extracted the texts for the titles and the contest ID of each contest to identify later 
which contest belongs to which category. I connected the contest titles with their corresponding 
content ID using tuples. Each category list consisted of up to 54 tuples. Thus, the most popular 
category had 54 contests. Using pandas (McKinney et al., 2010), I combined the lists in a data 
frame and saved them in a CSV data file. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 
2.5. I provided a list of all categories in Appendix 2.8.  
I changed the contest IDs in the main data set to consistent three-figure values by adding zeros 
in front of the values if they were not three figures long. To add the categories to the contest 
data file, I created for each category a list and appended to that list 1 if the contest ID in the 
contest data file matched a contest ID in the category and 0 if it did not. Finally, I added all the 
category lists as columns to the contest data file and saved it again. I provided the corresponding 
Python code in Appendix 2.6. 
After adding the categories to the contest data file, I removed duplicated columns and renamed 
the remaining ones according to their content. Using the length function, I appended the length 
of the title, subtitle, and overview text for each contest. I removed disruptive or unnecessary, 
reoccurring strings (e.g., “contributions”) from the number of contributions, participants, 
comments, evaluations, and views using the replace function to obtain only integers in the 
corresponding columns. Further, I reassigned (during the scraping) misaligned values by 
==PAGE:46==
37 
 
starting at the first affected column and controlling, if stripping the corresponding value reduces 
its length in a cell. If it did not, I assigned the value from the next column to the cell and checked 
again if it was affected by the stripping. I repeated this process until I appropriately aligned all 
data. I used the parse function to convert the dates into an appropriate data type and converted 
numeric values to integers. Finally, I dropped more theoretically irrelevant, duplicate (e.g., 
indices), or inconsistent (e.g., end of announcement period) columns and saved the data in a 
CSV data file. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 2.7. 
4.2.3 Data Acquisition - HYVECrowd Contributions 
To extract the essential data about the contributions, I started with the contribution overview 
for each contest using an approach with Python (Python Software Foundation, 2023) and 
Selenium (Selenium, 2023) as described above. I manually extracted the maximum of pages for 
a single contribution overview (23). Using the contest IDs extracted earlier, I iterated through 
the corresponding URLs for each contest and each contribution overview page (e.g., 
https://hyvecrowd.com/contest/{CONTEST}/contribution?page={PAGE}&per_page=18&tag
s=&phases=&sort=created_at&direction=desc) and extracted the solver`s name, the 
submission date, the title, the subtitle, the link, the contest ID and the contribution ID. The 
extraction followed the same approach as described above. First, I manually extracted the 
HTML paths in Chrome. Second, I used Selenium`s web driver, find elements function and the 
By.XPATH method (Selenium, 2023). I found the contribution ID by stripping the link to the 
solution page down to the ID. The IDs are increasing numbers from 1 to over 11,000. I used the 
replace function to generate consistent five-figure IDs. I extracted the number of likes, 
comments, views, and, if available, the solvers` contribution evaluations. I used the split 
function to assign the values for the number of likes, comments, and views and the evaluations 
to different columns. I combined the data for all contests and contributions and saved it in a 
CSV data file. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 3.1. 
After the overview scraping, I used the extracted contribution links to address each contribution 
page. However, one must log into the website to call the contribution data. I targeted the login 
fields using their HTML paths similar to those described above and used Selenium`s send keys 
function to insert my username and key. Further, I used its click function to activate the login. 
Another barrier to automatic scraping of the solutions is that one cannot start the login without 
accepting or denying the cookie usage on the website. I found the path to address this overlay 
behind a shadow root by manually using Chrome`s query Selector function (Google, 2023). 
Again, I used Selenium`s click function to close the overlay if it occurred. I used the same 
==PAGE:47==
38 
 
method with Selenium as described above to extract the data. For each contribution, I extracted 
the solution ID, the solver`s name, the text, the number of likes and comments, the user`s 
evaluation, the number of user evaluations, the users` evaluations for certain sub-criteria, the 
comment texts, and the comments` author names. During the scraping, I used the replace 
method to strip disruptive or unnecessary, reoccurring strings (e.g., "Published on" or "Share 
on Facebook") and paragraphs from the texts to shorten the data cleaning afterward. I combined 
the data for all solutions, renamed the columns according to their content, and saved them in a 
CSV data file. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 3.3. 
To combine the contribution overview data file with the contribution data file, I added the 
number of evaluations column and a text column to the contribution overview. Further, I 
assigned the corresponding values from the contribution data file if the solution IDs matched, 
as described above. I saved the combined data into a CSV data file. In the same step, I also 
added the number of evaluations to the data set. I provided the corresponding Python code in 
Appendix 3.5. 
To identify which contribution got an award, I again used a similar approach with selenium 
(Selenium, 2023) as described above. First, I manually identified the URL identification 
numbers for each place in the top ten (26-34 and 127). Some contests had only three winners, 
others up to twelve. Then, I iterated through all URLs with the contest IDs and the identification 
numbers to identify each winning contribution (e.g., Https://hyvecrowd.com/contest/ 
{CONTEST}/contribution?page=1&per_page=9&tags={PLACE}&phases=&sort=created_at
&direction=desc). As described above, I extracted the titles and IDs for each solution to later 
identify which contest got which place, combined the data, and saved it in a CSV data file. 
Finally, I rearranged the data file to have the solutions as rows and the corresponding data types 
as columns. I added the place to the main contribution data file by adding a place column to the 
data and assigning the corresponding place to the row if the solution IDs matched. I manually 
added the winners if they could not be extracted automatically. I provided the corresponding 
Python codes in Appendix 3.6, 3.7, and 3.8. 
Further, I stripped the remaining disruptive or unnecessary, reoccurring strings from the 
solution data file with the same stripping method as the one described above. Further, I used 
Python`s length function to add text, title, and subtitle lengths to the data set. I provided the 
corresponding Python code in Appendix 3.9.  
I should note that I mostly iterated only through parts of the URLs at once if the scraping was 
very time-consuming and combined them later. This approach prevented significant time and 
==PAGE:48==
39 
 
data loss in the case that the program would have stopped due to external problems or missing 
values. To combine the partial datasets, I used Python (Python Software Foundation, 2023) to 
import them as panda`s data frames (McKinney et al., 2010), combined them into a list and 
converted the merged list back to a data frame, and save it as a CSV data file. I used this 
approach for the solutions and solution overviews. I provided the corresponding Python Code 
in Appendix 3.2 and 3.4. In other cases, I used the copy function in the open command prompt 
(CMD) for Windows 11 (Microsoft Corporation, 2023). 
Finally, the winners in one contest (ID: 197) were published during my research, I used the 
same approach as presented above with Python and Selenium and the contest`s ID to extract 
the data of the corresponding contest again. Then, I manually searched for the winners of that 
contest. I provided the corresponding Python code in Appendix 3.10.  
 4.3 Methodology – Application Fields & Motivation 
To answer the first and the second research question about the relation between contest 
characteristics (e.g., application field and identity disclosure) and the number of solvers, I 
performed multivariate linear regressions to investigate the variables that might influence the 
number of solvers. At first, I used the Innocentive dataset. 
First, after the descriptive research presented below, I defined quadratic and logarithmic 
versions of the variables that I predicted to have a non-linear relationship with the number of 
solvers. These were the award size, the text length, and the time of the contest. I introduced 
time as a control variable to handle time-dependent, platform-wide trends. I predicted that the 
platform would attract more users over time, which might lead to more active solvers. I defined 
interaction terms for the award, text, disclosure, and time variable with the categories and 
showcases to control for interaction effects.   
Second, I performed multivariate linear regressions. The dependent variable was the number of 
solvers in a contest. I used an iterative approach to find the model that best describes the 
variance in the dependent variable. Thus, I started with one independent variable (e.g., award 
size) and added variables individually until I maximized the adjusted R-squared. The adjusted 
R-squared indicates the proportion of the variance in the dependent variable explained by the 
model while controlling for the number of independent variables (Long and Ervin, 2000). It 
takes on values between 0 (no explanation power) and 1 (it explains all variance). A higher 
adjusted R-squared indicates a better model. Therefore, I did not include variables that 
decreased the adjusted R-squared, which is the case if the variable does not significantly help 
==PAGE:49==
40 
 
to describe the variation in the dependent variable. During the process, some variables lost their 
description power, as I added other variables that described the identical characteristics of the 
variation in the dependent variable better. I excluded them again if they lost their explanatory 
power. After testing all variables and maximizing the adjusted R-squared, I screened all 
excluded variables again, as they might have become significant after including other variables. 
If I had excluded any of the variables of the final models the adjusted R-squared would have 
decreased.  
As the linear regression controls for linear relations between the independent and the dependent 
variable, I tried quadratic and logarithmic values of the variables that I predicted to have non-
linear relationships with the number of solvers. I found the most significant linear relationship 
between the award and the number of solvers for the natural logarithm of the award size. The 
same applies to the relation between time and the number of solvers. For the text length, the 
absolute value was most significantly linear related to the number of solvers. Thus, I included 
the forms of these variables with the highest descriptive power. 
The base model included the following task characteristic variables: The logarithmic award size 
was the value of the natural logarithm of the award size, stated in dollars. It controlled for the 
influence of the award size on the number of solvers. The time was the value of the time, which 
was measured by assigning increasing indices to the contest. The first contest posted on the 
platform had a time value of 1, and the last had a time value of the number of contests posted 
on the platform. While this method is only a proxy for the age of the contest, it is assumed to 
be precise enough for this research. I could not obtain a timestamp for each contest, as the 
platform did not provide it, see above. The time controlled for time-specific influences on the 
number of solvers (e.g., I expected an increase in the number of solvers over time). The 
overview length was the length of the overview text, which I measured by the number of 
characters in the text. It controlled for the length of the contest overview. The seeker known 
variable was 1 if the seeker disclosed their identity and 0 if they did not. It controlled for the 
influence of identity disclosure on the number of solvers. The final task characteristics variable 
was ideation. It was 1 if the contest was an ideation content and 0 if it was not. The ideation 
variable controlled for the difference between early innovation contests and other contests and 
how it influenced the number of solvers.  
4.3.1 Application Field and Motivation I 
Four categories and four showcase variables significantly improved the model. Math & 
Statistics, Chemistry, Business & Entrepreneurship, and Engineering & Design were 1 if the 
==PAGE:50==
41 
 
contest was related to the specific field and 0 if not. It controlled for the difference between 
tasks from that field and tasks from other fields and how this difference influenced the number 
of solvers. Civil Society, Elanco, Tec Edge Air Force, and Emerging Economies were 1 if the 
contest was part of the specific showcase and 0 if not. It controlled for the difference between 
tasks in the corresponding showcase and other tasks and how this difference influenced the 
number of solvers. It also controlled for the higher commitment by the group of solvers that 
joined the showcase to that application field. I provided a correlation matrix for the used 
variables in Appendix 4.8 and the R code for the inferential statistics in Appendix 4.10. They 
have no severe correlation (e.g., >0.8).  I presented the results from the first model in Table 
5.4.1 (A1). I denoted the model as follows. Note, in the following "𝛽0" denotes the intercept of 
the models, "𝛽𝑋" denotes the coefficient of the corresponding variable in the models, and "𝜀" 
denotes the error term of the models.  
𝑁𝑢𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟 𝑜𝑓 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑠= 𝛽0 +  𝛽1 ∗ln(𝐴𝑤𝑎𝑟𝑑) + 𝛽2 ∗𝑆𝑒𝑒𝑘𝑒𝑟 𝐾𝑛𝑜𝑤𝑛+ 𝛽3 ∗𝑇𝑒𝑥𝑡 𝐿𝑒𝑛𝑔𝑡ℎ+  𝛽4 ∗𝑇𝑖𝑚𝑒

Business & Entrepreneurship. I calculated it by multiplying the binary Seeker Known variable 
with the binary Business & Entrepreneurship variable. Thus, the interaction term was 0 if either 
or both variables were zero and 1 if both were one. It controlled for the moderating effect of 
identity disclosure on the relationship between the business fields and the number of solvers. I 
denoted the model as follows. 
𝑁𝑢𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟 𝑜𝑓 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑠= 𝛽0 +  𝛽1−6 ∗𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑠𝑡 𝐶ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑖𝑐𝑠 + 𝛽7−10 ∗𝐶𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑔𝑜𝑟𝑖𝑒𝑠 
+ 𝛽11−13 ∗𝑆ℎ𝑜𝑤𝑐𝑎𝑠𝑒𝑠+ 𝛽14 ∗𝑆𝑒𝑒𝑘𝑒𝑟 𝐾𝑛𝑜𝑤𝑛∗𝐵𝑢𝑠𝑖𝑛𝑒𝑠𝑠+ 𝜀 
Finally, the sixth model (Table 5.4.1, A6) included the interaction term between Seeker Known 
and Chemistry. I defined it correspondingly, see above. It controlled for the moderating effect 
of identity disclosure on the relationship between the chemistry fields and the number of 
solvers. I denoted the model as follows. 
𝑁𝑢𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟 𝑜𝑓 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑠= 𝛽0 +  𝛽1−6 ∗𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑠𝑡 𝐶ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑖𝑐𝑠 + 𝛽7−10 ∗𝐶𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑔𝑜𝑟𝑖𝑒𝑠

4.3.2 Application Fields and Motivation II  
While the first series of models can show the importance of particular categories and showcases, 
its formulation involved many possible variables (e.g., showcases and categories), which led to 
a small number of “True” values in the binary categories and decreased the generalizability of 
inducted results. Thus, I grouped the categories and showcases in variables of similar ones. I 
based the grouping on similarity, the significance of their t-tests, and the direction of their t-test 
(positive vs. negative). I provided the R code for all inferential statistics on the grouped data set 
in Appendix 4.11. 
==PAGE:52==
43 
 
Three groups of categories and three groups of showcases significantly improved the second 
range of models. They started with the same contest characteristics as the first. I formulated 
them using a similar approach as the one described above (e.g., starting with one independent 
variable and individually adding more).   
The category group Data was 1 if a contest was either in the category Business & 
Entrepreneurship, Math & Statistics, or both, and 0 if it was in neither. It controlled for the 
difference between the contests in the Data group and the other contests and how this difference 
influenced the number of solvers. The category group Engineering was 1 if a contest was either 
in the category Computer & Information Technology, Engineering & Design, or in both, and 0 
if it was in neither. It controlled for the difference between the contests in the Engineering group 
and the other contests and how this difference influenced the number of solvers. The category 
group Bio was 1 if a contest was either in the category Chemistry, Food, or both, and 0 if it was 
in neither. It controlled for the difference between the contests in the Bio group and the other 
contests and how this difference influenced the number of solvers. 
The showcase group Pharma was 1 if a contest was either in the showcase Elanco or the 
showcase Novartis and 0 if it was in neither. It controlled for the difference between the contests 
in the Pharma group and the other contests and how this difference influenced the number of 
solvers. The showcase group Aerospace was 1 if a contest was either in the showcase Tec Edge 
Air Force or the NASA showcase, and 0 if it was neither. It controlled for the difference between 
the contests in the Aerospace group and the other contests and how this difference influenced 
the number of solvers. The showcase group Social was 1 if a contest was in either of the 
showcases World Vision, Emerging Economies, Civil Society, Covid-19, or Habitat for 
Humanity and 0 if it was in none of the above. It controlled for the difference between the 
contests in the Social group and the other contests and how this difference influenced the 
number of solvers. I provided a correlation matrix for the used variables in Appendix 4.7. They 
have no severe correlation (e.g., >0.8). Table 5.4.2 (B1) shows the results of the initial model. 
I denoted it as follows. 
𝑁𝑢𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟 𝑜𝑓 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑠= 𝛽0 +  𝛽1 ∗ln(𝐴𝑤𝑎𝑟𝑑) + 𝛽2 ∗𝑆𝑒𝑒𝑘𝑒𝑟 𝐾𝑛𝑜𝑤𝑛+ 𝛽3 ∗𝑇𝑒𝑥𝑡 𝐿𝑒𝑛𝑔𝑡ℎ+  𝛽4 ∗𝑇𝑖𝑚𝑒 
  +  𝛽5 ∗𝑇𝑖𝑡𝑙𝑒 𝐿𝑒𝑛𝑔𝑡ℎ+  𝛽6 ∗𝐼𝑑𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 +  𝛽7 ∗𝐷𝑎𝑡𝑎+  𝛽8 ∗𝐸𝑛𝑔𝑖𝑛𝑒𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑛𝑔+  𝛽9 ∗𝐵𝑖𝑜

4.3.3 Application Fields and Motivation Control  
I applied both model series also to a smaller data set, see below, to test the robustness of the 
findings, especially regarding the Seeker Known variable. I provided the results for the 
ungrouped models on the smaller data set in Appendix 4.3 and for the grouped ones in Appendix 
4.4. I used the same models as for the big data set to achieve comparability, even though there 
might be better models to describe the variation in the number of solvers in the small data set. 
Further, I included the contests that the platform did not label in a different data set to increase 
the sample size and test the robustness of the findings. I manually defined the category variables 
for over 250 contests, as the platform did not label early contests. Thus, this extended data set 
had 1174 contests. However, the category definitions might not be in line with the platform`s 
labeling. I applied the grouped and ungrouped linear model series to the new data set and used 
the big sample Seeker Known definition, see below. I provided the results for the grouped 
models in Table 5.4.3 and the ungrouped models in Appendix 4.5. I used the same models and 
data handling as described above to achieve comparability, even though there might be better 
models to describe the variation in the number of solvers also in the extended data set. 
4.3.4 Methodology – Application Fields and Motivation: Contributions 
I investigated the relationships to the number of contributions separately, as the descriptive part 
suggested contradicting findings for the number of contributions and solvers, see below. The 
Innocentive data set did not include the number of contributions. Thus, I used the HYVECrowd 
contest data set. The number of observations in that data set is comparably low. Thus, one 
should not overestimate the implications of the findings but they could provide a basis for future 
research on the difference between the motivation to contribute and to participate, and its 
implications for seekers and researchers. I provided the R code for the following inferential 
statistics in Appendix 5.4. 
First, I applied a linear regression with the Number of Contributions as the dependent variable 
to the data set. I included the Number of Participants, the Time, the Award Type, the Subtitle 
Length, and the categories Financial Services, Aerospace, Mechanical & Plant Engineering, 
and Entertainment as independent variables in the ungrouped model. The Time is the number 
of the contest as a proxy for the time. The Award Type is 1 if the contest provided a monetary 
award and 0 otherwise. The Subtitle Length is the length of the contest`s subtitle in characters 
==PAGE:55==
46 
 
and can be perceived as a proxy for complexity or purpose. I changed the categories to the 
groups Engineering, Logistics, and Entertainment in the grouped model. I provided the results 
in Table 5.4.4 (Models H1 and H3).  
Second, I applied a linear regression with the Number of Solvers as the dependent variable to 
the data set to compare for differences. I included the Number of Participants, the Time, the 
Award Type, the Number of Views, and the categories Tourism, Automobile, and Mechanical 
& Plant Engineering as independent variables in the ungrouped model. I changed the categories 
to the groups Logistics, Consumption, Social, and Entertainment, and had to exclude the Award 
Type in the grouped model. I provided the results in Table 5.4.4 (Models H2 and H4). While I 
used consistent variables in all other comparing analyses in this study, I used different variables 
to describe the Number of Solvers and the Number of Contributions. I optimized the description 
of the variance of the two variables. I used this approach to compare the different influence 
factors that lead to either. Compared to the other comparing analysis, this analysis had two 
different independent variables and not just different data sets.     
4.4 Methodology – Evaluation  
I performed a multivariate logistical regression to investigate the variables that might indicate 
a winning solution to answer the research questions about the solver`s evaluation.  
First, after the descriptive research presented below, I defined mean and maximal values for the 
evaluations and weighted evaluations, the contribution lengths, the number of views, likes, 
comments, and evaluations for all contributions in each contest. Second, I defined quadratic 
and logarithmic versions of the tested variables to control for non-linearity. Third, I defined 
interaction variables for the evaluation with the number of views, likes, comments, and the 
Time variable. 
Fourth, I performed a logistical regression. The dependent variable was a binary variable that 
was 1 if the contribution won an award in the contest (e.g., got an award) and 0 if it did not. 
Thus, the coefficients for the independent variables indicate the influence that a change in that 
variable has on the probability that a contribution is winning an award. To find the model that 
best describes the variance in the dependent variable, I formulated the logistic regression model 
with one independent variable (e.g., winning percentage); and added variables that decreased 
the Akaike Information Criterion (AIC) until I minimized it. If I had excluded any variables 
from the final model, the AIC would have increased. The AIC has a similar function as the R-
squared. It indicates how well the chosen combination of independent variables describes the 
==PAGE:56==
47 
 
variation in the dependent variable (Akaike, 1974). A lower value indicates a better fit. Similar 
to the investigation on the number of solvers, I revisited prior excluded variables after 
formulating the final model to prevent excluding any variables with description power.  
Model A.1 (Table 5.5.1, first column) includes the control variables for the competitiveness of 
a contest. The Winners variable controls for the number of awards the seeker hands out. It is 
the absolute number of winners in a contest. The Winning Chance controls for the relative 
competition per award. I calculated it by dividing the number of winners by the number of 
contributions. I denoted Model A.1 as follows. 
ln (

1 −𝑝(𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑛𝑒𝑟)) = 𝛽0 +  𝛽1 ∗𝑊𝑖𝑛𝑛𝑒𝑟𝑠+ 𝛽2 ∗𝑊𝑖𝑛𝑛𝑖𝑛𝑔 𝐶ℎ𝑎𝑛𝑐𝑒+ 𝜀 
Model A.2 (Table 5.5.1, second column) adds the independent variables for the solver`s 
evaluation and their contest-specific control variables. The Weighted Evaluation controls for 
the wisdom of crowds effect that should lead to better evaluations by more (vs. less) solvers. It 
is the evaluation of the contribution by the solvers weighted by the number of evaluations. The 
Average Weighted Evaluation controls the average quality and willingness to evaluate 
positively in a particular contest weighted by the number of evaluations. It is the mean of the 
weighted evaluations for all contributions in a contest. I denoted Model A.2 as follows.

Model A.3 (Table 5.5.1, third column) adds the other engagement variables. Likes, which is the 
number of likes of a contribution, control in another way the ability of the solvers to evaluate 
contributions in line with the jury. Average Likes controls for the average quality and 
willingness to like in a particular contest. It is the mean number of likes of all contributions in 
a contest. Comments control for the more in-depth interest of the solvers in a contribution and 
their willingness to cooperate on it. It is the number of comments on a contribution. Maximum 
Comments control for the in-depth interest and willingness to cooperate in a contest. It is the 
maximum number of comments on all contributions in a contest. I will later discuss the 
maximum number of comments compared to the mean number of comments as a control 
variable. Finally, the mean of Average Weighted Evaluation was excluded from the model as 
it worsened the model. I denoted Model A.3 as follows.

After defining the models, I tested them on a contest that ended during the research period 
(Contest 197). I used the fourth model to predict the probability of winning for each contribution 
in Contest 197. I provided the predicted y-values, the winning probabilities for the winning 
contributions in Table 5.5.2, and the R code in Appendix 6.3.  
To test the robustness of the models, I used different methods to include the prior excluded 
contributions, see below. First, I assigned the average evaluation value and the average number 
of evaluations of the corresponding contest to the unevaluated contributions. I provided the R 
code in Appendix 6.4. It increased the data set to 6836 observations. I used the same data 
manipulations and models as for the first analysis. That way, I assured comparability. However, 
there might have been other models that would have described the variation in the winning 
probability better for this new data set. I provided the results in Table 5.5.3. After defining the 
models, I also tested them on Contest 197. I used the fourth model to predict the probability of 
winning for each contribution in Contest 197. I provided the predicted y-values and winning 
probabilities for the winning contributions in Table 5.5.4.  
Second, I assigned the average number of evaluations in the whole data set to all contributions 
from which I could extract the evaluation but not the number of evaluations. I provided the R 
code in Appendix 6.5. It increased the data set to 8505 observations. Again, I used the same 
data manipulations and models as for the first analysis and assured comparability. However, it 
still holds that there might be models that describe the variance in the winning probability better 
than the ones I used. I provided the results in Table 5.5.5. After defining the models, I also 
tested them on Contest 197. I used the fourth model to predict the probability of winning for 
==PAGE:58==
49 
 
each contribution in Contest 197. I provided the predicted y-values and winning probabilities 
for the winning contributions in Table 5.5.6.  
Finally, I assigned the average number of evaluations and average evaluations for the whole 
data set to all contributions from which I could not extract an evaluation. I provided the R code 
in Appendix 6.6. It increased the data set to 9618 observations. Again, I used the same data 
manipulations and models as for the first analysis and assured comparability. However, it still 
holds that there might be models that describe the variance in the winning probability better 
than the ones I used. I provided the results in Table 5.5.7. After defining the models, I also 
tested them on Contest 197, as described above. I provided the results in Table 5.5.8.  
4.5 Interviews  
I conducted three interviews with industry experts after the statistical analysis, discussion, and 
conclusion. The interviews had no quantitative purpose. Thus, I purely handled them 
qualitatively. I used the interviews to extend the discussion part, improve the managerial 
implications, and show the relevance of this study for crowdsourcing professionals. I selected 
potential interview partners based on their involvement in crowdsourcing contests on the 
platform HYVECrowd. I reached out to jury members of contests that ended in 2022 or 2023 
via LinkedIn (www.linkedin.com). Overall, I contacted 5 experts and got 4 positive replies, 
from which 3 resulted in an interview. I consider this an unusually high respondence rate, which 
indicates a high interest in the findings of this study. This will be further discussed below. The 
interviews were conducted via Microsoft Teams (www.microsoft.com/en-us/microsoft-teams) 
and each had a length of approximately 45 minutes. During the interview, I presented my 
findings and asked questions using presentation slides. The questions aimed at connecting the 
findings from the statistical investigations with the real-world implementation of 
crowdsourcing contests. I provided a representative example for the slides in Appendix 10.  
The first interview partner (further referred to as HYVECrowd responsible) introduced himself 
as follows (I1): He is responsible for the HYVECrowd and worked for around 12 years in this 
area. He also did considerable research in the area, both in his studies and work. Currently, next 
to his work for HYVE, he is pursuing a Ph.D. in the innovation and technology field. At HYVE, 
he bears the responsibility for all crowdsourcing activities. Further, he manages and is 
responsible for innovation processes in innovation consulting. This includes identifying trends, 
existing technologies, and customer needs, deriving strategies, identifying application areas, 
and prototyping. Through his background and current responsibilities in crowdsourcing, he is 
ideally qualified to discuss and evaluate the findings of this study, especially as he is responsible 
==PAGE:59==
50 
 
for one of the investigated platforms. He assumed that he has a good overview of crowdsourcing 
topics and is a good information source (I1). I provided the first interview in Appendix 7.  
In the second interview, I interviewed two practitioners from one company. First, a CEO of 
OeNPAY. He described himself and OeNPAY as follows (I2): He worked for over 30 years in 
banking and consults the National Bank of Austria. He deals with the two big payment 
transaction questions. These are the identification in payment transactions and the digitalization 
of payment transactions. They help the bank understand who transfers, what is transferred, and 
which problems might occur. He is a self-declared crowdsourcing fan and was the main initiator 
of their contest. Second, a Senior Innovation Manager of OeNPAY. She described herself as 
following (I2): She studied computer science and IT management, is at OeNPAY for one and 
a half years, and is responsible for innovation in payment transactions. The contest was one of 
her first assignments at OeNPAY and she considered it quite a challenge at the beginning. I 
provided the second interview in Appendix 8.  
The final interviewee was the Open Innovation Lead at UNIQA and introduced herself as 
following (I3): She is two years at UNIQA and responsible for open innovation. Her 
background is in marketing, but she diverted quickly in the direction of customer experience 
and innovation with a general human-centered design focus. She was responsible for their 
contest, as crowdsourcing is an important part of open innovation. However, she is not a big 
fan of crowdsourcing and UNIQA`s experience with crowdsourcing is quite limited. Their 
contest was very much lead user and research oriented. She thinks that companies should mostly 
learn from customers but designers should make the products. I provided the third interview in 
Appendix 9.   
To summarize, the first interview was very method-oriented and from the platform perspective. 
The second interview included a very enthusiastic perspective on crowdsourcing and 
experience in crowdsourcing from the seeker’s perspective. The third interview included a very 
skeptical perspective on crowdsourcing and less experience from the seeker perspective. Thus, 
I conducted three interviews that included three very diverse perspectives on crowdsourcing 
contests. The following chapter presents the results of the quantitative investigation.

5 Results 
5.1 Descriptive Statistics – Innocentive  
I performed the following statistics, data manipulations, and representations, if not cited 
otherwise, with packages that are part of the programming language R (R Core Team, 2021). I 
derived minimum, maximum, mean, and median values with the psych package for R (Revelle, 
2022). Further, I created the data subsets using the dply package for R (Wickham et al., 2021) 
and the result tables for the regression models with the stargazer package for R (Hlavac, M. 
2022). I provided the R code for the following descriptive statistics in Appendix 4.9.  
The initial Innocentive data set consisted of 1425 contests and 70 variables. However, after 
removing the instances that contained requests for partners and not innovation contests (134), I 
reduced the dataset to 1291 instances. The contests that did not provide the award size (109) 
were kept and used in the descriptive analysis. However, I excluded them from the inductive 
analysis for obvious reasons. I handled the contests that had no application fields (290) similar. 
I will address the implications of the reductions later. The variables consisted mainly of 
showcases (25), application fields (10), and task types (5). I also included the title and overview 
lengths, the award size, and the number of solvers. Finally, binary variables controlled for the 
disclosure of the seeker`s identity. Some variables only consisted of data for a small subset of 
contests, thus, I did not include them in the analysis (e.g., the structure of the overview). The 
maximum number of solvers was 5536, and the minimum was 0 at the extraction time. The 
mean was 314.89, and the median was 249. I provided further descriptive statistics in Table 
5.1.1.

evident. Thus, I excluded them in the following (Figure 5.1.1.B). The smoothened line, which 
visualizes the relationship between the award size and the number of solvers after cleaning the 
outliers, suggested a tendentially logarithmic relationship (Figure 5.1.2).

The high number of showcases and application fields decreases the generalizability of the 
findings and might introduce too many variables with a low variation in the following data 
analysis. Thus, I grouped the application fields and showcases in variables of similar ones. I 
based the grouping on similarity, the significance of their t-tests, and the direction of their t-test 
(positive vs. negative). The Data fields consisted of Math & Statistics and Business & 
Entrepreneurship. The Engineering fields consisted of Computer & Information Technology 
and Engineering & Design. The Bio fields consisted of Chemistry and Food. The Social 
showcases consisted of World Vision, Emerging Economies, Civil Society, Covid-19, and 
Habitat for Humanity. The Aerospace showcases consisted of NASA and Tec Edge Air Force. 
The Pharma showcases consisted of Elanco and Novartis. The T-test for all groupings showed 
a highly significant difference in the number of attracted solvers between the groups and other 
contests. The representations for Engineering (Figure 5.1.13.A) and Data (Figure 5.1.13.B) 
clearly supported the findings visually. The representations for the others showed also 
substantial differences (Appendix 4.6).

I defined the variable that indicated if the seeker disclosed their identity twice. First, if I could

not known), as a manual check of a sample indicated that this might be true for (almost) all of 
the affected contests. The first definition (further referred to as small sample) of the variable is 
more precise, but the second (further referred to as big sample) provides substantially more 
incidences. I will later compare the results to identify differences and similarities between the 
outcomes. I  expected a positive relationship between the disclosure of the seeker`s identity and 
the number of solvers.

 Finally, after describing the relationships between the variables above and the number of 
solvers, I investigated the potential moderating effects of variables on the relationships between 
the number of solvers and other variables. I used the logarithm of the award size for the 
descriptive analysis, as suggested by prior research (DiPalantino and Vojnovic, 2009). I found 
four potentially moderating effects on the influence of the award size. First, Figure 5.1.19 
clearly indicated a difference in the relationship between the award size and the number of 
solvers for high award sizes. An increase in high logarithmic award sizes seemed to attract more 
solvers in mathematical tasks than in others. While the relationship in other application fields 
seemed to be tendentially logarithmic, it seemed inverted U-shaped for small- and medium-
sized awards and linear for high award sizes in mathematical application fields. Second, Figure 
5.1.20 clearly indicated differences in the relationship between the award size and the number 
of solvers over time. The attractiveness of high awards seemed to have decreased over time, 
and smaller rewards seem to be sufficient to attract solvers now. Third, Figure 5.1.21 also 
clearly indicated a difference in the relationship between the award size and the number of 
solvers, if the contest is associated with data or if it is not. Even extremer than the moderating 
effect of mathematical fields alone, the increase in solvers is way steeper for all award sizes 
except for the extreme decrease for medium-sized awards. An increase in the logarithmic award 
size seems to attract more solvers in data tasks than in others except for medium-sized awards. 
Fourth, Figur 5.1.22 also clearly indicates a difference in the relationship. It seems to be 
inverted U-shaped for social tasks but S-shaped for others. An increase in high awards seems 
==PAGE:68==
59 
 
to decrease the attractiveness to contribute for solvers in social tasks. I plotted the following 
graphs using ggplot 2 for R (Wickham, 2016).

Key points: 
1. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of the award size and type.  
2. I did not find descriptive indications for the relevance of innovation phases. 
3. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of the task specificity. 
4. I did not find descriptive indications for the relevance of identity disclosure. 
5. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of application fields. 
6. I found descriptive indications suggesting that the relevance of the award size and 
identity disclosure might be moderated by different factors.

5.2 Descriptive Statistics - HYVECrowd Contests  
The initial contest data from HYVECrowd consisted of 82 contests with 41 variables each. 
Application fields constituted most of the variables (24). The data set contained the number of 
contributions, solvers, comments, evaluations, and views. Further, it included the titles, 
subtitles, overview texts and their prior calculated lengths, the end dates of the contest and 
evaluation phases, and the awards and award types. The most represented application field was 
Sustainability (25), followed by Consumer Goods and Trade (24) and Social (24). The least 
represented application fields were Aerospace (4) and Fashion (4), followed by Open 
Government (5) and Telecommunication (5). The mean number of occurrences in an 
application field is 11.46, and the median is 10. I provided an overview of the application fields 
and their number of occurrences in Table 5.2.1. The number of contributions ranged from 2 to 
402, had a mean of 117.39, and a median of 102. The number of solvers ranged from 6 to 343, 
had a mean of 105.26, and a median of 94. Fifty-three of the awards were, at least mainly, 
monetary. The rest consisted of, e.g., travel vouchers, invitations, and tablets. I provided further 
descriptive statistics in Table 5.2.2. I provided the R code for the following descriptive statistics 
in Appendix 5.3.

5.2.1 Number of Contributions 
I expected a positive relationship between the number of solvers and the number of 
contributions. The platform regards each user that signs up for the contest as a solver. A 
contribution is not necessary to gain that status. Their correlation was 43.20%. The plot (Figure 
5.2.1) of the number of solvers and contributions indicated a curvilinear relationship, but only 
for extreme cases that one might discount as outliers. In general, it clearly suggested a positive, 
linear relation. More solvers, intuitively, generate more contributions.

contributions indicated a curvilinear relationship, but only for extreme cases that one might 
discount as outliers. In this case, even more so than for the number of solvers, as they are clearly 
the reason for the low correlation. Thus, I excluded them later. In general, the visual

I disregarded the relation of the number of contributions with the number of comments and 
evaluations, as the number of contributions might influence the number of comments and 
evaluations rather than vice versa. Thus, the relation might describe the wrong direction for this 
research purpose or it at least suffers substantial reverse causality difficulties.  
I assumed that solvers prefer a monetary award over a non-monetary reward, as monetary 
awards are independent of the solver`s preferences. The correlation between the award type and 
the number of contributions (36.92%) supported this theory. The plot (Figure 5.2.3) indicated 
a clear difference, especially in the case of many contributions. This difference may, however, 
also be due to the circumstance that the monetary awards might mostly have a higher objective 
value than the non-monetary ones.

I assumed that some application fields have a significant relationship with the number of 
solvers. However, only the mean of the number of contributions of the Entertainment field 
differed significantly from that of all others. The p-value of the corresponding t-test (0.0262) 
was the only significant one (e.g., p < 0.05) for all application fields. The correlation between

the variable Entertainment and the number of contributions was -20.77%. The plot supports this 
visually but also indicates the importance of outliers (Figure 5.2.4). However, Financial 
Services (Figure 5.2.5) and Mobility (Figure 5.2.6) showed clear visual differences.  
I assigned the relevant application fields into groups, as using 24 variables for around 80 
instances is statistically unpractical. I used the results from the t-tests to identify relevant 
categories and grouped them according to their application field. I defined 5 groups that 
included 15 fields: Engineering, Logistics, Consumption, Social, and Entertainment. The 
Engineering contests had, in the mean, significantly more contributions than the other contests 
(p<0.05). Figure 5.2.7 supported this visually. The Social contests had, in the mean, also 
significantly more contributions than the other contests (p<0.05, Figure 5.2.8). The 
Entertainment group was identical to the Entertainment category. Thus, its contests had 
significantly fewer contributions than others in the mean (p<0.05, Figure 5.2.4). Logistics and 
Consumption did not show statistical relevance. I provided their figures in Appendix 5.1.

5.2.2 Number of Solvers 
I expected a positive relationship between the number of solvers and the number of views. A 
correlation of 63.36% clearly supported that. The plot (Figure 5.2.9) indicates a curvilinear 
relation between the number of solvers and views and shows the extreme outliers. Thus, I will 
exclude them later. Further, I assumed a similar relationship as for the number of contributions 
for the relation between the award type and the number of solvers. However, the correlation 
(4.52%) indicated barely any relationship between the variables. The plot (Figure 5.2.10) shows 
that this might be due to the outliers, but overall, the relationship between the award type and 
the number of solvers is weaker than the relationship between the award type and the number 
of contributions.

Solvers in Monetary Contests. Source: OR. 
I assumed that some application fields have a relationship with the number of solvers. 
Compared to the number of contributions, the mean number of solvers of more fields differed 
significantly from that of all other fields. IT had, in the mean, significantly fewer solvers than 
the other fields (p<0.01). Tourism had, in the mean, barely significantly more solvers than the 
others (p<0.1).  The visual representations for IT (Figure 5.2.11) and Tourism (Figure 5.2.12) 
showed substantial differences in the mean.  
 
Figure 5.2.11. Comparison between the Number of Solvers 
Figure 5.2.12. Comparison between the Number of          
in IT Contests and others. Source: OR.  
 
 
Solvers in Tourism Contests and others. Source: OR.   
Again, I used the groups defined above to achieve clearer results. There might be better groups 
to describe the variation in the number of solvers. However, I used consistent groups to maintain 
comparability between the number of solvers and the number of contributions as surrogates for 
crowdsourcing success. I found a significant difference in the mean for the group Logistics to 
the other contests (p<0.05). Figure 5.2.13 clearly supported this. The potentially most 
compelling discovery is that the Engineering variable went from a significantly positive 
relationship to the number of contributions to a tendentially negative relationship to the number 
of solvers (Figure 5.2.14). I provided the visual representation for Entertainment, Social, and 
Consumption in Appendix 5.2.

Key points: 
1. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of the award type.  
2. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of application fields. 
3. I found descriptive indications suggesting that application fields might have different 
relationships with the number of solvers than with the number of contributions. 
5.3 Descriptive Statistics - Evaluation 
The initial HYVECrowd contributions data set consisted of 9658 contributions. However, 3374 
did not include a solver evaluation, mainly because particular contests did not allow solvers to 
evaluate the contributions and because other contests had a barrier that prevented the scraping 
of the evaluations. The number of contests in the data set decreased from 83 to 72. After 
removing all contests that had less than 15 remaining contributions, 6256 contributions from 
65 contests were left. I will later discuss the implications of this data cleaning. The maximum 
number of contributions for a contest was 338, and the minimum was 15, with a mean of 146.95 
and a median of 133. The variables included the contribution date, the number of likes, 
comments, evaluations, and views. They also included the evaluation by other contributors, the 
placement in the competition (0, if not a winning contribution), and the length of the solution`s 
title, subtitle, and text.  
299 of the 6256 contributions won, correspondingly 5957 lost. I added a binary variable that 
was 1 if the contribution was winning and 0 if it was not. The mean of the solver evaluation 
score for the winning contributions was 2.18 (median 1.4). The maximum was 5, and the 
minimum was 1. Therefore, the evaluations represented the highest but also the lowest possible 
==PAGE:75==
66 
 
evaluation. Unless noted otherwise, I provided the R code for the following descriptive statistics 
in Appendix 6.1. 
5.3.1 Independent Variables 
The posting time variable, the amount of time that the solver posted its contribution after the 
first contribution in a contest, had a mean of 21.29 (median 16) and ranged from 0 (if it was the 
first contribution) to 199 (if the solver posted it on the last day). Most contests had a similar 
runtime. The density function of the posting time of all contributions (Figure 5.3.1) showed 
three peaks of contributions. The first peak occurred immediately after the beginning of the 
contest period, the second after approximately half of the contest period, and the last at the end. 
It indicates that solvers post either as fast as possible (most), after the time they need, or at the 
end of the period. The losing contributions density function looks similar (Figure 5.3.2). The 
density function for the winning contributions is different. Solvers posted the most winning 
contributions in the first half of the contest period. The density steeply declined after 
approximately the half-mark point (Figure 5.3.2). The decline is less steep during the first half 
of the contest period. However, some visual differences might be due to the significantly lower 
number of winning contributions. Thus, the density functions showed only a weak indication 
of a time-related influence on the winning chance. A t-test supported this showing only a 
slightly significant difference between the means of time of the winning and losing 
contributions (p<0.05).   
 
Figure 5.3.1. Density Function Time I. Source: Own representation. Figure 5.3.2. Density Function Time II. Black: All 
contributions, Red: Losing contributions, Green: Winning 
contributions. Source: Own representation. 
The number of views ranged from 1 to 2095 with a mean of 49.02 (median 35). The t-test 
between the means of views of the winners and the losers showed a highly significant difference 
(p<0.001). The difference was also clearly visible between the corresponding boxplots (Figure 
5.3.3). It indicated that the number of views for the winning contributions is significantly higher

than the number of views for the losing contributions. However, the variable should not be 
included in the following investigation, as this relationship suffered from severe reverse 
causality. Figure 5.3.4 clearly showed the reverse causality, comparing the change in the 
number of views before and after the evaluation of contribution 197, and indicated a crucial 
difference between the losing and winning ones. A t-test supported this and showed a highly 
significant difference between the means of change in views of the winning and losing 
contributions (p<0.001). I provided the R-code for the reverse causality tests in Appendix 6.2.

The number of likes ranged from 0 to 25 with a mean of 2 (median 1). The t-test between the 
means of views of the winners and the losers showed a significant difference (p<0.001). The 
difference was also clearly visible between the corresponding boxplots (Figure 5.3.5). It 
indicated that the number of likes for the winning contributions was significantly higher than 
the number of likes for the losing ones. The likes did not suffer from reverse causality. One 
cannot detect a significant difference in Figure 5.3.6 which compared the change in the number 
of likes before and after the evaluation of contribution 197 and indicated no difference between 
==PAGE:77==
68 
 
the losing and winning ones. Only one losing contribution received a like after the evaluation 
phase.  
The number of comments ranged from 0 to 46. They had a mean of 4.05 (median 3). The t-test 
between the means of comments of the winners and the losers showed a significant difference 
(p<0.001). The difference was also visible between the corresponding boxplots. However, not 
as convincing as the other independent variables (Figure 5.3.7). It still indicated that the average 
number of comments for the winning contributions was significantly higher than the average 
number of comments for the losing contributions. This relationship did not suffer from reverse 
causality. I did not detect a significant difference in Figure 5.3.8 which compared the change in 
the number of comments before and after the evaluation of contest 197 and indicated no 
difference between the losing and winning contributions. Only two losing contributions 
received comments after the evaluation phase.  
 
Figure 5.3.7. Comparison between the Number of Comments 
Figure 5.3.8. Comparison between the Evaluation Effect 
of Winning and Losing Contests. Source: OR.
