Figure 5.3.9. Comparison between the Number of Evaluations 
Figure 5.3.10.Comparison between the Evaluation Effect 
of Winning and Losing Contests. Source: OR. 
 
 
on Evaluations of Winning and Losing Contests. SO: OR.   
The number of evaluations had a mean of 3.08 (median 2) for all evaluated contributions and 
ranged from 1 to 29. For ten contests it was not possible to extract the number. Thus, the data 
==PAGE:78==
69 
 
set contained 4686 contributions and 49 contests, after removing them. I will adress this severe 
reduction later. The t-test between the number of evaluations on the winning and the losing 
contributions showed a statistically significant difference between the means (p<0.001). Figure 
5.3.9 provided only weak support for a difference similar to the number of comments. The 
number of evaluations suffered from no reverse causality, as solvers cannot evaluate after the 
end of the contest period (Figure 5.3.10). Thus, it also does not influence the evaluation itself.  
The solvers` evaluations as the most crucial independent variable had a mean of 2.21 (median 
1.4) for all contributions and ranged from the lowest (1) to the highest evaluation (5). The t-test 
between the evaluations of the winning and losing contributions showed no statistically 
significant difference between the means (p> 0.1). The lack of a difference is also clearly visible 
between the corresponding boxplots (Figure 5.3.11). It indicates that the evaluations for the 
winning contributions are not significantly higher than the evaluations for the losing 
contributions. 
 
 
Figure 5.3.11. Comparison between the Evaluation 
 
Figure 5.3.12. Comparison between the Weighted           
of Winning and Losing Contests. Source: OR. 
 
 
Evaluation of Winning and Losing Contests. SO: OR.   
As the evaluations showed no significant difference, I weighted them by the number of 
evaluations by multiplying the values. The weighted evaluation had a mean of 6.32 (median 4) 
for all contributions and ranged from 1 (one evaluation with an average rating of 1) to 105.8 
(twenty-three evaluations with an average rating of 4.6). Figure 5.3.12 indicated a slightly 
significant difference between the weighted evaluations of the winning and the losing 
contributions. The corresponding t-test showed a highly significant difference between the 
weighted evaluation of the winning and losing contributions` means (p<0.001).  
To summarize, the number of likes, comments, and evaluations seem to be positively related to 
winning. The number of views is also positively related, but this is at least partly due to reverse 
causality. The number of views after the evaluation increases way steeper for the winning 
contributions than for the losing ones. An early contribution time (e.g., in the first half of the 
==PAGE:79==
70 
 
contest period) might also have a positive relationship with winning. Finally, the most crucial 
metric solver evaluation seems unrelated to winning. However, if one weights it by the number 
of evaluations it might have a clear positive relation to it.    
5.3.2 Control Variables 
As each contest is different, it might influence the relative value of each proxy for quality. The 
mean of all contribution evaluations in a contest differs substantially between them. The highest 
evaluation mean of a contest is 4.25, and the lowest is 1.46. A rating of 3 would be far below 
the average in the first and far above in the second. Therefore, one must control for the average 
evaluation in each contest. The same argumentation holds for the mean of the number of 
comments per contribution in a contest (highest 7.55, lowest 0.52), the mean of the evaluation 
weight (highest 26.92, lowest 2.07), and the mean of the number of likes (highest 11.15, lowest 
1.03).  
Further, the number of contributions in a contest (highest 338, lowest 15), the number of unique 
solvers (highest 104, lowest 9), and the number of solutions per solver (highest 6.26, lowest 
1.16) could influence the chance of winning. The number of contributions in a contest control 
for its amount of absolute competition, as more contributions decrease the chance of winning 
for one. The number of unique solvers controls for the qualitative competition, as more unique 
solvers will increase the probability that a better solver participates. One could control for the 
same effect by the number of solutions per solver.  
Finally, the number of awards in a contest (highest 10, lowest 0) could influence the absolute 
chance of winning the contest. The number of awards per contribution (highest 0.21, lowest 0) 
could influence the relative chance of winning the contest. First, if the seeker hands out more, 
the probability increases that a single contribution wins. If the number of awards per 
contribution increases, the chance increases that a single contribution received an award.  
Key points: 
1. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of the number of likes. 
2. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of the number of comments.  
3. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of weighted evaluations. 
4. I found weak descriptive indications for the relevance of the contribution time. 
5. I found descriptive indications for the relevance of the number of views, but I had to 
exclude it from further investigations due to reverse causality. 
6. A manifold of variables might be used to control for contest dependent differences. 
==PAGE:80==
71 
 
5.4 Results – Application Fields & Motivation  
The results of the ungrouped data regressions (Table 5.4.1) showed positive coefficients for the 
Award Size (p<0.001), the Seeker Known (mostly p<0.05), the Ideation (p<0.01), all used 
application fields (mostly p<0.001) except for chemistry (p<0.001), and all showcases (mostly 
p<0.05) except for Elanco (p<0.1). The coefficients of the Title Length (p<0.05), the Text 
Length (p<0.001), and the Time (p<0.001) had negative signs. The interaction terms for Award 
& Math (p<0.05) and Seeker Known & Chemistry (p<0.01) had positive coefficients. Finally, 
the interaction terms for Award & Time (p<0.05), Seeker Known & Business (p<0.01), and 
Seeker Known & Math (p<0.01) had negative coefficients. All coefficients were at least on the 
10%-level significant, most on the 1%-level. Minor exceptions occured for the variables that 
were part of the introduced interaction terms. The constant was highly significant and negative 
in all models. The adjusted r-squared was between 0.28 and 0.29 for all models and increased 
with the interaction terms. Model A6 explained 28.7% of the variation in the number of solvers 
if one controls for the number of variables in the model. The F statistic showed a highly 
significant difference between all models to an empty model.  
The results of the grouped regressions (Table 5.4.2) showed positive coefficients for the Award 
Size (p<0.001), the Seeker Known (fluctuating significance), the Ideation (p<0.001), the Data 
(p<0.001), the Engineering (p<0.05), the Aerospace (p<0.05), and the Social group (p<0.001). 
They showed negative values for the Text Length (p<0.001), Title Length (p<0.01), Time 
(p<0.001), Bio (p<0.001), and the Pharma group (p>0.05). The interaction terms for Award & 
Data (p<0.05) and Seeker Known & Bio (p<0.001) had positive coefficients. The interaction 
terms for Award & Social (p<0.05), Seeker Known & Data (p<0.001), and Seeker Known & 
Engineering (p<0.01) had negative coefficients. The adjusted r-squared was around 0.3 for all 
models. Model B.6 explained 30.4% of the variation in the number of solvers if one controls 
for the number of variables used. The F statistic showed a highly significant difference between 
all models to an empty model. I will later discuss the implications of the findings. However, I 
should note that the variables that control for the contest characteristics behaved very similarly 
for both model ranges. Further, the second range of models described the variation in the 
independent variable better than the first one. 
The results of the grouped regressions (Table 5.4.3) for the extended data set showed positive 
coefficients for the Award Size (p<0.001), the Seeker Known (fluctuating significance), the 
Ideation (p<0.001), the Data (p<0.001), the Engineering (p<0.001), the Aerospace (p<0.01), 
and the Social group (p<0.001). They showed negative coefficients for the Text Length 
==PAGE:81==
72 
 
(p<0.001), Title Length (p<0.05), Time (p<0.001), Bio (p<0.001), and Pharma group (p>0.05). 
The interaction terms for Award & Data (p<0.05) and Seeker Known & Bio (p<0.001) had 
positive coefficients. The interaction terms for Award & Social (p<0.05), Seeker Known & 
Data (p<0.001), and Seeker Known & Engineering (p<0.05) had negative coefficients. The 
adjusted r-squared was around 0.25 for all models. Model C.6 explained 25.1% of the variation 
in the number of solvers if one controls for the number of variables used. The F statistic showed 
a highly significant difference between all models to an empty model. The coefficients of 
models C1-6 showed no significant difference from models B1-6. 
Key points: 
1. All models had high statistical validity.  
2. The models predicted up to 30.4% of the variance in the number of solvers. 
3. The values for the content characteristics were very robust throughout all models. 
4. The coefficients of most variables were highly statistically significant (p<0.001). 
5. Only the variables Engineering & Design, Elanco, and Pharma lacked (high) statistical 
validity. 
 
The results of the HYVECrowd analysis on the number of contributions (Table 5.4.4, M1 & 
M3) showed positive coefficients for the Number of Solvers (p<0.001), the Time (p<0.001), 
the Award Type (p<0.1), the Subtitle Length (p<0.05), the Engineering group (p<0.001), and 
the Aerospace (p<0.05) and Mechanical & Plant Engineering (p<0.01) groups. They showed 
negative coefficients for the Logistics (p<0.05) and Entertainment groups (p<0.05), the 
Financial Services (p<0.05), and Entertainment (p<0.05) application fields. The results of the 
analysis on the Number of Solvers (Table 5.4.4, M2 & M4) showed similar results for the Time 
(p<0.05), the Award Type (p<0.05), and the Logistics (p<0.1) and Entertainment (p<0.1) 
groups than the results on the Number of Contributions. The models added the positive Number 
of Views (p<0.01), Consumption (p<0.05), and Social (p<0.1) groups and the Tourism (p<0.05) 
and Automobile (p<0.1) application fields. I did not include the Engineering group, the 
Financial Services field, and the Aerospace field anymore, as they lacked explanatory power. 
The Mechanical & Plant Engineering application fields changed from a significantly positive 
coefficient to a significantly negative one.

5.5 Results – Evaluation 
The signs of the coefficients for the variables were robust throughout the models. In model A.4 
(Table 5.5.1, fourth row), the Winning coefficient was positive and significant (0.057, p<0.05). 
The Winning Chance coefficient was positive and highly significant (17.129, p<0.001). The 
Weighted Evaluation coefficient was positive and significant (0.041, p<0.01). The Likes 
coefficient was positive and highly significant (0.320, p<0.001). The Comments coefficient was 
positive and highly significant (0.070, p<0.001). The Interaction Evaluation & Likes coefficient 
was negative and significant (-0.003, p<0.05). The Average Likes coefficient was negative and 
highly significant (-0.465, p<0.001). The Maximal Likes coefficient was negative but not 
significant (-0.024, p>0.05). The AIC decreased substantially from model A.1 (AIC: 1735) to 
model A.4 (AIC: 1599). Especially the introduction of likes and comments (model A.3) led to 
a much better model. The increasing Log Likelihood values also indicated the increase in the 
model fit. 
The mean y-value for all contributions in contest 197 was -4.55 (median -4.65) for model A.4.  
The winning probability was above the mean and the median for 90% of the winning 
contributions (Table 5.5.2). Only contribution 10688 had a below-average winning probability 
and was even in the 0-25% quantile. Further, the winning probability was in the 75-100% 
quantile (y=-4.298) for 60% of the winning ones. Contribution 10360 had the highest winning 
probability and achieved eighth place.

Key points: 
1. The coefficients of most variables were highly statistically significant (p<0.01). 
2. The number of likes and comments had the biggest influence on the model quality. 
3. The values for the independent variables were very robust and had positive 
coefficients throughout all models. 
4. The values for the contest dependent variables were very robust and had negative 
coefficient throughout all models. 
5. 90% of the winning contributions had always a Y-value above the sample`s median. 
6. 90%  of the winning contributions had mostly a Y-value above the sample`s mean 
(approximately 60% quantile). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:91==
82 
 
6 Discussion & Limitations 
6.1 Discussion – Data Acquisition   
I labeled the application fields as “NA” if the platform indicated no application field. The 
procedure decreased the data set by 448 contests. However, all contests without application 
fields were performed before the platform introduced the application fields. Thus, it could be 
assumed that they were missing. The inclusion of the contests and labeling of all application 
fields with 0 would have biased the application field variables. I manually identified numerous 
missing application field values, created another data set, and tested the models on it, see above. 
The results were robust to that change.  
I disregarded the award modifications (e.g., "up to" and "in total"). It is evident that these 
modifications could have changed the actual award drastically. Thus, they probably also 
influenced the results of this study. However, due to the following circumstances, it was far 
more practical to define a less differentiated award size variable. First, the platform did not 
clearly state the modifying factors for all contests. Second, the numerous modifying factors 
would have led to a complex variable definition. Third, it can be assumed that the stated award 
size and the moderated award size are highly correlated. Finally, the total award size might 
attract the solvers, and the moderated award size might matter less. 
I dropped 90 contests because I could not extract their number of solvers. Further, I disregarded 
109 contests because I could not extract their award size. I regarded this data cleaning as 
reasonable as it was a comparably insignificant loss of data. Further, it was not evident that any 
confounder exists that lead to an influence on the number of solvers and is related to the 
existence of the number of solvers or the existence of the award size. Thus, this data cleaning 
should not have biased the sample. 
I substituted the precise time variable with the contest index as a surrogate variable to describe 
the time. Evidently, the index was less accurate than the actual time, but the precise time was 
missing for 1082 contests. Using the actual time variable would have caused a drastic data loss. 
Thus, using it was not an option. The index as a surrogate variable underestimates larger time 
differences between consecutive contests and overestimates smaller ones. This might have been 
more crucial for larger time differences, as small differences might have been more critical for 
the solvers because they might have had to split their efforts on multiple contests. Either way, 
the index was a practical proxy as it could clearly show a trend over time. Further, Google data 
clearly showing a negative trend in interest in the platform supported the results (Google, 
2023b). 
==PAGE:92==
83 
 
Similar to the scraping of Innocentive, I disregarded the award modifications (e.g., "up to" and 
"in total") in the HYVECrowd data. It is evident that these modifications could have changed 
the actual award drastically. Thus, they probably also influenced the results of this study. 
However, due to similar circumstances to those for the Innocentive data, it was far more 
practical to define a less differentiated award size variable. The most significant difference to 
the Innocentive data set was that I disregarded differences between currencies. I assume no 
resulting bias as the US Dollar and the Euro value are comparably close in value, and the 
differences between the prices were way more crucial than the effects of currencies.   
The HYVECrowd responsible supported my decision to remove the contests with a low number 
of contributions (I1). They were contests that awarded funding to social organizations. Thus, 
they were inherently different from the innovation contests with contributing individuals that I 
investigated in this study.  
6.2 Discussion – Application Fields and Motivation  
6.2.1 Discussion - Task Characteristics and Motivation  
The results of the variables used to describe the variation in the number of solvers mostly 
supported the above-presented theory. However, as described above, some variables could be 
surrogates for different influences, which makes a thorough discussion necessary.  
The logarithmic value of the award size is positively related to the number of solvers and 
describes the relationship between the award size and the number of solvers best. All models 
expect an increase in the award size to attract more solvers, ceteris paribus. In Model B2 (Table 
5.4.2), an increase in the logarithmic award size by 1 unit is expected to attract eighty additional 
solvers if the contest is not a data contest and an additional 41 if it is, ceteris paribus. I obtained 
a similar result for the award size and the mathematics category in model A2 (Table 5.4.1). In 
Model B3 (Table 5.4.2), an increase in the logarithmic award size by 1 unit is expected to attract 
108 additional solvers if the contest is not a social contest but 81 less if it is, ceteris paribus. 
Thus, an increase in the award size is expected to attract more solvers in data-related and fewer 
in social-related contests than in others. I will discuss the moderating influences on the 
relationship between the award size and the number of solvers later. The award size and the 
time are two continuous variables used in an interaction. Thus, the coefficient of the award size 
in model A3 (Table 5.4.1) is not directly interpretable. However, an increase in time by one 
unit is expected to decrease the number of attracted solvers by the logarithm of the award size 
by 0.05, ceteris paribus. Thus, an increase in the award size is expected to attract fewer solvers 
==PAGE:93==
84 
 
over time. Figure 5.1.20 shows how the relationship between the award size and the number of 
solvers changed over time.  
Higher awards seem to be preferred by potential solvers. However, the relative value of an 
increase in the award size decreases with its size. An increase in the award size is less influential 
for higher awards than for lower ones. OeNPAY added that they chose one high award to 
increase the quality of the solutions and achieved that goal, also in comparison to other contests 
with multiple awards (I2). They assessed this goal as achieved because they attracted three good 
contributions and continued to move forward with at least one of them. Thus, they also assumed 
that they chose a sufficient award size. 
The positive relation supports the hypothesis that an increase in monetary incentives increases 
the motivation to participate. The logarithmic relationship also shows a decrease in importance 
when the award reaches bigger sizes. It is in line with two arguments. First, most solvers that 
contribute only to receive the money have a threshold award size at which they contribute. This 
threshold seems to be relatively low for most of them. Second, solvers that contribute to receive 
appreciation perceive the award size as a means of appreciation. The threshold award size, 
which they perceive as sufficient, seems also comparably low. I argue that OeNPAY might 
have made use of this phenomenon. They could have set a higher award, but they already 
attracted more solvers than expected with a lower award (I2). An award increase would have 
probably attracted relatively fewer solvers. Thus, they could reduce costs while attracting many 
solvers.   
The logarithmic relation is also somewhat in line with the argumentation for the award as a 
surrogate for complexity. Solvers motivated by the task`s complexity are more likely to 
participate in the high award, therefore, high complexity tasks. The increase in motivation due 
to increasing task complexity decreases over time, as the solvers might become underqualified 
and overwhelmed by very complex tasks. For solvers that seek monetary gains or appreciation 
through the contests, the complexity has the opposite effect. They assess the award size relative 
to the complexity. More complex tasks should have higher awards. It might be that the task 
complexity increases faster than the award size, and thus, the relative value of the award 
decreases with higher award sizes. These groups would be affected most by this.  
Some of the generally discarded hypotheses on this relationship below might have been true at 
a specific platform life-cycle stage. However, the relationship between the award size and the 
number of solvers does not seem to be linear. I could not find support for a linear relationship 
==PAGE:94==
85 
 
between monetary gain and motivation to contribute. This relationship might also not occur 
because the monetary gain and the task complexity are presumed to increase at different rates.  
The relationship between the award size and the number of solvers does not follow an inverted 
U-shape. I could not find support for the argument for a decrease in the number of solvers for 
comparably high awards. Solvers do not seem to be less attracted to highly complex tasks. They 
might overestimate their capabilities and be more attracted by the high awards than repelled by 
the high complexity.  
To summarize, an increase in the award size increases the number of solvers. However, the 
relative increase decreases with the award size. Important arguments to describe this 
phenomenon might be the influence of task complexity on the number of contributors and the 
fact that most potential solvers are already motivated by comparably smaller award sizes.  
Finally, the increased importance of the award size in data tasks might be due to the possibly 
higher complexity of these tasks. As described above, seekers should provide higher awards for 
higher complexity tasks. Otherwise, the motivation to participate decreases. Monetarily 
motivated solvers would have a worse benefit-cost ratio, and reputationally motivated solvers 
would perceive less fairness and appreciation. One might also assume that these tasks evoke 
less internal motivation than others. The decreased importance of the award size in social tasks 
strengthens the internal motivation argument. Social tasks provide a higher purpose; the award 
size matters less.  
The text length is negatively related to the number of solvers. If the level of elaboration and 
specificity of the task's description increases, the number of solvers seems to decrease. It 
supports two arguments. First, the specificity might be a result of the task`s complexity. The 
more complex the task, the more specific the task description must be. But the more complex 
the task, the fewer solvers are confident to address it appropriately. Thus, the longer the 
description, the fewer solvers are attracted. Second, the specificity might be overwhelming and 
decrease the creative flexibility of the solvers. The potential solvers look at the description. If 
it contains too many instructions, it might provide too much information to process and, thus, 
requires too much initial investment. Further, tasks with less flexibility might be less fun for 
the solvers, and those who are motivated internally would be less attracted. OeNPAY agreed 
and chose a compelling approach to potentially circumvent the problem by splitting their text 
into small sections and assigning them to different headlines and pages (I2). The HYVECrowd 
responsible found the influence of the text length compelling but interposed that solvers in 
contests with long texts might be more committed (I1). UNIQA also did not consider the text 
==PAGE:95==
86 
 
length and provided much information for their task to increase involvement and contribution 
quality (I3). While this theory is intuitive, convincing, and a good example of the trade-off 
between the quantity and quality of crowdsourcing contributions, I could not find evidence to 
support it. Further, it enhances the goal conflict between maximizing the number of solvers and 
maximizing the number of contributions, which I discuss below. I could not find support for 
the argument that a very short overview might result in a lower number of solvers due to 
occurring uncertainties. Uncertainty might not be an influencing factor; it could even be 
motivating as it provides the flexibility to be creative. Thus, I could not find the predicted 
inverted U-shape relation, but the task`s specificity has a negative, linear relationship with the 
number of solvers.  
The time is negatively related to the number of solvers. Recent contests seemed to attract fewer 
solvers than earlier contests. The relative decrease in the number of solvers shrinks over time. 
The time variable was initially only included as a control variable for the platform`s growth and 
did not have further theoretical relevance. Now I have to discuss the surprising finding that the 
number of solvers did not increase over time but decreased. I predicted that the number of 
solvers increases over time as the platform grows and attracts more participants. The opposite 
might be because few solvers usually win contests (Archak and Sundararajan, 2009; Yang et 
al., 2008). Other solvers might have stopped contributing because solvers from this small group 
always won, and their winning chances seemed comparably low. This observation could also 
be due to more contests that the platform published in parallel. The platform hosted multiple 
contests in parallel during the time of this investigation. This was most likely not the case in 
the platform`s early stages. Users might only focus on one competition at a time. Therefore, the 
number of solvers per contest might decrease, while the number of solvers on the platform does 
not. Google data shows arguments against this reasoning, as the general interest in the platform 
decreased over time (Google, 2023b). Further, the task complexity might have increased over 
time. Thus, fewer solvers feel confident to contribute. It might also be difficult for platforms to 
attract new users as they might perceive their chance of winning as very low because many 
established solvers are already participating. The HYVECrowd responsible strongly supported 
the last argument and added that the crowdsourcing hype diminished a bit in general (I1). He 
also described a consolidation of the crowd, which might have left mainly internally motivated 
solvers (I1). This could also explain the negatively moderating effect of time on the relationship 
between the award size and the number of solvers. 
==PAGE:96==
87 
 
An association with the ideation phase is positively related to the number of solvers. Ideation 
contests seem to attract more solvers than other contests. Ideation contests have lower 
contribution costs, more flexibility, and room for creativity for the solvers, and the perceived 
complexity might be lower. Thus, the barriers to entering an ideation contest are lower than to 
entering other contests. The HYVECrowd responsible agreed specifically with the lower 
barriers, as anyone can contribute early (I1). UNIQA`s OIL added that crowdsourcing tasks 
should be superficial (I3). It is also in line with the focus of OeNPAY on high accessibility to 
attract ideas from many different fields independent of the solver`s background (I2). The results 
support this assumption. Ideation contests constituted more than 50% of the contests. Other 
innovation phases only constituted between 10% and 20%. Thus, I argue that seekers have 
realized the potential of crowdsourcing in ideation compared to other innovation phases and act 
accordingly.  
Key points:  
1. The relative increase in award size might be a crucial motivating factor. 
2. The award might be less/more crucial for internally/externally motivated fields.  
3. Highly specific and elaborated tasks might attract fewer solvers. 
4. Platforms might go through an evolution that influences the number of solvers. 
5. Ideation tasks might attract more solvers as they are flexible and accessible.  
6.2.2 Discussion – Application fields  
The results indicate relationships between multiple application fields, showcases, and the 
number of solvers. The application fields were Data, Engineering, and Bio. The showcases were 
Pharma, Aerospace, and Social.  
The data fields are positively related to the number of solvers. In Model B4 (Table 5.4.2) the 
data tasks are expected to attract 153 solvers more than other tasks if the seekers do not disclose 
their identity, ceteris paribus. However, they are expected to attract only thirty-eight more 
solvers if the seekers disclose their identity, ceteris paribus. I obtained similar results for 
mathematic and business tasks in models A4 and A5 (Table 5.4.1). These industries appear to 
attract more solvers when they engage in crowdsourcing than the average of all other industries. 
This relationship might occur because tasks in these fields might have low investment costs, as 
they are mostly theoretical. Further, it supports the argument that broader fields are more suited 
for crowdsourcing. Both the mathematical and the business fields have many intersections with 
other fields because mathematics and economics are essential for natural and social sciences 
==PAGE:97==
88 
 
respectively. Thus, many people may have basic knowledge in these fields and might be suited 
and confident to enter such contests. Solvers of these tasks seem to be mainly monetarily 
motivated, as the moderating effect of data industries on the relationship between the award 
size and the number of solvers is highly positive. This is especially true for high award sizes, 
as shown in Figure 5.1.19. Thus, I assume that easy accessibility is more important for many 
potential solvers than internal motivation in data contests. Similar phenomena could be 
observed in the OeNPAY contest. In this contest all application fields were addressed and, thus, 
many solvers were attracted (I2). The data categories constitute only around 15% of the contests 
even though their coefficient suggests that these tasks have the strongest positive relationship 
on the number of solvers out of all categories. Thus, one might assume that seekers do not 
realize the potential of crowdsourcing in these fields. However, it is also likely that the demand 
for external solutions is lower in these fields than in other fields. One reason might be that 
seekers from data fields might possess heterogeneous groups of employees who own the basic 
knowledge required for such tasks. Thus, the demand for diversity from external sources is not 
crucial.  
The engineering fields are positively related to the number of solvers. However, compared to 
the other application field groups, engineering had the lowest coefficient size and was also less 
significant than the other two. In Model B5 (Table 5.4.2) the engineering tasks are expected to 
attract fifty-eight solvers more than tasks that are not engineering-related if the seeker is secret, 
ceteris paribus. However, they are expected to attract fifteen solvers fewer than other tasks if it 
is not secret, ceteris paribus. These industries appear to attract more solvers when they engage 
in crowdsourcing than the average of all other industries if the seeker is kept secret and fewer 
if it is not. The positive effect might occur because engineering tasks could offer a lot of 
flexibility, complexity, and room for creativity, which addresses the internal motivation of 
solvers. I will discuss the moderating effect of identity disclosure on the relationship between 
engineering fields and the number of solvers below. The engineering fields constitute over 60% 
of the tasks. Thus, I assume that the demand for external solutions in these fields is high and 
that seekers realize the potential of crowdsourcing in them.  
The biology fields are negatively related to the number of solvers. In model B6 (Table 5.4.2) 
the bio tasks are expected to attract 107 solvers fewer than unrelated tasks if the seeker is secret, 
ceteris paribus. They are expected to attract only three solvers fewer than other tasks if the 
seeker is not secret, ceteris paribus. I obtained similar results for the chemistry tasks in model 
A6 (Table 5.4.1). These fields seem to attract fewer solvers when they secretly engage in 
==PAGE:98==
89 
 
crowdsourcing than the average of all other fields. This result further supported the argument 
that more specified fields and highly-specific tasks might be less suited for crowdsourcing. This 
argument was highly supported by all interviews. They highlighted that crowdsourcing tasks 
should be superficial, accessible, and should address many fields (I1, I2, I3). A frequently set 
task in these fields was developing a new production method for a material. One might argue 
that such tasks are less flexible and attractive than others (e.g., engineering ones). Further, 
highly-specific knowledge of the composition and characteristics of materials and chemicals 
and their production methods is a prerequisite to contribute to such contests. One can assume 
that such knowledge is more specific and fewer people have such knowledge compared to the 
knowledge required for the categories presented above. The HYVECrowd responsible 
strengthened that argument by adding that their crowd would not be suited to solve such tasks 
(I1). The bio fields, especially chemistry, constituted over 50% of the contests. Thus, one can 
argue that a mismatch exists between the demand and supply for external solutions in these 
fields. Further, seekers in these fields might not realize that their tasks might not be suitable for 
crowdsourcing. However, as noted above, engagement in crowdsourcing can have multiple 
reasons beyond solution finding. All of these are dependent on identity disclosure. However, 
the biofields have by far the lowest percentage of disclosed identities. Thus, motivations like 
reputational gains or community building do not explain the occurrence of many contests in 
these fields. 
The showcase variables controlled for an even stronger identification with a specific brand or 
crowdsourcing aim. If a showcase variable has a significant coefficient, it should show that 
substantially more (or fewer) solvers shared the showcase aims or were highly motivated by 
their brands. They most likely signed up for them, which is a strong commitment to a case and 
its tasks.  
The pharma showcases are negatively related to the number of solvers. Contests in them are 
expected to attract 139 fewer solvers (Model B6, Table 5.4.2) than contests in other showcases, 
ceteris paribus. These seekers seem to attract fewer solvers when they engage in crowdsourcing 
than the average of all others. However, the relationship was not statistically significant. Thus, 
the results provide only a very weak, if any, indication. The showcase group included Elanco 
and Novartis but not Astra Zeneca, as it tended to have a positive relationship with the number 
of solvers. This finding might support the argument of negative reputational effects that might 
occur in the pharmaceutical field. For Elanco, the argument is straightforward as it is a 
comparably small company and has a low environment, social, and governance (ESG) rating 
==PAGE:99==
90 
 
of 35 (CSR Hub, 2023a). However, this is not true for Novartis. Novartis has a CSR of 95 (CSR 
Hub, 2023b), and AstraZeneca has a CSR of 94 (CSR Hub, 2023c). They are also comparable 
in size and are both from Europe. Thus, it is puzzling why there is such a difference in the 
relationship between the number of solvers and Novartis and AstraZeneca, respectively. To 
summarize, the results cannot sufficiently support the hypothesis of reputational effects in the 
pharma industry.  
The Aerospace showcases are positively related to the number of solvers. Contests in them are 
expected to attract 110 more solvers (Model B6, Table 5.4.2) than in other showcases, ceteris 
paribus. I assume that aerospace topics have many highly enthusiastic followers, as they are 
compelling and fascinating for many. Further, solving tasks for the Air Force or NASA should 
also lead to the reputational effects that some solvers try to achieve. These institutions also have 
many followers that strongly identify with their brands. Thus, aerospace tasks offer strong 
internal motivation due to their compelling nature, brand identification, and strong external 
motivation due to the high-status brands behind them.  
The Social showcases are positively related to the number of solvers. Contests in them are 
expected to attract 131 more solvers (Model B6, Table 5.4.2) than in other showcases, ceteris 
paribus. It supports the argument that purposeful tasks attract more solvers (Cappa et al., 2019). 
The CEO of OeNPAY also underlined the importance of values for seekers that aim to include 
everyone (I2). This is strengthened by the strong, negatively moderating effect of social 
showcases on the relationship between the award size and the number of solvers. Figure 5.1.22 
shows that this is especially true for higher awards. Solvers contributing to socially motivated 
tasks seem dominantly internally motivated, and external motivation decreases substantially in 
value. Further, even though H2 (Table 5.4.4) showed only weak support for a positive effect of 
a social goal on HYVE, HYVE assumes that it exists (I1). 
Key points:  
1. Data tasks might attract more solvers as they are easily accessible. 
2. Engineering tasks might attract more solvers as they are compelling and flexible. 
3. Bio tasks might attract fewer solvers as they are hardly accessible and unilateral. 
4. Aerospace tasks might attract more solvers because of strong brands and fascination. 
5. Social tasks might attract more solvers because they are purposeful.  
 
 
==PAGE:100==
91 
 
6.2.3 Discussion – Seeker`s identity  
The disclosure of the seeker`s identity is positively related to the number of solvers. The number 
of solvers is expected to increase if the seeker discloses their identity. This is in line with prior 
research by Pollock et al. (2019). They found that identity disclosure is positively related to the 
number of contributions. It supports the following arguments. First, the solver`s brand identity 
seems to be a crucial facilitator of crowdsourcing. Solvers are more likely to contribute if they 
have a connection to the seeker's brand, which was also highlighted by UNIQA`s OIL (I3). 
Second, an attractive brand might be a critical facilitator of crowdsourcing. Solvers that 
contribute to increase their reputation can only do that through an attractive brand and if the 
seeker discloses it. HYVE supported the importance of brand and reputation (I1). They held 
only a few secret contests and would always encourage their seekers to disclose their identity, 
as brands can be important drivers to enhance contribution (I1). Solvers approach them 
regularly to get certificates to showcase their successful participation to third parties (I1). Third, 
crowdsourcing is a form of open innovation, and disclosing important information is a 
substantial part of open innovation, which was also highlighted by UNIQA`s OIL (I3). Solvers 
contributing in the spirit of open innovation contests might be less attracted to tasks with secret 
seekers than to the ones with a disclosed seeker identity. Fourth, the secrecy might reduce the 
solver`s ability to solve the task, as they cannot put them in the context of the seeker's projects 
and environments, which might also decrease their motivation to participate. Finally, solvers 
might assume that seekers that keep their identity secret might do so because they have a bad 
reputation or bad intentions, even if they have not.  
The coefficients for Identity Disclosure fluctuated in significance. Thus, I could not find 
significant support for the isolated importance of identity disclosure throughout all models. 
However, identity disclosure is also a highly significant moderating factor for the application 
fields. I will discuss this moderating influence in the following.  
The results of the descriptive analysis of the disclosed identity contests suggested that especially 
foundations might reap the benefit of identity disclosure and attract many solvers. This supports 
prior research that suggests that a meaningful task is a source of internal motivation (Cappa et 
al., 2019). I assume that foundations set tasks that serve a higher purpose and are more 
meaningful compared to tasks from commercial companies. Further, if the posted task is not 
inherently meaningful, disclosing that the seeker is a foundation might provide meaningfulness. 
The opposite effect would explain the negative relationship between commercial seekers and 
the number of solvers, as they do not provide this meaningfulness. The results also suggest that 
==PAGE:101==
92 
 
this negative relationship loses significance with increasing company size. The moderating 
factor of company size on the negative influence of commercialization can be explained by 
reputation-seeking behavior and brand identity. The company size is likely an important 
influence factor on the reputation that solvers could build. Further, bigger seekers have probably 
reached many loyal customers that might turn to crowdsourcing as described above. Similar 
findings were made by Pollock et al. (2019). They found that high-status companies profit more 
from identity disclosure. While high-status seekers might profit the most from identity 
disclosure, HYVE experienced that they have the biggest secrecy concerns (I1). Thus, a bigger 
trade-off occurs for large companies. Finally, seekers can only reach secondary goals (e.g., 
marketing, branding, and positioning) by disclosing their identity. OeNPAY did not even 
consider doing an anonymous contest as they could benefit from marketing, external reach, 
signaling, and positioning (I2). They focused approximately 40% of their effort on such 
secondary goals and did not have any reason to stay closed (I2). This worked well for them, as 
companies approached them after noticing them through the contest. Similar holds for UNIQA 
(I3). The OIL assessed the secondary goals as equally important (I3). Their secondary goals 
were mainly marketing and branding by creating touchpoints with students and research by 
discovering the underlying needs behind the contributions (I3). While the findings on open 
seekers have a strong theoretical foundation and were clearly shown with descriptive tools, I 
could not replicate them with more advanced statistical methods (e.g., regression analysis). The 
reasons might be that the data set of disclosed contests was too small, and I might have defined 
the variables inconsistently. Thus, this offers a path for future research.  
Model B4 (Table 5.4.2) shows that identity disclosure negatively moderates the relationship 
between the data tasks and the number of solvers. The findings from the research on the 
disclosed identity tasks showed that medium-sized commercial companies attract fewer solvers 
than other seekers. The data contests had significantly fewer medium-sized commercial 
company seekers than others. However, the moderating influence is highly negative even 
though the organization types would suggest the opposite. Further, the research on data fields 
mentioned above showed a high focus on external motivation suggesting low importance of the 
seeker`s identity unless the seeker cannot provide reputational gains. Reputation might partially 
explain the effect, as the data companies were tendentially smaller. Further, the HYVECrowd 
responsible suggested that it might not matter for whom solvers solve mathematical problems 
(I1). However, he could not explain the moderating effect on business & entrepreneurship (I1).  
==PAGE:102==
93 
 
Model B5 (Table 5.4.2) shows that identity disclosure negatively moderates the relationship 
between the engineering tasks and the number of solvers. While engineering tasks in secret 
identity contests have a positive relationship with the number of solvers, they have a negative 
one in the disclosed identity contests. The findings from the research on the disclosed identity 
tasks could not indicate any significant difference in organization types between the engineering 
tasks and others. Thus, this effect cannot be explained by such a difference. I argue that identity 
disclosure might often be associated with decreasing flexibility and task focus as seekers 
provide more context. It could help explain the finding, as the primarily internally motivated 
solvers would lose a substantial part of their motivation.  
Model B6 (Table 5.4.2) shows that identity disclosure negatively moderates the relationship 
between the bio tasks and the number of solvers. The findings from the research on the disclosed 
identity tasks showed that commercial companies attract fewer and foundations attract more 
solvers than other seekers. The data contests included significantly more commercial companies 
and fewer foundations than others. Thus, one expects a negative moderating effect based on the 
organization types. However, the findings suggest the opposite. I assume that reputational 
motivation can partially explain it. As discussed above, bio contests seem to evoke less internal 
motivation than others. Bio solvers also seem less motivated by monetary awards than data 
solvers. Hence, reputational gains and the possibility of employment are left over as the main 
motivating factors. Foundations might provide less of both than most commercial companies. 
Large commercial companies might provide both. The disclosed identity bio contests are 
tendentially held by larger commercial companies. Thus, these seekers might evoke more 
motivation in the solvers by providing reputational gains. 
The manually extended data set results (Table 5.4.3) show mostly the same signs and 
significance levels as the results discussed above. Higher statistical significance strengthened 
the findings for Engineering and Aerospace. Seeker Known is statistically significant for all 
relevant models (C1-5, Table 5.4.3), which was not the case before, and supports a positive 
relationship between identity disclosure and the number of solvers. Overall, the manually 
extended data set supports the findings from the first data set and shows their robustness. 
Similar holds for the small data set, which supports robust findings but yields less statistical 
significance due to the substantially smaller data set.   
Key points: 
1. Disclosing large/small companies might attract more/fewer solvers in fields with 
mainly reputationally motivated solvers. 
==PAGE:103==
94 
 
2. Disclosing foundations might attract fewer solvers in externally motivated fields.  
3. Disclosing foundations might attract more solvers in purpose-based internally 
motivated fields. 
4. Disclosing the seeker might attract fewer solvers in task-based internally motivated 
fields. 
5. Secondary goals might be very important and identity disclosure is indispensable to 
reaching secondary goals. 
6.2.4 Discussion – HYVECrowd contests 
The HYVECrowd data set was comparably small. Thus, one should be cautious about 
generalizing the findings. However, they support multiple assumptions and findings from the 
Innocentive data, yield some new findings and provide interesting paths into future research. 
Therefore, I will shortly discuss the most compelling findings. 
I assumed that the time is positively related to the number of contributions and solvers. I found 
highly significant support for both. The platform’s reach seems to have increased over time. 
Thus, HYVECrowd was probably not yet affected by the intimidating dominance of a few very 
experienced solvers. 
I expected that monetary awards attract more contributions and solvers than non-monetary 
awards. The analyzed data offers weak support for this assumption. The mean number of 
contributions to contests with a monetary award is significantly higher than to contests with 
non-monetary awards. Some models also showed a weakly significant, positive relation 
between a monetary award and the number of solvers (e.g., H4, Table 5.4.4). There could be 
multiple reasons why solvers might prefer monetary awards. The values of non-monetary 
awards differ more between the solvers than the values of monetary awards. Further, monetary 
awards might be more precious, or non-monetary rewards are connected to high transaction 
costs to generate monetary value. HYVE added that they often have solvers from around the 
world that cannot profit from specific awards, for example, an invitation to the seeker`s head 
office in Germany (I1). Further, the HYVECrowd responsible assumed that seekers use non-
monetary awards mainly to reduce costs (I1). Hence, for many solvers, a lower monetary award 
is more useful than a higher non-monetary. Thus, awards should, if possible, be monetary. He 
supported that statement and added that only a few exceptions exist (e.g., the sponsoring set-up 
for universities) (I1). However, UNIQA`s OIL opposed that brand-related awards increase the 
motivation of lead users, which should increase the quality of the contributions (I3). I argue that 
==PAGE:104==
95 
 
most lead users are already motivated by the task and the recognition by the seeker. Thus, the 
loss in quantity might not be justified by the slightly better targeting of lead users.  
I assumed that the text lengths are negatively related to the number of contributions. The 
analyzed data cannot support that assumption and contradicting indicates a positive relation. 
The subtitle length has a positive, significant relationship with the number of contributions. The 
other lengths showed no significant relations. Assuming that they are proxies for complexity, 
but can also be proxies for the comprehensiveness of the contest description, the influence of 
complexity seems irrelevant, or that of comprehensiveness might be stronger. I should note that 
while the lengths show, at least, to some degree, the expected relations to the number of 
contributions, it does not for the number of solvers. Participants that already decided to 
participate seem to have overcome the complexity barrier and contributed even more. The level 
of complexity might motivate them or leave more room for different solutions. Further, the 
subtitle length might indicate the possibility for solvers to quickly receive information about a 
contest. A longer subtitle might present the task more clearer and help potential seekers decide 
if they want to contribute. Thus, a longer subtitle might also attract more solvers. However, as 
the relationship between the subtitle length and the number of contributions is positive and 
significant and the relationship between the subtitle length and the number of solvers is not, it 
does not explain the findings. Thus, they remain puzzling.  
As expected, some application fields are predicted to attract more solvers and contributions than 
others. The results showed weak support for the assumption that tasks from the logistics fields 
attract fewer solvers and contributions. They showed similar results for the relationship between 
the entertainment fields and the number of solvers and contributions. 
The most compelling result is that engineering tasks might attract fewer solvers but more 
contributions. Models H1 and H3 (Table 5.4.4) support the findings from above that 
engineering and aerospace tasks are more compelling to solvers. However, engineering tasks 
are unrelated to the number of solvers in Model H2 and negatively related to them in Model H4 
(Table 5.4.4). I assume this might be due to accessibility and the differences between the 
platforms. First, engineering tasks might require specific knowledge that only a few solvers can 
acquire in the contest period. Thus, fewer solvers participate in engineering contests. However, 
solvers might be highly internally motivated and contribute even more after crossing that 
barrier. Second, the Innocentive crowd might consist mainly of highly-capable solvers, as self-
selection excluded the others over time. HYVE agreed that the Innocentive crowd is better 
equipped to solve engineering tasks (I1). Thus, the barrier to participation is less relevant for 
==PAGE:105==
96 
 
the Innocentive than for the HYVE crowd, which might not have gone through this self-
selection process yet. Overall, this relation increases the severity of many contributions by only 
a few solvers, which HYVE confirmed (I1).  
Further, the results showed weak support for positive relationships for social and consumption 
tasks and the number of solvers but not the number of contributions. The differences between 
the coefficients for the number of contributions and the number of solvers have high theoretical 
values. It indicates that the number of solvers and contributions are not directly interchangeable 
as independent variables in crowdsourcing research. But it might also have high practical 
implications for the seekers, which I discuss in the following.  
Seekers can choose between maximizing the number of solvers and contributions. Both are 
legitimate (sub-)goals in crowdsourcing. Seekers might prefer a high number of contributions 
if the pure quantity of ideas is crucial, which would follow the concrete wisdom of the crowd 
approach. However, as described above, the wisdom of the crowd theory (Surowiecki, 2005) is 
often not directly applicable to the reality of crowdsourcing for innovation. The value of 
crowdsourcing for innovation mostly comes from the quality of a few contributions, not the 
quantity of all contributions (Archak and Sundararajan, 2009). Thus, attracting more solvers 
might be better to receive a small number of very good solutions. HYVE tried limiting the 
number of contributions per solver by setting a maximum number (I1). Their reasons were that 
the solvers should spend more time on the contributions, occupy fewer solution fields, and that 
potential solvers would be less intimidated by the number of contributions (I1). Thus, they 
would also rather maximize the number of solvers than the number of contributions (I1). 
However, implementing a hard limit led to resistance from the highly active solvers (I1). 
Further, the number of contributions is often used to prove the crowdsourcing success to the 
seekers (I1). OeNPAY used the award structure to promote fewer but better ideas and 
experienced that contributions by solvers that contribute more tend to be worse than others (I2), 
which contradicts the intuition of UNIQA`s OIL, which assumed that such solvers might be 
more involved and improve their contributions over time (I3). OeNPAY used the number of 
contributions rather than the number of solvers as the main Key Performance Indicator (KPI) 
for the contest`s success (I2). They also mentioned the number of new solvers on the platform 
due to their contest as a KPI (I2). However, I argue that it is less precise than the number of 
solvers, as it does not measure the attraction from the existing crowd and, after all, the platform 
was used to reach that crowd. Finally, UNIQA`s OIL added that the number of solvers is also 
an important KPI for the secondary goals (I3). As secondary goals are also very important for 
==PAGE:106==
97 
 
many seekers, the number of solvers increases even more in value as a KPI for seekers. To 
summarize, the number of solvers seems to be a better KPI than the number of contributions to 
measure participation in crowdsourcing contests in line with the outcome goals.  
Key points: 
1. HYVECrowd might not yet be as affected by crowd consolidation as Innocentive. 
2. Monetary awards might attract more solvers than non-monetary awards. 
3. The number of solvers and the number of contributions are not perfect substitutes. 
4. The number of solvers might be a better KPI than the number of contributions. 
6.2.5 Relevance for Decision-Making  
As the primary aim of this study was to facilitate decision-making for crowdsourcing, I 
developed a framework (Figure 6.2.1) to visualize the potential implications of this study`s 
findings for decision-making and discuss them with the interviewees. The aim of the framework 
was to assist seekers in the decision to engage in crowdsourcing and researchers to extend 
existing crowdsourcing frameworks. The framework is not exhaustive. It is a possible extension 
of existing frameworks, has a narrow focus on this study`s findings, and was used to investigate 
the relevance of this study`s findings for real-world decision-making.  
The findings suggested that seekers might need to consider their industry and innovation phase 
while assessing the potential of crowdsourcing regarding their innovation efforts. Further, 
seekers might face several trade-offs while trying to attract high participation in their contests. 
Such trade-offs might occur for the identity disclosure, the award size, the attraction, and the 
task specificity. Figure 6.2.1 visualizes the potentially crucial influence factors of this study for 
decision-making. First, seekers might assess their current innovation phase. They can skip step 
two (Focus) and continue with step three (Application Field) if their task is located in the area 
of ideation or comparable early innovation phases, as such phases seem to be highly suitable 
for crowdsourcing. Second, seekers might assess if finding a solution is their primary aim or if 
they have a different reason to engage in crowdsourcing (e.g., brand, reputation, etc.). They can 
skip step three if finding a solution is not the primary aim, as the application field does not 
matter for secondary goals, and continue with the right side of step four (Identity Disclosure). 
Third, seekers might assess their task`s application field. They continue on the left side of the 
fourth step (Identity Disclosure) if it is in the Business, Mathematics, Engineering, or IT fields, 
as these fields are very suitable for crowdsourcing. Otherwise, they continue on the right or 
middle, as other fields are less suited. Fourth, seekers might assess if they want to disclose their 
==PAGE:107==
98 
 
identity or keep it secret. They can decide on it compared to the other influence factors which 
appear to be fixed. Finally, seekers can use the framework to approximate the potential to attract 
solvers for crowdsourcing contests based on the innovation phase, focus, application field, and 
identity disclosure. 
Figure 6.2.1. Decision-Making Framework. Considering the Innovation Phase, Focus, Field, and Disclosure. Yes: Green. No: Red. 
Yes, without an option: Black. Source: OR  
 
I presented the framework to the interviewees to investigate if seekers consider such steps and 
if frameworks as such could help during the decision-making. First, I discussed the framework 
and decision-making for crowdsourcing in general with the HYVECrowd responsible and 
uncovered the following (I1): In the past, seekers approached HYVE to plan and implement
