very suitable 
Suitability 
Crowdsourcing is 
likely suitable 
Variable Influence Factors 
==PAGE:108==
99 
 
crowdsourcing campaigns for them. These efforts were often isolated and lacked the processes 
that facilitate sustainable use of the contributions. Thus, HYVE started to redefine the role of 
crowdsourcing from an innovation strategy to an innovation tool. Sometimes seekers still 
approach HYVE and request crowdsourcing. He assumes that they are definitely also driven by 
the secondary effects that I mentioned before (e.g., marketing, branding, recruiting, and 
community exchange) (Comparable to Step 2). However, HYVE wants to focus on the 
problems and regards such efforts as partly pointless. Crowdsourcing has to be sufficiently 
supported by appropriate processes, otherwise, success becomes unlikely. If they are 
approached by seekers that search for many ideas from crowdsourcing, they do not refrain from 
the job, but they also point at different tools that might be more appropriate. The aim is to 
innovate successfully, even if this leads to the consequence that crowdsourcing is not used. 
HYVE does not use any frameworks to decide if crowdsourcing has potential. They base their 
decisions on good judgment and experience. Their main deciding factors are the technical 
characteristics. They consider crowds as well suited to finding use cases for existing 
technologies, which is comparable to an ideation stage (Step 1). Thus, they use crowdsourcing 
mostly as a tool to discover application fields for seekers that developed new technologies or 
search for new applications. However, they are also involved in the decision of identity 
disclosure (Step 4). Further, they can sometimes influence the application field (Step 3).  
Even though HYVE does not use frameworks, Figure 6.2.1, which is solely based on the 
quantitative investigation and not on the interviews, shows obvious parallels to their decision-
making. Step 1 supports the strong focus on the search for use cases, which is a subcategory of 
ideation. Step 2 includes the dilemma of secondary vs. primary crowdsourcing goals, which 
HYVE actually faces and which forced them to decide on one (namely primary goals) in their 
company strategy. Seekers still approach HYVE to do crowdsourcing to reap primary and 
secondary benefits.  Thus, Step 2 is still relevant for HYVE. Step 3 is surprisingly sometimes 
influenced by HYVE and not as fixed as expected. Thus, it is relevant for HYVE, but rather a 
hybrid between fixed and variable. Finally, Step 4 is definitely a decision that HYVE faces and 
influences. HYVE is always encouraging disclosure, however, this investigation`s results 
suggest that they should be interested in a more differentiated perspective on identity disclosure.  
Second, I discussed the frameworks and decision-making for crowdsourcing in general with 
OeNPAY and uncovered the following (I2): OeNPAY`s decision was very much influenced by 
the personal preferences of the interviewed CEO, which is an example of the influence of 
company culture on the crowdsourcing decision (see Fayard et al., 2016). However, he also 
==PAGE:109==
100 
 
highlighted the importance of accessibility. Thus, they considered to some degree the 
innovation phase (Step 1). They also clearly considered Step 2 in their process. Their first goal 
was to find use cases for their technology (60% focus). Their second goals were to create 
attention, external reach, branding, signaling, and positioning (40% focus). They considered 
secondary goals as very important, which was also the main reason to stay open (step 4). As 
their contest was independent of a specific application field, Step 3 was not relevant to their 
process. The SIM was not an employee of the company at the decision point, but she strongly 
agreed that such frameworks could help them in decision-making because it is not easy to make 
such a decision. She would also like to use such frameworks for implementation decisions, 
which partly refers to Step 4. In general, she would appreciate a framework to map occurring 
tasks to appropriate tools. Figure 6.2.1 could help to develop such a holistic framework. 
Finally, I discussed the framework and decision-making for crowdsourcing in general with 
UNIQA and uncovered the following (I3): UNIQA decided to solve their task with 
crowdsourcing as crowdsourcing was a fixed part of a cooperation with a university. Thus, it 
was not a thought-through decision. The OIL would not have chosen to crowdsource if it was 
not part of the cooperation. Her decision factors would otherwise be the following: What is the 
Innovation stage? (Step 1) What is the output goal? (Step 2) Which capabilities do solvers need? 
While I covered the first steps in the framework, she put a lot of focus on the solver`s 
capabilities and even questioned if cases exist, in which crowdsourcing is the best method. The 
importance of openness (Step 3) to mainly reach secondary goals was considered by UNIQA. 
To summarize, the application field is mostly not a focus of the seekers, but, surprisingly, 
sometimes flexible. However, the framework describes and facilitates three important steps that 
are considered by practitioners (innovation phase, problem focus, and anonymity). Thus, 
especially seekers with less experience could use such frameworks to facilitate their decision-
making. 
6.3 Discussion – Evaluation  
6.3.1 Discussion – Contribution dependent variables 
As theorized earlier, I expected the time of contribution to have a curvilinear relation to the 
probability that the contribution wins. The analyzed data did not support this relation. The 
contribution time did not differ significantly between the winning and the losing contributions. 
Neither their means nor their distributions differed significantly. Moreover, the contribution 
time did not improve the model for predicting the probability that the contribution wins. The 
==PAGE:110==
101 
 
analysis did neither show that users post fast if they have a good solution immediately nor that 
users develop good solutions over time. This might be due to highly individual user preferences 
and external factors that cannot be categorized as effortlessly as expected. Some solvers might 
immediately have a good idea and contribute it as quickly as possible. Others might have a good 
idea similarly fast but prefer to submit it later. Further, one might assume that the solution 
development does not require the whole contribution period. Thus, the extra time that late 
contributors use might not influence the quality of the solution if it reaches a certain threshold. 
Random external factors that might affect the time of contribution could be the point in the 
contribution period when the solvers learn about the contest or the schedules of the solvers at 
that time.   
I expected a positive relationship between the number of views and the probability of the 
contribution being a winning one. The analyzed data supported this relation. The mean number 
of views differed significantly between the winning and the losing contributions. The number 
of views had a positive, highly significant relationship with the probability of the contribution 
being a winning one. Better solutions seem more attractive and inspiring than worse solutions 
and, therefore, get more views. However, one must consider that the number of views suffers 
from reverse causality, as it further increased after the evaluation phase. One must assume that 
the winning contributions received more views because they are the winning ones. Thus, I 
excluded them from the models. However, I conducted a small data analysis of the number of 
views from contest 197. It included the number of views before the evaluation publication and 
showed that the number of views also differed significantly (p<0.01) before the jury published 
its evaluation (Figure 6.3.1; Appendix 6.8). I assume that seekers that have access to the number 
of views before the evaluation publication can use them to improve the model further.   
 
Figure 6.3.1. Difference between the number of views of winning and losing contributions before the evaluation was 
published. Source: Own representation.  
==PAGE:111==
102 
 
I expected a positive relationship between the number of likes and the probability of the 
contribution being a winning one. The analyzed data supported this relation. The mean number 
of likes differed significantly between the winning and the losing contributions. The number of 
likes had a positive, highly significant relationship with the probability of the contribution being 
a winning one. The solvers seem to be able to differentiate appropriately between better and 
worse contributions and actively reward the superb ones with likes. Overall, the number of likes 
is quite limited. That increases the relative value of each like, as solvers seem to give likes only 
if they are very convinced. However, it also decreases the possibility of finely differentiating 
between solutions solely based on the number of likes.  
As described above, the relation between the number of comments and the probability of the 
contribution being a winning one is quite complex from a theoretical standpoint. One could 
expect relationships in both directions. The analyzed data supported a positive relation. The 
mean number of comments differed significantly between the winning and the losing 
contributions. The number of comments had a positive, significant relationship with the 
probability of the contribution being a winning one. Better solutions seem more compelling and 
inspiring. They might also be better presented than worse solutions, and motivate the solvers to 
contribute comments. However, one must consider that the number of comments might also 
suffer from reverse causality, as it was, at least in some contests, possible to comment after the 
evaluation phase. One could have assumed that the winning contributions get more comments 
because they were selected as the winning ones (e.g., a congratulatory comment). However, as 
described above, I found no significant difference in the number of comments after the 
evaluation between the winning and losing contributions. Further, the HYVECrowd responsible 
questioned the useability of the number of comments (I1). The HYVECrowd managers and the 
seekers were often comment authors. Thus, the evaluating parties influenced the models. It 
decreases the theoretical value of the comment variables for the explanation of solver behavior. 
However, I formulated the models, first of all, to showcase the potential to predict good 
contributions. The pre-selection by the platform and the seeker decreased the importance of the 
solvers for the models. However, this has only a slight effect. The coefficient for the number of 
likes was approximately four times larger than the coefficient for the number of comments. 
Thus, the importance of the number of comments was comparably low. Finally, even though 
the seekers and the platform managers influence the model, they do it before the evaluation 
phase. Thus, the models do not lose their potential to decrease workload during the evaluation 
phase. Summarizing, the useability of the number of comments for theoretical purposes is 
==PAGE:112==
103 
 
decreased by the rightful objection by the HYVECrowd responsible, but its useability for its 
intended practical purpose is not.   
There are different levels of complexity of evaluation tools. Likes are straightforward to use for 
the solvers. Thus, the barrier to usage is comparably low, and seekers can receive many 
evaluations. However, more complex evaluation tools (e.g., X out of 5 stars) lead to a 
substantially more differentiated evaluation even if fewer users evaluate. The findings 
suggested that both options might be suitable. I assumed that if seekers offer both options in 
parallel, one might undermine the other, and solvers could be reluctant to use both. However, I 
could not find any support for this assumption and provided the R code for a short test in 
Appendix 6.7. 
I expected a positive relationship between the weighted solver evaluations and the probability 
of the contribution being a winning one. The analyzed data supported this relation. The mean 
of the solvers` evaluations differed significantly between the winning and the losing 
contributions. The weighted solver evaluations had a positive, highly significant relationship to 
the probability of the contribution being a winning one. UNIQA`s OIL highlighted the 
importance of the capabilities to appropriately asses the contributions (I3). The Solvers seem to 
be able to differentiate appropriately between better and worse solutions and to accordingly and 
actively evaluate contributions. Further, the HYVE responsible verified the assumption that the 
influence of the solver evaluations on the jury evaluation was negligible (I1). However, he also 
said that he inherits a skepticism towards user evaluations because users tend to be harsh 
towards each other`s ideas (I1). OeNPAY added that it is a must that experts assess the 
contributions` feasibility (I2). The high predictability of the winning contributions, see below, 
suggests other notions and conclusions, as solvers seem to evaluate in line with the expert juries. 
Evaluations are compared to the likes more finely differentiated between the contributions. 
However, the evaluations seem to be a worse predictor than the number of likes, as it is less 
significant in the models. The predictive capability of the evaluation variable and, thereby, the 
model should increase with the number of evaluations. First, the better predictive capability of 
the weighted (vs. unweighted) evaluations underlined the importance of the number of 
evaluations. Second, an increase in the number of evaluations should increase their value from 
a theoretical standpoint based on the wisdom of the crowd theory (see Surowiecki, 2005). The 
average of the evaluations of many solvers should be more precise than the evaluations of fewer 
solvers. HYVE and OeNPAY strongly agreed that the number of evaluations is crucial to obtain 
==PAGE:113==
104 
 
good predictability (I1, I2). Finally, HYVE also supported the argument that more evaluations 
might ease the problem of negative evaluations noted above (I1).   
6.3.2 Discussion - Contest dependent variables 
The model to predict the probability of a contribution being the winning one contained different 
control variables to include contest-specific effects (e.g., average evaluation in the contest). I 
expected all control variables to have a negative relation to the probability of the contribution 
being a winning one. The analyzed data supported these relations except for the number of 
views and the number of likes being a unique case. The average number of comments and 
evaluations and the average evaluation of a contest had a negative, significant relation to the 
probability of a contribution being the winning one. If the contributions for a contest are 
perceived or at least evaluated as good in general, it is harder to stand out than vice versa. The 
same holds for the number of comments and evaluations. The maximal number of likes in a 
contest was negatively and significantly related and a better predictor of the probability that a 
contribution wins than the average number of likes in a contest. I used the maximal number of 
likes to control its competition, while I mostly used mean values as control variables on other 
evaluation measures. The statistical reason is that it led to a better model fit. The mean does not 
represent the competition`s distribution accordingly if the variable is highly skewed. The 
theoretical logic might lie in the likes` nature. The number of likes is unlimited. Many contests 
received only a few likes, but a few aggregated many. Thus, only the number of likes of the 
most liked contributions matters.  
To summarize, most tested variables behaved as expected. The analyzed data supported the 
hypothesis that solvers are able and willing to evaluate other solvers’ contributions 
appropriately.  
6.3.3 Discussion – Workload and Accuracy  
The results showed 90% accuracy on 50% workload reduction on the tested contest for all four 
tested data sets. In other words, the contest jury must have assessed only 50% of the 
contributions and would have excluded only one of the ten winning solutions if it had used any 
of the models and excluded all contests that had a Y-value below the median of the Y-values. 
The results showed 90% accuracy for around 60% workload reduction on the tested contest for 
two of the four tested data sets. In other words, the contest jury could have assessed only 40% 
of the contributions and would have excluded only one of the ten winning solutions if it had 
used the first or the third model and excluded all contests that had a Y-value below the mean of 
==PAGE:114==
105 
 
the Y-values. 75 % workload reduction could have been obtained with 50% accuracy in two 
models and 60% accuracy in the others. If the jury had used the 75%-quantile as a cut-off for 
the Y-value they would have excluded 4 or 5 of the winning contributions. One of the winning 
contributions was also one of the contributions with the lowest Y-value. Thus, significant 
workload reduction could have only been obtained at the loss of that contribution.  
The results showed the very promising practicality of the models. Especially the consistency in 
their ability to exclude 50% of the contributions while maintaining 90% accuracy is convincing. 
Such a workload could substantially ease the information overload and reduce flexible 
evaluation costs. I used the mean, median, and other quantiles to maintain ease of use and 
comparability but seekers could optimize the expected workload-accuracy ratio for their 
purposes. OeNPAY agreed that user evaluations have a high indicational value and can be 
helpful during the evaluation (I2). Further, OeNPAY described that the evaluation workload 
was very high and that they did not have the right tools to include user evaluations (I2). 
However, they also articulated clear doubts to use the model as intended, as they feel that it is, 
in general, better to have a look at all contributions. Thus, they lack trust in the solver 
evaluations. UNIQA trusted students to pre-select the contributions and ease the workload 
significantly (I3). Thus, they had a demand for solutions to ease the workload and they trusted 
external, amateur evaluations. Further, UNIQA`s OIL agreed with this model’s practical value, 
especially if the solvers are potential users and if the workload can be reduced significantly (I3). 
Finally, the HYVECrowd responsible assessed the findings as follows (I1): Even considering 
the assumed selection bias as discussed above, he evaluated the results positively and found it 
compelling that the models achieved good results. Using user evaluation is, in general, 
conceivable for him. Further, he agreed that solver evaluation has potential, but the 
implementation is challenging. Finally, he strengthened the assumption that the effort that the 
evaluation requires poses a big problem and that automation would ease it. 
To summarize, the model had high predictive capability. It supported the hypothesis that 
seekers can use solver evaluations efficiently to reduce their workload while maintaining 
reasonable evaluation accuracy. Seekers can increase the efficiency of this method by attracting 
more user evaluations and including the number of views before the evaluation in the analysis. 
The professionals realized the potential of solver evaluations, were partly willing to use them, 
and articulated a need for workload reduction in the evaluation phase. However, doubts and 
critical barriers to the implementation of solver evaluations in real contests still exist.  
 
==PAGE:115==
106 
 
Key Points: 
1. Solvers evaluate other solvers` contributions appropriately. 
2. Seekers can efficiently reduce workload by using solver evaluations. 
6.4 The Future of Crowdsourcing  
In the discussion with the HYVECrowd responsible, among others, the parallels between this 
research and similar research based on artificial intelligence (AI) evaluations by Bell et al. 
(2023) and my findings on crowdsourcing platform stages led the discussion to the future of 
crowdsourcing. Thus, I will shortly address the development of crowdsourcing and its 
implications for practitioners.  
First, my investigation found that crowdsourcing platforms might go through different stages 
of development, which influence the number of solvers on the platform and their composition. 
The HYVECrowd responsible added the following (I1): HYVE`s crowd size is stable at the 
moment. However, the specifically designed crowdsourcing campaigns for the seekers, which 
they performed before, had and would still have considerably more contributions. In general, 
the crowdsourcing hype has decreased. Communities, nowadays, are stable and built around a 
core group of solvers. These solvers have probably high intrinsic motivation, and they 
participate regularly. Thus, the award size might matter less. Further, it is not ideal that these 
solvers optimize their winning chances and increase the barriers for beginners. Identical solvers 
win most of the time.  
It seems like crowdsourcing platforms suffer from reduced efficiency to serve their purpose. 
The stagnation and dominant core of the crowd are counterproductive to crowdsourcing goals 
(e.g., reaching a large heterogeneous group and generating a manifold of ideas from experts 
from different fields). While it seems like crowdsourcing is now cheaper for the seekers, as the 
importance of the award size seems to have decreased, the potential of attracting many capable 
solvers by using awards seems to have decreased. However, it is unclear if this problem only 
affects crowdsourcing platforms or all crowdsourcing initiatives.      
Second, the HYVECrowd responsible added the following to the parallels between this research 
and the research by Bell et al. (2023) (I1): HYVE has ongoing research on the topic. They 
contributed AI-generated ideas and found such contributions from solvers. The AI-generated 
contributions did not perform impressively well. However, it is important to develop rules for 
the usage of AI, as they want to maintain fairness. Copying ideas from AI should be forbidden. 
However, AI can also improve ideas in combination with the human ability to abstract on 
==PAGE:116==
107 
 
different levels. They observed that the AI is not yet capable of that abstraction ability. 
However, AI can probably decrease the relevance of crowdsourcing to find ideas soon. Thus, 
the secondary effects of crowdsourcing contests (e.g., marketing, branding, recruiting, and 
community exchange) could become even more relevant. 
While AI in crowdsourcing exceeds the scope of this investigation, this side note is relevant on 
different levels. First, the study on AI evaluations supports the assumption that automation in 
the evaluation phase is demanded. Second, the incapability of AI to abstract on higher levels 
strengthens the relevance of solver evaluations as an alternative to automating the evaluation. 
Third, the predicted increase of focus on secondary effects strengthens the relevance of many 
assumptions on the goal focus set up in this study. 
6.5 Limitations - Application fields 
Like every research, this paper suffers from limitations that researchers and practitioners should 
carefully consider while building on its implications. 
First, this investigation did not test the direct effect of the application fields and innovation 
phases on the success and potential of crowdsourcing but only on the number of solvers as a 
surrogate to answer the first research question. While prior research laid out the strong 
connection between the number of solvers and the success of crowdsourcing (Cappa et al., 
2019, DiPalantino and Vojnovic, 2009), the number of attracted solvers is undoubtedly not the 
only influence factor on the success of crowdsourcing. Thus, one should assume that using other 
surrogates might influence future findings. However, as one can assume that the number of 
solvers is a good indicator of crowdsourcing success, the relations` general trend (e.g., the 
direction of the influence) should hold.  
Second, confounders are very likely to exist (e.g., number of contests in parallel, time of the 
year, etc.), but I especially did not particularly control for the task`s complexity. One could 
regard task complexity as a crucial motivational factor. Thus, a causal interpretation of the 
findings is difficult. However, as noted before, the task complexity might be controlled partly 
by the award size and overview length. The HYVECrowd responsible also pointed to the 
complexity of crowdsourcing and the manifold influence factors (I1). He specifically named 
the marketing budget as one of the crucial influence factors that I could not consider. Some 
industries could have more marketing budgets than others and seekers with disclosed identities 
might also invest more into marketing. Thus, intuitive arguments exist why the marketing 
budget could be a confounder.  
==PAGE:117==
108 
 
Third, the analysis of the innovation phases lacked precise differentiation, as the platform 
provided merely limited information about it. However, it should not substantially affect the 
general implication that early innovation might be better suited for crowdsourcing.     
Fourth, the HYVECrowd data set was limited to a considerably low number of contests and is 
therefore not very representative. However, the dataset might still be sufficiently large enough 
to serve its presented function of supporting the findings of the Innocentive data set and 
investigating the potential differences between the number of solvers and the number of 
contributions. 
To summarize, practitioners and researchers should be careful about causally interpreting these 
results due to a lack of data and likely existing confounders 
6.6 Limitations - Motivation 
First, as the second research question was investigated with the same model as the first one, the 
same limitations regarding confounders and the control data set exist. However, the number of 
solvers directly measured the solvers` participation and posed no limitations for the second 
question.  
Second, the identity disclosure variable`s definition posed practical problems. The platform did 
not provide consistent information on it. I tried to overcome this problem by carefully defining 
keywords to detect the seeker`s identity and trying to correct mistakes manually after the 
automatic definition. These individually defined keywords could have missed identities in some 
cases. However, the use of differently defined identity disclosure variables indicated the high 
robustness of the findings. As noted, I also could not control the types of undisclosed seekers 
(e.g., size and ESG), which might be important confounders, as high-reputation seekers might 
rather hold open contests. Thus, the identity disclosure variable might result from a self-
selection process rather than a definite positive effect.  
Third, the likely confounder complexity is especially crucial regarding the findings of the award 
size and the task specificity. Both variables might partly explain the effect of complexity. If a 
better proxy would explain it, the results might behave differently. However, as noted, they can 
also act as an indicator of complexity for potential solvers and still show similar relations to the 
number of solvers after introducing a complexity proxy.  
Fourth, the proxy for task specificity (e.g., overview text length) is very ambiguous and might 
only explain some of the effects I theorized about in this paper.  
==PAGE:118==
109 
 
To summarize, some proxies might not be immaculately suited to represent the investigated 
effects, the confounders drastically weakened the causal interpretation, and the data sampling 
accuracy may have been flawed in some cases. 
6.7 Limitations - Evaluation 
First, one limitation is that the solvers did not evaluate more than one-third of all contributions. 
This decreased the sample size substantially and might have biased the analysis. However, the 
results showed robust findings after including the missing contributions in the analysis. Further, 
the bias should be in the direction opposing the findings, as solvers might be less likely to 
evaluate worse contributions, as they do not want to engage with them (see results for views 
and comments) and potentially do not want to discourage other solvers. 
Second, a small fraction of the solvers evaluated most contributions. Thus, I only controlled for 
the evaluations by a few solvers. The results might differ if more solvers had involved 
themselves in the evaluation. However, one might assume that increasing the number of solvers 
would only strengthen the results. The finding that the evaluation is a better predictor if 
weighted by the number of evaluations, supports this notion.  
Third, one cannot rule out the existence of confounders biasing the results and rendering a 
causal interpretation of the findings difficult. However, no evident confounders were identified. 
Further, the models aim to have a more practical function (e.g., helping the seeker to predict 
worse contributions) rather than a theoretical one (e.g., describing the effect of a variable on 
another).  
Fourth, I could not sufficiently clarify the degree to which reverse causality poses a problem to 
the theoretical interpretation of the model. However, as with the possibility of confounders, the 
presented models aim at a rather practical value. One should mainly assess them based on their 
ability to predict winning solutions rather than on the theoretical value of individual variables 
in the model. 
Fifth, I only analyzed one platform. Seekers might find other results on different platforms, 
especially if the competitive nature of the platform would wrongly incentivize solvers to give 
unfaithful evaluations. Further, it is unclear how solvers` behavior would change if they knew 
that seekers consider their evaluation during their jury`s evaluation process.  
Sixth, I could only test the models` accuracy on a fraction of contests. Thus, more testing is 
necessary to strengthen the results. However, the results are sufficient to indicate the potential 
==PAGE:119==
110 
 
of machine learning approaches based on solver behavior data in evaluation processes for 
crowdsourcing contributions.  
To summarize, causal interpretations of isolated relations (e.g., the relationship between the 
number of likes and the probability to win) found by the models are limited by the above-
mentioned flaws. However, the overall predictive value maintains promising.   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:120==
111 
 
7 Managerial Implications & Future Research 
In the following, I will transpose the compelling findings from the data analysis and discussion 
into managerial implications for potential seekers and crowdsourcing platforms. 
7.1 Managerial Implications - Application Fields & Motivation 
The first research question investigated in which application fields and under which 
circumstances seekers gain the greatest advantages from crowdsourcing. The analysis 
controlled for two influence factors: the industry and the innovation phase. I found significant 
indications of a relationship with the number of solvers for both. The relationship suggests that 
the innovation phase and the industry matter for the success of a crowdsourcing campaign.  
First, seekers must consider the current phase of their innovation process while assessing how 
appropriate crowdsourcing might be. Ideation as an early innovation phase seems better suited 
for crowdsourcing than other innovation phases. Seekers must consider crowdsourcing as an 
innovation technique before innovating. Otherwise, it might be too late for promising crowd 
involvement.  
Second, seekers must consider their task`s application field while assessing how appropriate 
crowdsourcing might be. Rather specific, closed fields seem to be less suited for crowdsourcing. 
This notion does not mean that seekers from closed industries should never consider using a 
crowd. But they have to carefully consider their application fields in their analysis of the 
potential of a crowdsourcing approach for their task. While ideation in a less suitable industry 
might still be appropriate, crowdsourcing in a later innovation phase in such an industry might 
not. Both the innovation phase and the industry must be considered in the complex decision-
making but should not be regarded as exclusion factors. Seekers could also ease their 
dependence on specific application fields by increasing the accessibility of their contests. 
OeNPAY provided a good example of such practice by addressing all application fields and 
formulating the task very consciously (I2).  
The second research question investigated which practices allow seekers to draw on the solvers` 
internal and external motivation to increase their participation in crowdsourcing contests. The 
analysis controlled for mainly three influence factors: identity disclosure, award size, and task 
specificity. I found significant indications of a relationship with the number of solvers for all, 
suggesting that the identity, the award size, and the task specificity matter to attract more 
solvers.  
==PAGE:121==
112 
 
First, seekers must consider disclosing their identity to the crowd. The disclosure seems to help 
to tap into many of the seekers` motivating factors. It might be particularly practical if the seeker 
has a good reputation and brand recognition or if solvers perceive their good intentions as 
genuine. Especially seekers from the chemistry field must consider disclosing their identity, as 
chemistry seems less fitting for crowdsourcing than other fields. However, identity disclosure 
is probably inhibiting the success of the crowdsourcing contests in engineering, mathematics, 
and business fields. Secrecy, to protect the seeker's interests (e.g., tactics in the competitive 
environment), must be carefully weighed against the motivational effects of identity disclosure. 
Finally, seekers should also consider the secondary goals that can be reached by openness. Both 
OeNPAY and UNIQA are good examples, as they focused between 40% and 50% on secondary 
goals such as marketing, positioning, and research. To summarize, the role of identity disclosure 
is highly relevant for practitioners.  
Second, seekers must carefully consider the size and type of their award, not only because the 
award might be a costly investment but because it might have contrary influences on the number 
of solvers. They must also consider the size of the award relative to the complexity of the task. 
It is crucial to attracting mainly monetarily motivated users. A high award might also be 
perceived as a signal of high complexity and daunt potential solvers. Further, it could repel 
primarily internally motivated solvers (e.g., in social contests). Overall, the award is one of, if 
not the most important mechanism to increase the solvers` motivation. The award size must be 
carefully determined and should reflect the complexity of the task realistically.  
Third, seekers must appropriately define and present the task. Not only because solvers need to 
understand it to generate good contributions, but it might have a direct influence on their 
participation. Specifically defined tasks seem to attract fewer solvers. The seekers must attempt 
to compress the information needed to solve the task as muss as possible. Otherwise, they might 
daunt potential solvers. Seekers should also provide sufficient room for flexibility and creativity 
to increase the solvers` motivation and address a broader range of potential solvers.  
Fourth, seekers need to differentiate between maximizing the number of solvers and 
contributions. The number of solvers might be the better KPI to measure participation goals in 
line with crowdsourcing outcome goals. Thus, seekers should use the number of active solvers 
rather than the number of contributions as the KPI for crowdsourcing participation. Platforms 
and solvers must find compromises to increase the number of solvers and the individual solver 
effort on every single contribution. Thereby, platforms must especially weigh the interests of 
high-output solvers against the interests of new solvers.     
==PAGE:122==
113 
 
Finally, the implications above mostly address real-world decisions that practitioners have to 
make, which can be facilitated by frameworks such as the one presented in Figure 6.2.1.  
7.2 Managerial Implications - Evaluation 
The third research question investigated to what extent solver involvement could affect the 
workload and accuracy of the evaluation in a crowdsourcing contest. The analysis controlled 
for four measures of solvers` evaluation, namely the number of views, number of comments, 
and number of likes, and the solver evaluation weighted by the number of evaluations. I found 
significant indications of a relationship with the chance of winning the contest for all. This 
relationship suggests that one can find appropriate measures to predict the success of 
contributions based on the solvers` engagement.  
Seekers should consider involving the solvers in the evaluation process. Not only because it 
might shorten the evaluation process, ease the jury`s information load, and save money but 
because solvers can effectively distinguish good and bad contributions. Further, the right tools 
exist for solvers to submit their evaluations and for seekers to shorten their innovation 
processes.  
Seekers must consider which evaluation tool to use, especially regarding their expected usage 
rates. Further, seekers must incentivize the solvers to use the evaluation tools to receive 
adequate evaluations for all contributions. Making evaluations of a small number of 
contributions from other solvers before contributing mandatory might be a compelling method 
to increase the number of evaluations for seekers and innovation intermediaries.  
Other engagement measures might help seekers to distinguish between good and worse 
contributions. They might measure the number of views and comments to control the users` 
interest in particular contributions, which could help predict their quality.  
To summarize, seekers should consider using evaluation tools and other measures to predict the 
quality of a contribution and, thus, reduce their evaluation workload. Further, seekers can 
increase the prediction quality by encouraging more solver engagement.  
Finally, I want to emphasize the importance of this study for practitioners in general based on 
the reactions from industry experts. First, as mentioned above, the respondence rate for the 
interviews was much higher than expected and a first indication that the investigated questions 
were relevant for practitioners. Second, UNIQA`s OIL requested the final results of this study 
(I3). She was really interested to read it, even though she thinks that methods (e.g., 
==PAGE:123==
114 
 
crowdsourcing) are usually overrated and she is not interested in pursuing crowdsourcing soon 
again. However, she assumes that crowdsourcing might be a good method if one invests the 
effort to do it right. This study`s goal was to facilitate exactly that. Third, the OeNPAY 
interviewees reacted very positively to this investigation`s design and findings (I2). The SIM 
highlighted the quantification of crowdsourcing success criteria, which was one of the crucial 
parts of this study`s motivation. As mentioned, she also assessed frameworks such as the one 
presented in this study as very useful. Further, she requested the final results to potentially help 
them improve in future contests. Finally, the HYVECrowd responsible assessed this study as 
very similar to the work they do (I1). Further, he requested the final results, even though he was 
aware that he saw the most interesting findings during the interview. He would also appreciate 
a presentation of this investigation`s findings at their office. All interviewees’ comments 
suggested that this study`s research questions and results are highly relevant for practitioners. 
However, not only new insights but also new questions emerged during this investigation. In 
the following, I propose directions for future research based on the findings and limitations of 
this investigation. 
7.3 Future Research – Fields of Application & Motivation  
First, to better assess the potential of crowdsourcing in different fields and innovation phases, 
the seeker`s perception of the contest`s success should be considered in future research (e.g., as 
a dependent variable).  
Second, confounders that were not systematically considered in this investigation should be 
controlled in future research. This would enable more reliable results and corroborate causal 
interpretations. Complexity may be the most interesting confounder to control. The task`s 
complexity and interplay with other influence factors (e.g., award size) will potentially unfold 
interesting dynamics in crowdsourcing contests.  
Third, future research should test samples from different platforms to increase the result`s 
robustness and to identify potential differences between platforms. As the results indicated, 
crowds on distinctive platforms might also differ in their interests and in their capabilities for 
specific fields. A crucial platform difference shortly mentioned in this paper might exist 
between cooperative and competitive platforms. However, one can also assume that some 
platforms are more technology-focused than others, and other, here unconsidered, differences 
may become revealed by future research. 
==PAGE:124==
115 
 
Fourth, the results showed interesting findings regarding identity disclosure. However, many 
possibilities for follow-up research are left over. Further research should investigate influence 
factors like the seeker`s size and CSR/ESG rating, as this investigation might provide 
compelling insights into the solvers` motivation. As mentioned, researchers investigated some 
of these influencing factors, but a broader quantitative foundation is still required. Further, 
researchers should start investigating the characteristics of seekers, who chose to keep their 
identity secret. Thereby, they could minimize self-selection effects, investigate the importance 
of secrecy, and improve the derivation of managerial implications.  
The potential research questions could be: 
1.  In which fields are the most seekers satisfied with crowdsourcing? 
2. How does the task`s complexity influence the number of solvers?  
3. Which crowdsourcing platforms are suitable for which type of innovation? 
4. How do seekers who disclose their identity differ from seekers who do not? 
7.4 Future Research – Evaluation  
First, I suggest an experiment on a crowdsourcing platform that investigates if the evaluation 
accuracy changes after seekers introduce mandatory solver evaluations. Otherwise, the 
evaluation quality could change unnoticed. One result could be that more unfaithful solvers 
could try to influence the contest to achieve an advantage. Further, such an experiment would 
generate a sample that would not miss any evaluations and could prevent reverse causalities. 
Thus, it would be less biased.  
Second, the investigation of another platform might be compelling. There might be differences 
in, for example, the crowd`s competitiveness which could substantially change the evaluations` 
dynamics. This is particularly true given that the awards on the investigated platform were 
comparably low compared to considerably higher awards on platforms like Innocentive.  
Third, further research should test the models and develop them further on more data. Thereby, 
one could more accurately assess the potential of machine learning based on solver behavior in 
facilitating the evaluation.  
Fourth, further research should test more variables for the models. Developing methods that 
can generate further information from the contribution`s content will increase their practical 
usefulness substantially. For example, text analysis software could group similar contributions 
==PAGE:125==
116 
 
and rank them in their groups based on specific evaluation criteria. Through the grouping, one 
could also quickly discover unique ideas not fitting in groups containing similar contributions.  
To summarize, this paper presented a foundation that showed the potential of machine learning 
based on solver evaluations in evaluation processes. However, machine learning improves 
mainly by generating and analyzing more data, which should be the main focus for future 
researchers, as well as for practitioners. 
The potential research questions could be:  
1. Does mandatory (vs. voluntary) solver evaluation also lead to high workload reduction 
while maintaining reasonable accuracy? 
2. Based on which characteristics can machines distinguish between bad and good 
contributions?  
3. How much of the evaluation effort could seekers automize?    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:126==
117 
 
8 Conclusion 
I adopted two research questions from a broad literature review by Ghezzi et al. (2018), one on 
the crowdsourcing application fields and another on the solver`s motivation. Further, I 
formulated a research question regarding the potential of solvers` involvement in the evaluation 
phase. These questions targeted crucial research gaps in the crowdsourcing and open innovation 
literature. I adapted theory from prior research and developed it further to derive hypotheses.  
I built data sets of contests from two different crowdsourcing platforms and formulated linear 
models to describe the variance in the number of solvers between contests. First, I found a 
significant positive relationship between ideation and the number of solvers. Second, I found 
significant positive relationships between general, flexible, and social fields and the number of 
solvers. Further, I found significant negative relationships between rigid, specific fields and the 
number of solvers. The relationships between the fields and the number of solvers are 
moderated by identity disclosure. Third, I found a significant positive relationship between 
identity disclosure and the number of solvers. Fourth, I found a significant negative relationship 
between the text length and the number of solvers. Fifth, I found a significant logarithmic 
relationship between the award size and the number of solvers. This relationship is moderated 
by the application fields. I suggested that seekers must consider their innovation phase and 
application field before engaging in crowdsourcing. Further, I proposed that seekers engaging 
in crowdsourcing should consider disclosing their identity, set an appropriate award size, and 
carefully formulate their tasks. Finally, I proposed an extension of existing decision-making 
frameworks in crowdsourcing and suggested how researchers should extend this research to 
improve the understanding of crucial crowdsourcing components. I built data sets of 
contributions from contests from one crowdsourcing platform to answer the third research 
question and used the solvers` evaluations to formulate a logistic model to predict the winning 
contributions of a contest. First, I found a significant positive relationship between the 
evaluations and the winning probability. Second, I found a significant positive relationship 
between the number of likes and the winning probability. Third, I found a significant positive 
relationship between the number of comments and the winning probability. Fourth, I found that 
seekers might be able to reduce their workload by 50% while maintaining 90% accuracy. I 
suggested that seekers should involve the solvers in the evaluation phase to decrease their 
evaluation costs substantially. Further, I proposed that researchers should extend this research 
to real-world experiments to control for changes in solver behavior. Finally, I conducted 
interviews with crowdsourcing professionals and found substantial indications that the 
conducted quantitative analysis may have direct implications for practitioners.

10.1 Scraping - Innocentive 
10.1.1 Scraping Innocentive – Extracting the Link for Each Contest  
 
##Go through Innocentive get all links and titles and save in file. 
import requests 
import pandas as pd 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
import requests 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
#scrape data  
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    links = [] 
    texts = [] 
    url = f"https://challenge-center.community.wazoku.com/discover?entities=challenge&sort=-
posted&page=1&pageSize=15&include_descendants&communityId=9396a088f8614c2eac89aacf2ae1c624&filt
ers=%7B%22show_archived%22:true%7D" 
    response = requests.get(url) 
    driver.get(url) 
    time.sleep(10) 
 
    #Scroll to the end of the page 
    for i in range(0,113): 
        driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
        time.sleep(7)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
        print() 
 
    #Locate the title 
    txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@data-selenium,'card-header-title')]") 
    title = len(txttitle) 
    #wk-card-header__title md-font-size u-line-clamp u-line-clamp--3 
    for i in txttitle: 
        texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "")) 
        links.append(i.get_attribute("href")) 
         
    #to csv  
    df = pd.DataFrame(texts)  
    df1 = pd.DataFrame(links)  
    df["links"] = df1 
    df.to_csv("Inno Links_06.03.csv")

# import libaries 
 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
import pandas as pd 
 
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #read in the urls 
    url_list = pd.read_csv("Inno Links_06.03.csv") 
    #url_list = ["https://challenge-
center.community.wazoku.com/challenge/e45337fc0611477fbe63f7ce3fa7e753?searchIndex=1","https://environ
ment.community.wazoku.com/challenge/ad180a0d0d8949e0a585745759556214?searchIndex=1","https://sustain
able-energy.community.wazoku.com/challenge/f7a71d9931154a2082198148ad8ec3a1?searchIndex=2"] 
     
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    texts = [] 
 
    #loop through all instances 
    for url in url_list["Link"]: 
    #for url in url_list:  
        #search for the url 
        text_i = [] 
        print(url) 
        driver.get(url) 
 
        #Scroll to the end of the page 
        driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
        time.sleep(1)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
        #Locate the title 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h1[contains(@class,'ng-binding ng-scope')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            text_i.append(i.text) 
            #k = i.text.replace("\n","") 
         
        #Locate the texts from the description   
        txtResults = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//p[contains(@class,'ng-binding')]") 
        totalResults=len(txtResults) 
 
        for i in txtResults: 
            text_i.append(i.text) 
         
        #Locate the main text  
        txtmain = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'article-description rte-content ng-
binding ng-scope')]") 
        main = len(txtmain) 
 
        for i in txtmain: 
            text_i.append(i.text) 
==PAGE:137==
128 
 
             
        #Append to the dataset 
        texts.append(text_i) 
        print(len(texts)) 
     
    #to csv  
    df = pd.DataFrame(texts)  
    df.to_csv("Innocentive_02.02_part_9.csv") 
 
10.1.3 Scraping Innocentive – Data Cleaning (Big Data Set as an Example) 
 
import pandas as pd 
import re 
import dateutil.parser as dparser 
from dateutil.parser import * 
 
#define a function to select text between strings 
def foo(s, before, after): 
  end_before = s.index(before) + len(before) 
  start_after = s.index(after, end_before) 
  return s[end_before:start_after] 
 
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #read data 
    data = pd.read_csv("InnocentiveCom.csv") 
 
    #drop useless 
    #data.drop("6", axis = 1, inplace =True) 
    #data.drop("7", axis = 1, inplace=True) 
    #data.drop("8", axis = 1, inplace=True) 
 
    #rename columns  
    data.rename(columns = {'0':'Title'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'1':'Award'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'2':'Solvers'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'3':'Deadline'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {"4": "Tags"}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'5':'Keywords'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'6':'SBL'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {"7": "SUB"}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'8':'SOL'}, inplace = True) 
    data.rename(columns = {'9':'Text'}, inplace = True) 
 
    #drop empty 
    for i in range(len(data)): 
        if pd.isnull(data.loc[i, 'Title']) == True: 
            data.drop(i, axis=0, inplace = True) 
    data.reset_index(inplace = True)

    for i in range(len(data)):

10.2.1 Scraping HYVECrowd – Extracting the Contest Numbers  
##Go through HyveCrowd, get all contest numbers and save in file 
import requests 
import pandas as pd 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
import requests 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
#scrape data  
if __name__ == "__main__": 
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    contests_numbers = [] 
 
    for contests in range(1,202): #1 -> 201 
        url = f"https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contest/{contests}/overview" 
        response = requests.get(url) 
        driver.get(url) 
 
        #Scroll to the end of the page 
        driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
        time.sleep(1)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
        #Locate the title 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h1[contains(@class,'tw-text-white tw-font-bold tw-mb-0 tw-
text-2xl tw-leading-2xl tw-mb-3 md:tw-text-4xl md:tw-leading-4xl')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
         
        if len(txttitle) != 0: 
            contests_numbers.append(contests)  
         
    print(contests_numbers) 
    ''' 
    [1, 10, 11, 13, 15, 18, 19, 20, 21, 22, 23, 25, 26, 27, 28, 29, 30, 32, 33, 34, 35, 37, 38, 39, 41, 42, 43, 44, 46, 
52, 54, 57, 58, 59, 76, 92, 131, 133, 137, 138, 140, 142, 143, 149, 151, 152, 153, 154, 156, 157, 158, 159, 160, 
161, 162, 163, 167, 168, 169, 170, 171, 173, 175, 177, 178, 179, 180, 181, 182, 183, 184, 185, 186, 187, 188, 
190, 194, 195, 197, 198, 199, 200, 201] 
    ''' 
    df2 = pd.DataFrame(contests_numbers) 
    df2.to_csv("HyveCrowdContests_26.01.csv") 
 
10.2.2 Scraping HYVECrowd – Extracting the Contest Data 
##Go through HyveCrowd, get all contests and save in file. 
import requests 
import pandas as pd 
==PAGE:158==
149 
 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
import requests 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
#scrape data  
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    combined = [] 
    contest_numbers = pd.read_csv("HyveCrowdContests_26.01.csv") 
 
    for contests in contest_numbers: 
        texts = [] 
        url = f"https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contest/{ID}/overview" 
        response = requests.get(url) 
        driver.get(url) 
 
        #Scroll to the end of the page 
        driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
        time.sleep(1)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
        #Save the ID 
        texts.append(url.replace("/overview", "")[-3:].replace("/", "0").replace("t","0")) 
 
        #Locate the title 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h1[contains(@class,'tw-text-white tw-font-bold tw-mb-0 tw-
text-2xl tw-leading-2xl tw-mb-3 md:tw-text-4xl md:tw-leading-4xl')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "")) 
 
        #Locate the subtitle 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h2[contains(@class,'tw-text-white tw-m-0 tw-font-normal tw-
text-xl tw-leading-xl md:tw-text-2xl md:tw-leading-2xl tw-hidden md:tw-block')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "")) 
 
        #Locate the Description  
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//p[contains(@class,'contest-description-text')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "")) 
 
        #Locate the Integers 
==PAGE:159==
150 
 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'stat')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", " ")) 
 
        #Locate finish Date 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'phase-date')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "")) 
         
        #Locate Tabs 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'menu-wrapper')]") 
        title = len(txttitle) 
 
        for i in txttitle: 
            texts.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "")) 
 
        #Append to the dataset 
        combined.append(texts) 
 
    #check  
    print(combined) 
 
    #clean of empty lists  
    combined_clean = list(filter(lambda x: x != [], combined)) 
 
    #to csv  
    df = pd.DataFrame(combined_clean)  
    df.to_csv("HyveCrowd_08.02.csv")

#Award size  
import pandas as pd 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
import requests 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
titles = [] 
awards = [] 
links = [] 
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    for page in range(1,9): #1 -> 10 
        texts = [] 
==PAGE:160==
151 
 
        url = 
f"https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contests?page={page}&per_page=18&tags=&statuses=&sort=published&directi
on=desc" 
        driver.get(url) 
        print(page) 
 
        #Scroll to the end of the page 
        driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
        time.sleep(5)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
        #Locate the title 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h4[contains(@class,'tw-m-0 tw-text-lg tw-leading-base tw-
font-bold')]") 
        for i in txttitle: 
            titles.append(i.get_attribute("textContent")) 
         
        #Locate the link 
        txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@class,'tw-flex tw-flex-col tw-h-full tw-min-h-
480 hover:tw-no-underline')]") 
        for i in txttitle: 
            links.append(i.get_attribute("href")[-3:].replace("/", "0").replace("t","0")) 
 
        #Locate the Award 
        award = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//span[contains(@class,'tw-text-right')]") 
        for i in award: 
            awards.append(i.get_attribute("textContent")) 
 
    d = pd.DataFrame(titles) 
    d1 = pd.DataFrame(awards) 
    d2 = pd.DataFrame(links) 
    d["awards"] = d1 
    d["links"] = d2 
 
    d.to_csv("HyveCrowd_Awards_08.02.csv")

10.2.5 Scraping HYVECrowd – Extracting the Categories 
#Scrape all categories and corresponding contests  
 
import requests 
import pandas as pd 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
#scrape data  
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    combined = [] 
 
    for categories in range(1,25): #1->24 
        contest_in_category = [] 
        links_in_category = [] 
        contest_and_link = [] 
        for pages in range(1,7): #1->6 
            url = 
f"https://www.hyvecrowd.com/contests?page={pages}&per_page=9&tags={categories}&statuses=&sort=publis
hed&direction=desc" 
            response = requests.get(url) 
            driver.get(url) 
 
            #Scroll to the end of the page 
            driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
            time.sleep(3)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
            #Locate the titles 
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h4[contains(@class,'tw-m-0 tw-text-lg tw-leading-base tw-
font-bold')]") 
            for i in txttitle: 
==PAGE:162==
153 
 
                contest_in_category.append(i.get_attribute("textContent")) 
 
            #Locate the link 
            link = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@class,'tw-flex tw-flex-col tw-h-full tw-min-h-
480 hover:tw-no-underline')]") 
 
            for i in link: 
                links_in_category.append(i.get_attribute("href")[-3:].replace("/", "0").replace("t","0")) 
             
            for i in range(len(contest_in_category)): 
                contest_and_link.append((contest_in_category[i], links_in_category[i])) 
 
        combined.append(contest_and_link)           
        print(combined) 
 
    #to csv  
    df = pd.DataFrame(combined)  
    df.to_csv("HyveCrowd_categories_08.02.csv", sep = ";")

center tw-text-text-primary tw-text-sm tw-leading-sm tw-py-12')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                rest.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n","").strip()) 
             
            #Locate the link!!!  
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@class,'tw-block tw-flex-1 hover:tw-no-
underline')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                link.append(i.get_attribute(str("href"))) 
                if len(str(contest)) == 3: 
                    ide.append(str(contest)) 
                elif len(str(contest)) == 2: 
                    ide.append("0"+str(contest)) 
                elif len(str(contest)) == 1: 
                    ide.append("00"+str(contest)) 
        for i in range(len(link)): 
            sol_id.append(str(link[i])[-5:].replace("/", "0").replace("w","0").replace("o","0").replace("h","0")) 
             
            #if ide[] == True 
                 
    #likes, comments, evaluation views  
    d1 = pd.DataFrame(solver) 
    d2 = pd.DataFrame(date) 
    d3 = pd.DataFrame(title) 
    d4 = pd.DataFrame(subtitle) 
    d5 = pd.DataFrame(rest) 
    d6 = pd.DataFrame(link) 
    d7 = pd.DataFrame(ide) 
    d71 = pd.DataFrame(sol_id) 
 
    d1["date"] = d2 
    d1["title"] = d3 
    d1["subtitle"] = d4 
    d1["rest"] = d5 
    d1["link"] = d6 
    d1["id"] = d7 
    d1["sol_id"] = d71 
 
    likes = [] 
    comments = [] 
    evaluation = [] 
    views = [] 
    for i in range(len(d1)): 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace("    "," ") 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace("   "," ") 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace("  "," ") 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace(" ", ",") 
        l=[e.strip() for e in d1["rest"][i].split(',')] 
        likes.append(l[0]) 
        comments.append(l[1]) 
        evaluation.append(l[2]) 
        try: 
            views.append(l[3]) 
==PAGE:169==
160 
 
        except: 
            views.append(l[2]) 
            evaluation[i] = "NA" 
     
    d8 = pd.DataFrame(likes) 
    d9 = pd.DataFrame(comments) 
    d10 = pd.DataFrame(evaluation) 
    d11 = pd.DataFrame(views) 
 
    d1["likes"] = d8 
    d1["comments"] = d9 
    d1["evaluation"] = d10 
    d1["view"] = d11

Facebook","").replace("Share on Twitter","").replace("Share on LinkedIn","").strip())

163 
 
                        solutions.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "").replace("based 
on","").replace("member ratings","").replace("basierend auf", "").replace("Mitgliederbewertungen","").strip())

                txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'vue-star-rating')]") #upvote, 
comment, evaluation, views

views 
 
                for i in txttitle: 
                    solutions.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "").strip())    
 
                #Locate if comment by author 
                txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'tw-flex tw-items-center tw-flex-
1')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views

                    if i != txttitle[0]: 
                        solutions.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n", "").replace("             ","

import requests 
import pandas as pd 
from selenium import webdriver

import time

from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
#scrape data  
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    contest_numbers = [1, 10, 11, 13, 15, 18, 19, 20, 21, 22, 23, 25, 26, 27, 28, 29, 30, 32, 33, 34, 35, 37, 38, 39, 
41, 42, 43, 44, 46, 52, 54, 57, 58, 59, 76, 92, 131, 133, 137, 138, 140, 142, 143, 149, 151, 152, 153, 154, 156, 
157, 158, 159, 160, 161, 162, 163, 167, 168, 169, 170, 171, 173, 175, 177, 178, 179, 180, 181, 182, 183, 184,

    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    combined = [] 
 
    for contest in contest_numbers: 
        contest_in_category = [] 
        for place in (26,27,28,29,30,31,32,33,34,127): #identitfication numbers for winning contributions 
            url = 
f"https://hyvecrowd.com/contest/{contest}/contribution?page=1&per_page=9&tags={place}&phases=&sort=cre
ated_at&direction=desc" 
            print(url) 
            response = requests.get(url) 
            driver.get(url) 
 
            #Scroll to the end of the page 
            driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
            time.sleep(1)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
            #Locate the titles 
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h4[contains(@class,'tw-m-0 tw-text-lg tw-leading-lg tw-
font-bold')]") 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                contest_in_category.append(contest) 
                contest_in_category.append(i.get_attribute("textContent")) 
             
            #Locate ID  
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@class,'tw-block tw-flex-1 hover:tw-no-
underline')]") 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                contest_in_category.append(i.get_attribute("href")) 
                contest_in_category.append(i.get_attribute("href")[-5:].replace("/", 
"0").replace("w","0").replace("o","0").replace("h","0")) 
 
        combined.append(contest_in_category)           
    print(combined) 
 
    #to csv

import pandas as pd 
 
data = pd.read_csv("HyveCrowd_Winners_25.01.csv", sep=";") 
 
l = [] 
for i in range(len(data)): 
    l.append([data["1"][i],data["2"][i],data["3"][i],data["0"][i],1]) 
    l.append([data["5"][i],data["6"][i],data["7"][i],data["0"][i],2]) 
    l.append([data["9"][i],data["10"][i],data["11"][i],data["0"][i],3]) 
    l.append([data["13"][i],data["14"][i],data["15"][i],data["0"][i],4]) 
    l.append([data["17"][i],data["18"][i],data["19"][i],data["0"][i],5]) 
    l.append([data["21"][i],data["22"][i],data["23"][i],data["0"][i],6]) 
    l.append([data["25"][i],data["26"][i],data["27"][i],data["0"][i],7]) 
    l.append([data["29"][i],data["30"][i],data["31"][i],data["0"][i],8]) 
    l.append([data["33"][i],data["34"][i],data["35"][i],data["0"][i],9]) 
    l.append([data["37"][i],data["38"][i],data["39"][i],data["0"][i],10]) 
    l.append([data["41"][i],data["42"][i],data["43"][i],data["0"][i],11]) 
    l.append([data["45"][i],data["46"][i],data["47"][i],data["0"][i],12])

            print("X")

#HyveCrowdSolWin 
import pandas as pd

d2 = pd.read_csv("HyveCrowd_W_14.02.csv", sep=";")

    l.append(0) 
 
le = pd.DataFrame(l) 
d1["place"] = le.values 
 
for i in range(len(d2)): 
        d2["2"][i] == str(d2["2"][i]) 
 
for i in range(len(d2)): 
    try: 
        d2["2"][i] == int(d2["2"][i].replace(".0","")) 
    except: 
        pass 
 
x = 0 
for i in range(len(d1)): 
==PAGE:178==
169 
 
    for j in range(len(d2)): 
        if int(d1["sol_id"][i]) == d2["2"][j]: 
            d1["place"][i] = d2["4"][j] 
            x = x+1 
            print(x) 
        elif int(d1["sol_id"][i]) != d2["2"][j] and d1["title"][i] == d2["0"][j]: 
            d1["place"][i] = d2["4"][j] 
            x = x+1 
            print(x) # found 419/419 
 
d1.to_csv("HyveCrowd_Sol_Win.csv", sep=";") 
 
10.3.9 Scraping HYVE II – Data Cleaning Date and Adding Lengths 
#Hyve Solution cleaning 
import pandas as pd 
 
d = pd.read_csv("HyveCrowd_Sol_Win.csv", sep=";") 
 
l = [] 
k = [] 
j = [] 
for i in range(len(d)): 
    l.append(len(d["text"][i])) 
    k.append(len(d["title"][i])) 
    j.append(len(d["subtitle"][i])) 
 
le = pd.DataFrame(l) 
ke = pd.DataFrame(k) 
je = pd.DataFrame(j) 
d["text_l"] = le.values 
d["title_l"] = ke.values 
d["subtitle_l"] = je.values 
 
for i in range(len(d)): 
    d["date"][i] = d["date"][i].replace("Published on","").replace("Veröffentlicht am", "").replace(".","/").strip() 
 
print(d["date"]) 
 
d.to_csv("HyveCrowd_Sol_Win_clean.csv", sep=";") 
 
10.3.10 Scraping HYVE II – Scraping Overview Contest 197 after Evaluation Phase  
#scrape solution overview 
import requests 
import pandas as pd 
from selenium import webdriver 
import time 
from webdriver_manager.chrome import ChromeDriverManager 
from selenium.webdriver.common.by import By 
 
#scrape data  
if __name__ == "__main__": 
 
    #install driver 
    chrome_options = webdriver.ChromeOptions() 
    chrome_options.add_argument('headless') 
==PAGE:179==
170 
 
    chrome_options.add_argument('window-size=1920x1080') 
    chrome_options.add_argument("disable-gpu") 
    driver = webdriver.Chrome(ChromeDriverManager().install(), chrome_options=chrome_options) 
 
    #create dataset  
    combined = [] 
    contest_numbers = [197] 
 
    solutions = [] 
    solver = [] 
    date = [] 
    title = [] 
    subtitle = [] 
    rest = [] 
    link = [] 
    ide = [] 
    sol_id = [] 
    for contest in contest_numbers: 
        print(contest) 
        for pages in range(1,24): #max 402 contributions -> 18 per page -> 23 pages  
            url = 
f"https://hyvecrowd.com/contest/{contest}/contribution?page={pages}&per_page=18&tags=&phases=&sort=cr
eated_at&direction=desc" 
            response = requests.get(url) 
            driver.get(url) 
 
            #Scroll to the end of the page 
            driver.execute_script("window.scrollTo(0, document.body.scrollHeight);") 
            time.sleep(3)#sleep_between_interactions 
 
            #Locate solver  
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@class,'tw-text-text-primary tw-font-bold tw-
cursor-pointer hover:tw-text-primary hover:tw-no-underline')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                solver.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n","").strip()) 
 
            #Locate date   
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'tw-mt-4 tw-text-xs tw-leading-xs tw-
text-text-secondary tw-truncate')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                date.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n","").strip())                 
 
            #Locate the titles 
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//h4[contains(@class,'tw-m-0 tw-text-lg tw-leading-lg tw-
font-bold')]") 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                title.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n","").strip()) 
             
            #Locate the subtitles 
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//p[contains(@class,'tw-m-0 tw-mt-8 tw-text-base tw-
leading-base')]") 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                subtitle.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n","").strip()) 
==PAGE:180==
171 
 
             
            #Locate the Rest  
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//div[contains(@class,'tw-flex tw-justify-between tw-items-
center tw-text-text-primary tw-text-sm tw-leading-sm tw-py-12')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views 
 
            for i in txttitle: 
                rest.append(i.get_attribute("textContent").replace("\n","").strip()) 
             
            #Locate the link!!!  
            txttitle = driver.find_elements(By.XPATH, "//a[contains(@class,'tw-block tw-flex-1 hover:tw-no-
underline')]") #upvote, comment, evaluation, views 
            for i in txttitle: 
                link.append(i.get_attribute(str("href"))) 
                if len(str(contest)) == 3: 
                    ide.append(str(contest)) 
                elif len(str(contest)) == 2: 
                    ide.append("0"+str(contest)) 
                elif len(str(contest)) == 1: 
                    ide.append("00"+str(contest)) 
        for i in range(len(link)): 
            sol_id.append(str(link[i])[-5:].replace("/", "0").replace("w","0").replace("o","0").replace("h","0")) 
      
            #if ide[] == True      
    #likes, comments, evaluation views  
    d1 = pd.DataFrame(solver) 
    d2 = pd.DataFrame(date) 
    d3 = pd.DataFrame(title) 
    d4 = pd.DataFrame(subtitle) 
    d5 = pd.DataFrame(rest) 
    d6 = pd.DataFrame(link) 
    d7 = pd.DataFrame(ide) 
    d71 = pd.DataFrame(sol_id) 
 
    d1["date"] = d2 
    d1["title"] = d3 
    d1["subtitle"] = d4 
    d1["rest"] = d5 
    d1["link"] = d6 
    d1["id"] = d7 
    d1["sol_id"] = d71 
    likes = [] 
    comments = [] 
    evaluation = [] 
    views = [] 
    for i in range(len(d1)): 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace("    "," ") 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace("   "," ") 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace("  "," ") 
        d1["rest"][i] = d1["rest"][i].replace(" ", ",") 
        l=[e.strip() for e in d1["rest"][i].split(',')] 
        likes.append(l[0]) 
        comments.append(l[1]) 
        evaluation.append(l[2]) 
        try: 
            views.append(l[3]) 
        except: 
            views.append(l[2]) 
==PAGE:181==
172 
 
            evaluation[i] = "NA” 
    d8 = pd.DataFrame(likes) 
    d9 = pd.DataFrame(comments) 
    d10 = pd.DataFrame(evaluation) 
    d11 = pd.DataFrame(views) 
    d1["likes"] = d8 
    d1["comments"] = d9 
    d1["evaluation"] = d10 
    d1["view"] = d11 
    #to csv   
    d1.to_csv("HyveCrowd_197_overview_24.03.csv", sep = ";")

#Innocentive descriptives 
 
################### 
##import packages## 
################### 
 
library(psych) 
library(dplyr)  
library(stargazer)  
library(ggplot2)  
 
############### 
###Read Data### 
############### 
 
#read .csv 
d <- read.csv(file = "C:/Users/t_fud/OneDrive/Desktop/Master 
Thesis/Crowdsourcing/Data/Hacken/Innocentive_with_time_06.03.csv", sep=";") 
#d <- read.csv(file = "C:/Users/t_fud/OneDrive/Desktop/Master 
Thesis/Crowdsourcing/Data/Hacken/Innocentive_with_time_06.03_2.csv", sep=";") 
 
#remove unnecessary data 
#exclude calls for partnerships and similar 
df3 <- d %>% filter(Ideation == 1 | Reduction.to.Practice == 1 | Theoretical...Licensing == 1 | Theoretical.IP.Transfer == 1) 
#Introducing a second Seeker Known variable that is 0 if their is no entry 
df3 <- transform(df3, Seeker.Known_2 = ifelse(is.na(Seeker.Known) != TRUE, Seeker.Known, 0)) 
#remove missing award values 
df4 <- df3 %>% filter(Award != "See details", Award != "20000 10000 and 5000", is.na(Award) != TRUE) 
#remove missing application fields  
df5 <- df4 %>% filter(is.na(Social.Innovation) != TRUE & is.na(Business...Entrepreneurship) != TRUE & is.na(Chemistry) 
!= TRUE & is.na(Computer.Info..Technology) != TRUE & is.na(Math.Statistics) != TRUE & is.na(Food.Agriculture) != 
TRUE & is.na(Life.Sciences) != TRUE & is.na(Physical.Sciences) != TRUE & is.na(Engineering.Design) != TRUE & 
is.na(Environment) != TRUE) 
#make the award an integer 
df3$Award <- strtoi(df3$Award) 
df5$Award <- strtoi(df5$Award) 
#number of contests in each  
showcases <- c(12,86,15,1,4,4,9,7,92,29,2,14,13,4,18,19,1,11,13,6,4,5,3)

df3 <- transform( 
  df3, Data = ifelse(Business...Entrepreneurship == 1  
                     | Math.Statistics == 1 
                     , 1, 0))

  df3, Social = ifelse(Emerging.Economies.Showcase == 1 | Habitat.for.Humanity.Showcase == 1 
                       | World.Vision.Showcase == 1 | Civil.Society.Showcase == 1 
                       | COVID.19.Showcase == 1, 1, 0)) 
df3 <- transform( 
  df3, BIO = ifelse(Chemistry == 1  
                    | Food.Agriculture == 1 
                    , 1, 0)) 
df3 <- transform( 
  df3, ENG = ifelse(Engineering.Design == 1  
                    | Computer.Info..Technology == 1 
                    , 1, 0)) 
df3 <- transform( 
  df3, Aerospace = ifelse(Tec.Edge..Air.Force..Showcase == 1 | NASA.Showcase == 1 
                          , 1, 0)) 
df3 <- transform( 
  df3, Pharma = ifelse(Novartis.Showcase == 1 | Elanco.Showcase == 1 
                       , 1, 0))
