#Inferential analysis without grouping 
df4 <- df5  
############################ 
###Inferential Statistics### 
############################ 
 
####new variables####  
#award 
df4$Award_sq <- df4$Award^2 
df4$Award_ln <- log(df4$Award) 
#time 
df4 <- transform( 
  df4, NEW = ifelse(is.na(df4$Abstract) == TRUE, 1, 0)) 
df4 <- transform( 
  df4, TIME = ifelse(is.na(df4$Time) == TRUE, NA, -1*df4$Time)) 
df4 <- transform( 
  df4, Solvers = ifelse(df4$Solvers >= 3000, NA,df4$Solvers)) 
 
#Lengths  
df4$Title.length_sq <- df4$Title.length^2 
df4$Title.length_ln <- log(df4$Title.length) 
df4$Full.text.length_sq <- df4$Full.text.length^2 
df4$Full.text.length_ln <- log(df4$Full.text.length) 
 
#Interactions 
df4$I_AwardxText <- df4$Award_ln*sqrt(df4$Full.text.length) 
df4$I_AwardxBusiness <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Business...Entrepreneurship 
df4$I_AwardxChemistry <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Chemistry 
df4$I_AwardxMath <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Math.Statistics 
df4$I_AwardxFood <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Food.Agriculture 
df4$I_AwardxEng <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Engineering.Design 
df4$I_AwardxNovartis <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Novartis.Showcase 
df4$I_AwardxCivil <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Civil.Society.Showcase 
df4$I_AwardxEmerging <- df4$Award_ln*df4$Emerging.Economies.Showcase

  df4, NEW = ifelse(is.na(df4$Abstract) == TRUE, 1, 0))

  df4, TIME = ifelse(is.na(df4$Time) == TRUE, NA, -1*df4$Time))

  df4, Solvers = ifelse(df4$Solvers >= 3000, NA,df4$Solvers))

  df4, Data = ifelse(Business...Entrepreneurship == 1

                     , 1, 0))

  df4, ENG = ifelse(Engineering.Design == 1  
                       | Computer.Info..Technology == 1 
                    , 1, 0))

                   | Food.Agriculture == 1 
                   , 1, 0))

#Data Analysis Innocentive Seeker Known  
###########################

########################### 
####import packages#### 
library(psych)  
library(dplyr)  
library(stargazer) 
 
###Data set#### 
#raw data set 
d <- read.csv(file = "C:/Users/t_fud/OneDrive/Desktop/Master 
Thesis/Crowdsourcing/Data/Hacken/Innocentive_SK_10.04.csv", sep=";") 
#remove unnecessary data 
#exclude calls for partnerships and similar 
df3 <- d %>% filter(Ideation == 1 | Reduction.to.Practice == 1 | Theoretical...Licensing == 1 | Theoretical.IP.Transfer == 1) 
#remove missing award values 
df4 <- df3 %>% filter(Award != "See details", Award != "20000 10000 and 5000", is.na(Award) != TRUE) 
#remove missing application fields  
df5 <- df4 %>% filter(is.na(Social.Innovation) != TRUE & is.na(Business...Entrepreneurship) != TRUE & is.na(Chemistry) 
!= TRUE & is.na(Computer.Info..Technology) != TRUE & is.na(Math.Statistics) != TRUE & is.na(Food.Agriculture) != 
TRUE & is.na(Life.Sciences) != TRUE & is.na(Physical.Sciences) != TRUE & is.na(Engineering.Design) != TRUE & 
is.na(Environment) != TRUE) 
#make the award an integer 
df3$Award <- strtoi(df3$Award) 
df5$Award <- strtoi(df5$Award) 
df5 <- df5 %>% filter(Award < 500000 & Solvers < 3000) 
df3 <- df3 %>% filter(Solvers < 3000) 
df5 <- df5 %>% filter(Solvers <= 2000) 
==PAGE:206==
197 
 
df3 <- df3 %>% filter(Solvers <= 2000) 
df5 <- df5 %>% filter(Solvers <= 2000) 
df3 <- df3 %>% filter(Solvers <= 2000) 
 
#################### 
####Descriptive##### 
#################### 
 
####Seeker Type#### 
#subsets 
Fou <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Foundation") 
Fou_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Foundation") 
Bu <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Bureau ") 
Bu_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Bureau ") 
Wa <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Wazoku ") 
Wa_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Wazoku ") 
UM <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "US Military") 
UM_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "US Military") 
L <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Large") 
L_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Large") 
M <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Medium") 
M_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Medium") 
S <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Small ") 
S_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Small ") 
Co <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type == "Medium" | Seeker.Type == "Small " | Seeker.Type == "Large") 
Co_n <- df3 %>% filter(Seeker.Type != "Medium" & Seeker.Type != "Small " & Seeker.Type != "Large") 
df3 <- transform(df3, Foundation = ifelse(Seeker.Type == "Foundation", 1, 0))

1, 0))

df3 <- transform(df3, Medium = ifelse(Seeker.Type == "Medium", 1, 0)) 
df3 <- transform(df3, Large = ifelse(Seeker.Type == "Large", 1, 0))

t.test(Wa$Solvers, Wa_n$Solvers)$p.value 
t.test(UM$Solvers, UM_n$Solvers)$p.value 
t.test(Co$Solvers, Co_n$Solvers) #0.000 negative 
cor(df3$Foundation, df3$Solvers)

cor(df3$Small, df3$Solvers) 
cor(df3$Commercial, df3$Solvers)

t.test(ENG$Large, ENG0$Large) #No difference

10.6.2 Reverse Causality Check 
 
#import 
d3 <- read.csv(file = " HyveCrowd_Sol_Win_clean_27.02.csv.csv", sep=";") 
d3 <- d3 %>% filter(id == 197) 
d4 <- read.csv(file = "C:/Users/t_fud/OneDrive/Desktop/Master 
Thesis/Crowdsourcing/Data/Hacken/HyveCrowd_197_overview_24.03.csv", sep=";") 
 
#transform data 
d3 <- transform( 
  d3, winner = ifelse(place >= 1, 1, 0)) 
d4 <- transform( 
  d4, winner = ifelse(place >= 1, 1, 0)) 
d3$changeviews <- d4$view - d3$views  
d3$changelikes <- d4$likes - d3$likes  
d3$changecomments <- d4$comments - d3$comments 
d3$changenumeva <- d4$num_evaluations - d3$num_evaluations 
w3 <- d3 %>% filter(winner == 1) 
w4 <- d4 %>% filter(winner == 1) 
l3 <- d3 %>% filter(winner != 1) 
l4 <- d4 %>% filter(is.na(winner) == T) 
#test if it changed more for winning 
t.test(w3$changeviews,l3$changeviews) 
t.test(w3$changelikes,l3$changelikes) 
t.test(w3$changecomments,l3$changecomments) 
t.test(w3$changenumeva,l3$changenumeva) 
windows(800,600) 
boxplot(w3$views,w4$view, names=c("Winner","Loser"), cex.lab=1.5, cex.axis=1.5, cex.main=1.5, 
cex.sub=1.5, pch = 19) 
windows(800,600) 
boxplot(w3$changeviews,l3$changeviews, names=c("Winner Change","Loser Change"), cex.lab=1.5, 
cex.axis=1.5, cex.main=1.5, cex.sub=1.5, pch = 19) 
windows(800,600)

10.6.3 Inferential Statistics – Initial Model  
############### 
##Inferential## 
############### 
 
#Interaction terms and non-linearity  
d2$time_sq <- d2$time*d2$time 
d2$time_ln <- log((d2$time+1)) 
 
d2$I_eva.likes <- d2$eva_weigth*d2$likes 
d2$I_eva.comments <- d2$eva_weigth*d2$comments 
d2$I_eva.time <- d2$eva_weigth*d2$time 
d2$I_eva.time_sq <- d2$eva_weigth*d2$time_sq 
 
#Models 
winning1.1 <- glm(formula = winner ~  
                    win_percentage + num_winning 
                  , family = binomial,data = d2) 
summary(winning1.1) #AIC: 1735.3 
 
winning1.2 <- glm(formula = winner ~  
                    win_percentage + num_winning 
                  + eva_weigth 
                  + meva_weight 
                  , family = binomial,data = d2) 
summary(winning1.2) #AIC: 1695.9 
 
winning1.3 <- glm(formula = winner ~  
                    win_percentage + num_winning 
                  + eva_weigth 
                  + likes 
                  + comments 
                  + mlikes 
                  + xcomments 
                  , family = binomial,data = d2) 
summary(winning1.3) #AIC: 1603 
 
winning1.4 <- glm(formula = winner ~  
                    win_percentage + num_winning 
                  + likes 
                  + comments 
                  + eva_weigth 
                  + mlikes 
                  + xcomments 
                  + I_eva.likes 
                  , family = binomial,data = d2) 
summary(winning1.4) #AIC: 1599.1 
 
stargazer(winning1.1, winning1.2, winning1.3, winning1.4,star.cutoffs = c(0.05, 0.01, 0.001), type = "html", 
out="C:/Users/t_fud/OneDrive/Desktop/Master Thesis/Crowdsourcing/Data/Hacken/winning_07.04.html")

10.6.5 Inferential Statistics – Second Extended Model  
#transforming contests without an evaluation  
d$mevaluation <- with(d, ave(evaluation, id, FUN=function(x) mean(x, na.rm=TRUE))) 
d$mnum_evaluation <- with(d, ave(num_evaluations, id, FUN=function(x) mean(x, na.rm=TRUE)))

  d, num_evaluations = ifelse(is.na(evaluation) == T, mnum_evaluation, num_evaluations))

d$mnum_evaluations2 <- mean(d$num_evaluations, na.rm = TRUE)

  d, num_evaluations = ifelse(is.na(evaluation) != T & num_evaluations == 0, mnum_evaluations2, 
num_evaluations)) 
10.6.6 Inferential Statistics – Third Extended Model  
#transforming contests without an evaluation  
d$mevaluation <- with(d, ave(evaluation, id, FUN=function(x) mean(x, na.rm=TRUE))) 
d$mnum_evaluation <- with(d, ave(num_evaluations, id, FUN=function(x) mean(x, na.rm=TRUE))) 
d <- transform( 
  d, num_evaluations = ifelse(is.na(evaluation) == T, mnum_evaluation, num_evaluations)) 
d <- transform( 
  d, evaluation = ifelse(is.na(evaluation) == T, mevaluation, evaluation)) 
d$mnum_evaluations2 <- mean(d$num_evaluations, na.rm = TRUE) 
d <- transform( 
  d, num_evaluations = ifelse(is.na(evaluation) != T & num_evaluations == 0, mnum_evaluations2, 
num_evaluations)) 
d$mevaluation2 <- mean(d$num_evaluations, na.rm = TRUE) 
d <- transform( 
  d, num_evaluations = ifelse(num_evaluations == 0, mnum_evaluations2, num_evaluations)) 
d$evaluation[is.na(d$evaluation)] <- mean(d$evaluation, na.rm = TRUE) 
d <- transform( 
  d, mevaluation = ifelse(is.na(mevaluation) == T, evaluation, mevaluation))

10.7 Interview HYVECrowd responsible (I1)  
09.05.2023, 14:15, 45 MINS, Interviewee: HYVECrowd Responsible, Setting: Microsoft Teams Meeting 
1. [Casual Exchange and Introduction] 
2. I: As part of my master's thesis, I dealt with various questions and I'll share a few slides. 
3. [Sharing the slides. They can be found in Appendix 10] 
4. I: The first was which fields of application are best for companies. The second is how to attract more 
participants. The third is similar to the paper you shared. However, not based on artificial intelligence, but on the 
basis of the evaluations made by other participants. (Bell et al. (2023), AN). I have investigated this on both 
HYVECrowd and InnoCentive. I obtained the data and then did a multiple linear regression with the number of 
participants as a dependent variable and the field of application, innovation phases, and various other variables 
as independent variables. Then, for the evaluation, as I just said, I obtained the evaluation by the other participants 
and the likes and views and so on. Then, I performed a logistic regression. So, practically like the paper you sent 
me, to predict which entries win. 
5. HYVECrowd Responsible: So, you probably only have the data that is freely accessible on the platform. 
6. I: Yes. 
7. HYVECrowd Responsible: I think, what comes out of the paper… I honestly haven't read it. I've been involved 
in this whole process for too long. On the one hand, there is the evaluation of experts and the evaluations of users, 
which are not distinguished on the platform. 
8. I: Exactly, I only have the user rating.

expert evaluation is included, but I don't think it is. Only the user rating, i.e. the expert rating, is not visible on the

10. I: Yes, and I also tested on one contest where the expert evaluation was not even finished when I obtained the 
data. 
11. HYVECrowd Responsible: Okay. 
12. I: Maybe you could tell me what you do? 
13. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, exactly, I'd love to, I also studied business administration. Did both my 
bachelor's thesis and my master's thesis in innovation management. Exactly on the subject of crowds and I have 
been working in this area for almost 12 years. I once started an internship here, where I still am now. I worked in 
different areas, then got stuck in the crowdsourcing area, and then took over team leadership after graduation for 
the whole area. I actually bear responsibility in the entire area of crowdsourcing. The whole area has been further 
developed from actually a service that we offered. We have built up a separate platform, our own community for 
each individual customer, towards a solution that is now the HYVECrowd, in which we... Yes, actually offer 
service, so to speak, this community and the platform. In other words, Crowdsourcing as a Service. Accordingly, 
I have already done a lot of research in this direction. I think I wrote 5, or 6 papers somehow in this whole crowd 
direction, rather qualitatively. Bachelor's and Master's thesis. I'm also currently writing a Ph.D. in the field of 
crowdsourcing. But now approach it a little broader and more general. Innovation and technology, and how 
technology innovation changes and innovation management. Exactly, that's one topic, and on the other hand, I'm 
somehow driven by all sorts of other things. Operations, which means that I also manage processes that arise in a 
semi-innovation consultancy. Innovation consulting, which first of all provides for the identification of trends and 
derived strategies. What will happen in 5 years, what will happen in 10 years Derived from this, innovation road 
maps also support innovation projects. The identification of customer needs or existing technologies and areas of 
application. Then, we prototype the whole thing and test it. These are typical tasks. Exactly, and I'm actually 
responsible for all processes, i.e. project management, but also marketing sales. Everything that has to do with it. 
That's exactly what I have a pretty good overview and now a few things to tell. 
==PAGE:223==
214 
 
14. I: That sounds good, I have an immediate question: If you support companies with crowdsourcing, do they 
come to you and say we want to do it or do they come to you and say, for example, we want to change or improve 
our innovation processes and you suggest that crowdsourcing could be interesting. 
15. HYVECrowd Responsible: In the meantime, I want to say rather the latter. Historically, however, it has always 
been the case that companies have approached us and said that they wanted to do crowdsourcing. Then it was very 
stand-alone. Afterward, it was also felt that nothing happened to the output. In the meantime, it is actually the case 
that we see crowdsourcing as a tool that can be used for certain questions of our customers. This means that we 
have also moved away from selling crowdsourcing, but we sell product innovations. And if it's a useful tool to 
develop product innovations, then we use the crowd. 
16. I: So, the actual goal is to innovate a product? I had the feeling that some companies are just crowdsourcing 
to maybe achieve secondary goals, to be perceived as more open, or something like that. 
17. HYVECrowd Responsible: So, there are, and that is certain, side effects. Especially with the companies that 
send us inquiries in the sense of we would like to do that. I think secondary effects play a big role here. But when 
we use it in our projects, we try more and more to really aim for the primary results. That's also my personal goal. 
Ultimately, we want to make them usable for the customer. This means that even if the customer is interested in 
it, but hasn't thought about what to do with the ideas afterward, then it's somehow all a bit pointless, I would almost 
say. So, then we have a hard time because, in the end, it has no impact. You won't get a product on the market with 
it. It's still a long journey after a competition, but if you have some processes from the beginning and you've 
thought about them, then I think the chances are much greater. 
18. I: And how do you assess in advance, if crowdsourcing is a useful approach? 
19. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, I think that's where we get a little bit closer to your first question, probably. 
20. I: Yes. 
21. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, I'll put it this way: if the customer really wants to do it anyway and says that 
he just wants to generate ideas from a broad group of people because he doesn't have a solution himself yet. Then, 
we say that we can do it with pleasure but another method might fit better. What I think works quite well is to find 
applications for existing or new technologies. Meaning, the customer approaches us after developing a technology, 
which is either already included in existing products or which is not used, yet, and wants us to help search for 
possible applications. Then, we can use, among others, the crowd to identify use cases. After that we evaluate the 
proposed use cases. Which ones are useful? Which are doable? Which have a business case? So, we perform a 
quite typical validation. But, most importantly, does the technology solve the problem proposed by the crowd? We 
evaluate it internally and afterward, with our customers. Then, we take 2 to 5 use cases and perform a second 
validation by interviewing industry experts. We try to find out if this idea has some potential and use for the market. 
Ideally, we keep working on it to a prototyping stage and the handover. We are not handling the implementation. 
That’s where our customers, that are after all mostly from the industry, are mostly very skilled.   
22. I: Well, let`s get to the findings from my thesis. We can, at first, talk about the fixed factors. That would be the 
second point here (referring to the Slide 4 (S4), AN). Contests that are held in the ideation phase attract more 
solvers than contests in other innovation phases.  
23. HYVECrowd Responsible: Which are the other phases? If it isn`t ideation? 
24. I: They are mostly regarding licensing, MVP, and reduction to practice.  
25. HYVECrowd Responsible: Okay, well, that`s good. I think that it is quite logical, as they have the lowest 
barrier to entry. For licensing, you need to have developed a full product or have a patent on something. In 
principle, everyone can contribute to the ideation phase. I would endorse that statement.  
26. I: The application fields, especially Math & Statistics, and Business and Entrepreneurship attract substantially 
more solvers. I also observed a negatively moderating effect of identity disclosure on them. I consider this a 
compelling finding.  
27. HYVECrowd Responsible: Is the effect reversed after disclosing the identity?  
28. I: If they disclose their identity, they have an approximately similar number of solvers as other contests. I 
would like to discuss the identity disclosure with you, as HYVECrowd contests are mostly open.  
==PAGE:224==
215 
 
29. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, I think, there are 2 or 3 contests in which we did not disclose their identity. I 
read some research by Linus Dahlander about it. However, I forgot the name of the paper and could not find it, 
yet. He also discovered that anonymous contests attract fewer solvers. The argument was, I think, that the solvers 
could assess worse what the seeker wants. Thus, they assess that their own chances of winning are worse. So, your 
findings on identity disclosure in general support the existing literature. But I find it interesting that you found a 
different effect for math and entrepreneurship. Maybe it does not matter for whom you solve a mathematical 
problem. That would be my guess. However, for business and entrepreneurship, it is hard to explain. I don`t have 
an explanation for that.  
30. I: We see a similar effect in Engineering. However, I observed a compelling difference between InnoCentive 
and HYVECrowd. On InnoCentive, Engineering contests attract more solvers than other application fields. 
However, on HYVECrowd, they attract less. But they attract more solutions. This is also interesting from the 
perspective: What should a seeker maximize? The number of solutions or the number of solvers. For example, an 
increase in solvers is related to an increase in heterogeneity and creativity. Probably more so than an increase in 
solutions.  
31. HYVECrowd Responsible: Exciting, yes. Yes, absolutely. I think that this is an actual problem that we face 
on our platform. We get many solutions from only a few solvers. We tried limiting the number of solutions per 
solver to a contest. However, we received mixed feedback from the community. The solvers, that usually 
contribute 20 ideas, were obviously limited to 10 or 5 contributions. We hoped that we could improve the quality 
that way. But we dropped that idea, among others, because a lot of ideas leave a better impression on the customer. 
In general, I think that quality over quantity is probably better. 5 thought-through ideas are better than 20 
undeveloped ones.  
32. I: So, you would also prefer maximizing the number of solvers rather than the number of solutions?  
33. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, exactly. I would prefer more solvers with fewer solutions over fewer solvers 
with a higher overall number of solutions.  
34. I: Okay. There are more application fields that attract more or fewer solvers than others. I observed repeatedly 
that social goals were related to more solvers. Which underlines the importance of internal motivation.  
35. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, I think this is also true for our platform. We had for example two cases 
considering neurodermatitis. But, pharmaceutical contests seem to attract fewer solvers (referring to the slides).  
36. I: Yes, however, they overlap and are not mutually exclusive.  
37. HYVECrowd Responsible: I compared two contests in my bachelor thesis and found that there are many 
different factors that you can`t know at this point. As an insider, I can consider these factors. In the end, it is about 
the size of the data set and how much these factors level each other. However, in the example of the Sanofi case, 
we approached the topic differently. The award structure is also completely different. No award, but funding, 
which is higher. You had to be an NPO. On the other hand, we had way more marketing budget compared to other 
contests. That makes it quite complex for regression because there are so many variables. Anyways, that is only 
from the inside perspective.  
38. I: I excluded some contests that had only a few solvers, as I did not consider them similar to other contests. 
They were not individual contestants but, as you said, NPOs or similar. They participate and receive funding, 
which is a different structure than the innovation contests I aimed at.  
39. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, exactly. Absolutely. 
40. I: Under the topic of identity disclosure, I also found that small and medium-sized commercial companies 
might not profit from identity disclosure. However, big companies and especially foundations, which include again 
the social aspect, profit the most. For big companies, I had the theory that reach and status can be reached better 
with bigger companies. This was my main argument. So, basically, the solvers can adorn themselves with their 
contributions to the problems of major companies.  
41. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, I think that the brand attracts many people and thus, more solvers. However, 
bigger companies also have bigger legal departments and more big discussions about intellectual property. But, in 
the end, they simply profit from their strong brand. We also see that some solvers request a certificate that they 
==PAGE:225==
216 
 
contributed or similar. Basically to prove that they achieved a specific place in a particular contest. They also adorn 
themselves with that on social media and similar.    
42. I: Let`s talk about the influence of the text length (Referring to S4, AN) 
43. HYVECrowd Responsible: Wait a second, the relationship between the text length and the number of solvers 
is negative. Does text length mean overview? Ok, this is interesting, isn`t it? 
44. I: Yes, exactly. For me, this negative effect is not surprising. I would not want to fight through a long text 
before contributing, would you?    
45. HYVECrowd Responsible: However, the fewer solvers that put in the effort to read the text might contribute 
more afterward.   
46. I: Yes, and they are probably quite committed to the task and put in the effort also in finding a good solution. 
Then again, what is better? In the end, the quality of the contributions matters, which I sadly could not control.  
47. HYVECrowd Responsible: How is it on Innocentive? We have this very open approach, with open

InnoCentive, isn`t it?  
48. I: Exactly, that is, in general, not the case. 
49. HYVECrowd Responsible: Is it public, how many contributions were made? 
50. I: No one can actually only see the number of solvers.  
51. HYVECrowd Responsible: Ok. Because this is something I thought about multiple times. It has probably an 
effect on potential contributors if it is public how many contributions were made, which is true for our platform. 
There is probably a declining effect at some point, if like 150 ideas were contributed. The winning percentage for 
a solver obviously decreases compared to like 10 ideas.   
52. I: This is another argument to decrease the number of solutions per solver. Anyways, let`s discuss the award. 
(referring to the S3, AN) The influence of the award size is logarithmic. Which was already found in prior research. 
The award size is especially relevant in mathematics, statistics, business, and entrepreneurship. It is less relevant 
in social contests. Again, probably a result of internal vs. external motivation. Finally, monetary awards are 
preferred. You have compared to InnoCentive also non-monetary awards.  
53. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, I can fully support that. I am for monetary awards only. However, there are 
exceptions. Recently, we had a very good contest with the University of Innsbruck. Normally, it is just a pain for 
everyone. The solvers that are somewhere in the world and get an award they can`t use, but also the seekers that 
have to coordinate the things or trips. Overall, I 100% support monetary awards.   
54. I: Do you know the main reasoning behind non-monetary awards? Is it just cheaper or are there other reasons?  
55. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, mostly because it`s cheaper. I think so. Also, it is easier in specific set-ups 
e.g., sponsoring in universities. However, I would tendentially always prefer cash.  
56. I: Next, we can talk about the findings of the user evaluation. I used the number of comments and likes, and 
the solver evaluation. I also controlled for average evaluation in a contest and so on. I tested the model in the last 
contest. I could exclude 50% of the contributions while maintaining 90% of the ones that were chosen by the jury, 
which was 9 out of ten. It is somewhat equally effective than the model based on AI, which you send me. However, 
as I used a different evaluation method, we should not compare them directly.  
57. HYVECrowd Responsible: Mhm, yes. 
58. I: I think it is quite promising. Also, I could not even include the number of views, as I could not obtain them 
before the evaluation and they would have suffered from severe reverse causality. You, as a platform could add 
them to the model.  
59. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, but one should also question how much of this is related to a selection bias. 
Let`s take the number of comments. Some of them are from HYVE community management or from experts. They 
tendentially comment on the contributions that have a high quality. Thus, the model includes at least to some 
==PAGE:226==
217 
 
degree a human pre-selection. Further, if we comment on good solutions, we often send them also to the jury. 
Thus, they are more likely to also write a comment on these solutions. There is most definitely some sort of 
selection bias in the data. However, in the end, the results look good, regardless.  
60. I: Ok, yes. The strongest predictor was, by the way, the number of likes. It was even better than the evaluation. 
This is likely a result of a lack of evaluations. One could think about making evaluations mandatory to successfully 
implement such models. Is that realistic?  
61. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, generally conceivable, I would say. I am always skeptical when it comes to 
user evaluation. It is quite interesting that you achieved a good prediction value with them. However, 
(HYVECrowd Responsible sights, AN) the users are partially not that nice on such platforms. There is this 
tendency that one evaluates purposefully negatively. And yes, I think the biggest problem is that we don`t have 
enough evaluations. Thus, they don`t have high validity. So, because of that, yes.  
62. I: I can imagine. Increasing one`s chances by giving bad evaluations etc. However, I assume that it does not 
matter if one can obtain a critical mass of evaluations.   
63. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, exactly. We would need way more evaluations and because we don`t have 
them, we mostly disregard them. Definitely in most cases.    
64. I: Yes, I also realized that if only a few solvers evaluated a post, the evaluation was often quite bad. Bad in the 
sense of accuracy but also as an evaluation value. Often only a few stars. But if they had multiple evaluations they 
had more. This is likely also related to the fact that better contributions get more evaluations, as they have more 
likes, better evaluations, etc. This is a reinforcing effect. To summarize, there is potential in the user evaluation, 
but the implementation is challenging. Did I understand that correctly?   
65. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, this is a good summary. 
66. I: Great. Next, I developed some kind of framework to help companies to decide if crowdsourcing is a valid 
alternative. (Referring to the S7, AN) This is obviously quite simplified and should be only understood as an 
extension to existing frameworks. In general, are such frameworks useful to improve your decision processes 
regarding the potential of crowdsourcing in a specific case?  
67. HYVECrowd Responsible: Nowadays, I would say it is a lot of gut feeling if the topic is fitting or not and what 
we think should be the end result. Yes, if a customer wants to do crowdsourcing at any cost, we will not tell them 
completely out of it, but we might advise them to do something else. Then, that is rather, for example, identity 
disclosure. We would always advise our customers to disclose their identity. Yes, that is rather the thing that can 
be influenced, and partly even the application field. However, for me, the differences in technology are most 
important. Do we have a push or pull market? So, do we start with an existing customer need and develop fitting 
ideas for an existing problem? Or, do we approach it from the technology side and search for potential applications? 
For us, the application topic is the most relevant. Meaning, we see the most potential in that, because one can really 
profit from the heterogeneity of the crowd, as they come from totally different fields and you get different ideas. 
That way, one achieves a variety that is probably not achievable with different processes. I would say that is a 
very, very exciting subject area from which we developed a new offering. We communicate with and search for 
customers that have new technologies or use these technologies somewhere but might want to also apply them 
differently. We build a broad project around these technologies. We use the crowd, make interviews, and different 
validations. In the end, we take a handful of such use cases and explore them deeper or start talking about them 
with our customers to implicate them. Or, we propose to sell the technology to like 5 companies or similar. So, 
this is, at the moment, one main topic, which we can address the best with the HYVECrowd. Even though you 
said prior that engineering is in general working. This is more true for InnoCentive. This works quite well over 
there. We struggle with these topics. You know what I mean? We most definitely have similarities to InnoCentive, 
but they have obviously simply more participants and a bigger community. They are also specialized in such 
challenges in the chemical area and we don`t even need to consider that. 
68. I: For the topic of the difference between platforms, I have two findings here. So, InnoCentive`s number of 
solvers decreased over the time of the data set. At the same time, the relevance of the award size lost some of its 
relevance over time, which is also interesting. But, at first, we should talk about the number of solvers, as the trend 
was positive on HYVECrowd. 
==PAGE:227==
218 
 
69. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, one could say that our community is growing somewhat because no one 
leaves. If we look at the number of active solvers, I don`t know, I would have to look it up. We say that 
HYVECrowd maybe grows or rather stagnates but not really shrinks. If we make a brutally honest comparison to 
the time when we build platforms for each customer, with their own branding, and with big marketing campaigns 
5 years ago, we would need to acknowledge the participation would be higher even today. Back then, we had 
totally different numbers of participation. Once we had a contest approximately 12 years ago with 12000 
participants. We don`t even have that many people registered on HYVECrowd. It was also a little bit different 
from the setup. However, I generally think that the crowdsourcing topic and its hype diminished a bit. Thus, there 
are fewer participants nowadays. However, the relevance of the award size decreases. Do I interpret that correctly? 
(Referring to the slides, AN) 
70. I: Yes. 
71. HYVECrowd Responsible: I think, communities developed that maintained or developed high intrinsic 
motivation. The solvers that contributed for quite some time, are probably a stable main community. That`s what 
we also observe at HYVECrowd. So, 20 or 30 solvers regularly participate in contests. I could imagine that it is 
the same for InnoCentive. I can imagine that they just participate regularly and maybe the award size is not the 
deciding factor anymore.  
72. I: Yes, and at the same time, I thought that these solvers gain experience and get better at winning. These 
solvers win regularly and it becomes increasingly hard and less interesting for outsiders to compete. Then, higher 
awards don`t matter that much, as the winning chance is very small anyways. Also, on InnoCentive, a small group 
of solvers wins regularly.  
73. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, exactly. I mean, at HYVECrowd it is the same. Even though we sometimes 
have new winners, they are mostly the same ones. That`s also why I`d like to limit the number of submissions per 
solver. They also profit highly from the combination of quantity and quality.  Contributing early, take a lot of 
topics, and the chances of winning are high. It is not really ideal.  
74. I: Let`s talk shortly about the topic of AI. I also looked at AI a bit, similar to the paper you send me (Bell et al. 
(2023), AN), I evaluated contributions with AI. Further, I asked ChatGPT (https://chat.openai.com/, AN) to 
develop solutions for the last contest on HYVECrowd. In the end, I observed very similar solutions from other 
solvers. It does not mean that they used it, even though I also read such accusations in some comments, but it 
raises the following question: To what extent does Crowdsourcing still make sense? 
75. HYVECrowd Responsible: Absolutely. We have an ongoing research project on that topic. We found some 
contributions that were developed by ChatGPT. But, we also knowingly contributed ideas developed by ChatGPT 
anonymously to compare their performance to other ideas. Meaning, we did not inform our customers that we 
contributed these ideas and looked at their evaluations. We contributed around 10 ideas to 5 or 6 contests with 
approximately 150 contributions each. I think, only one contribution developed by AI won or maximum one per 
contest. So, it is not really a lot.   
76. I: Apparently not yet. I could imagine that companies should use AI before starting crowdsourcing. Simply to 
include or exclude such ideas prior. It is, after all an aspect of fairness, which is very important in these contests.  
77. HYVECrowd Responsible: Exactly. I think, what we have to do is, first, develop rules. At the moment, it is 
quite easy to find the contributions developed by ChatGPT. However, it should not be allowed to copy and paste 
ideas. On the other hand, there could be good ideas developed from it. The question is, how much did you develop 
the idea yourself because it can be a good inspiration. It`s a similar hybrid to chess. A computer is better than a 
human. But a human and a computer might be better than both humans and computers. That is, I think, the really 
interesting thing. Thus, absolutely, yes.   
78. I: I had the feeling that I found a lot of similarities between ChatGPT`s answers and the ones in the contests. 
Coming back to the question: To what extent does Crowdsourcing still make sense? 
79. HYVECrowd Responsible: Yes, a big problem is, of course, the evaluation. If one puts a lot of effort into the 
evaluation. Yes, quite difficult to say. Automating the evaluation would ease this problem. On the other hand, 
there can be really good ideas from it. At the moment, I`d say that the current solutions from AI are not good. 
However, it can become a real problem, even though, in the end, there is still a big gap. Creative thinking, finding 
hundreds of applications, and abstracting on different levels. Especially abstraction is still a challenge. But it could 
==PAGE:228==
219 
 
be completely different in 2 years or even in half a year. Then, one has a problem. It`s funny, as we did 5-7 years 
ago a SWOT analysis and evaluated the threats to crowdsourcing. We said, more or less jokingly AI, because it 
was not even close. But nowadays one needs to think about it. Crowdsourcing could lose again a lot of relevance 
and then, the secondary effects might become more interesting again. Marketing, branding, and the exchange with 
the community. It will also become more interesting for the participants, as they can build a reputation or similar. 
I am not sure. I mean, there are crowdsourcing platforms already that focus on recruiting.

10.8 Interview CEO and SIM at OeNPAY (I2)  
31.05.2023, 15:30, 45 MINS, Interviewees: CEO and Senior Innovation Manager (SIM) OeNPAY, Setting: 
Microsoft Teams Meeting 
1. [Casual Exchange and Introduction] 
2. I: Could you please introduce yourself and how you were involved in the addressed crowdsourcing contest? 
3. CEO: My name is Franz and I worked for over 30 years in banking in Austria and Europe. I was called to the 
National Bank to fix a problem. I am dealing with the big questions around payment transactions. The two big 
questions we are dealing with are: First, the identification in payment transactions (among others, money 
laundering, sanctions, and control). Second, the digitalization of payment transactions (e.g., Crypto). Thus, the big 
and slow transactions and especially the fast ones. We help the national bank understand payment transactions. 
Who does transactions? What is transferred? And, what are the potential problems? 
4. SIM: I studied Computer Science and IT Management. I am SIM at OeNPAY for one and a half years. I am 
responsible for the electronic identity. It is a basis to make payment transactions more innovative because this 
identification process is the basis for each transaction and many topics around simplifying and securing payments. 
One of my first assignments at OeNPAY was this crowdsourcing contest. We searched for ideas, not money. It 
was quite a challenge at the beginning. Especially, the set-up. What exactly do we want? How do we transfer that 
on the platform and attract enough contributions? Our second CEO said that 20 contributions are satisfying. In the 
end, we had 156. There was a moment where I maybe thought: Please, not so many! Because I had to read and 
evaluate them all. From that perspective, it was a huge success. Also, to generate attention for the topic. Through 
the contest, we found the topic of identity in the metaverse and other virtual rooms. Which became a priority for 
us this year. It was influenced by the winning idea.  
5. CEO: Yes, it was the winning idea. However, we had three ideas worthy of winning. We might change the 
number of winning ideas in the future. It was not our first contest. I am a big fan of it. Especially in our field of 
banking. We have a lot of solutions. However, we don`t have corresponding problems. The question is always: 
Why would I need it? We can pay already without the solutions. Thus, the challenge is to find use cases.  
6. SIM: On the technical side, a lot of stuff exists. There are good examples. For example, we work with the EU 
on technical implementations. However, we don`t even know yet for what we would need them. Where is the 
problem? What can we solve?  
7. [Sharing the slides. They can be found in Appendix 10] 
8. I: I will share slides now and introduce you to what I did and follow up with some questions. I investigated the 
research questions, which you can see here (referring to S1, AN). First, in which application fields and 
circumstances do seekers generate the most advantages from crowdsourcing? Second, which methods could help 
==PAGE:229==
220 
 
seekers address the solvers` intrinsic and extrinsic motivations? Finally, how can user evaluations be used to 
improve the evaluation? You just mentioned that evaluating 160 contributions was challenging. Here I quickly 
summarized the statistical approach (referring to S2, AN). It might not be of direct interest, but it should be noted. 
I investigated two crowdsourcing platforms. I performed a multiple linear regression with the number of solvers 
as the dependent variable. Thus, I investigated how seekers can improve the number of solvers based on the 
application field, anonymity, innovation phase, and more. For the third question, I investigated the contributions 
on HYVECrowd and build a model to predict which would win. Based on the solvers’ evaluation, number of likes, 
and number of comments.   
9. SIM: Very nice. That way you can quantify it.   
10. I: Yes, exactly, thank you! The first result I`d like to discuss with you is the award size (referring to S3, AN). 
The relationship between the award size and the number of solvers is logarithmic. This has already been shown 
in other papers. I further found that the award size is especially relevant for the areas of Math & Statistics and 
Business & Entrepreneurship. However, they are less relevant in Social areas. Finally, monetary awards are 
always preferred. Leading to the first question (referring to the slides, AN): Why did you only award one winner?   
11. SIM: We discussed this topic for quite some time. I think we went back and forth for at least two months. At 
first, we wanted to award money to the winner and smartphones/VR glasses to the runner-ups. Then, we wanted 
to split the money. Finally, we chose one big award to increase the quality of the contributions. The winner gets a 
lot of money; however, they have to sit down and work hard on their contributions. We wanted more sophisticated 
use cases. We partly achieved that goal. We saw that solvers contributed 10 ideas with only a few sentences in 
other contests with more awards. We set one big award to force the solvers to put more effort into their 
contributions.  
12. CEO: Afterwards, we think that we should have a top 3. The top solutions were very close to each other. Thus, 
we should probably award a top 3 next time.   
13. SIM: Yes, it might have been better. It was very close. Thus, we also have two honorable mentions. In the end, 
we found out that it is quite hard to decide.  
14.CEO: Coming to the award size (referring to the slides, AN). We could have afforded more. However, also not 
extremely more. In the end, we are a non-profit organization and not Microsoft or similar. Judging based on the 
number of attracted contributions, we think that we weren´t far off with our award size.   
15. I: You mentioned that there were more ideas that you wanted to pursue and also mentioned in the contest 
description that you would like to work with the solvers of good contributions on more use cases. Was that only 
addressed to the winning idea or did you also move forward with the runner-ups? 
16. SIM: We talked multiple times with the winner of the contest. We are even planning a follow-up contest based 
on that contribution and talking with partners to implement it and bring it to market soon. Further, we want to do 
a Hackathon. Among the runner-ups, there was one idea that we liked to pursue. However, a contest to follow up 
on that idea was delayed, because our partner was involved in a different project. We also work on other projects 
with many EU states based on ideas from the contest. This is already in the project implementation phase and will 
be publicized soon.    
17. I: Did you discuss how you would handle the ideas that did not win if you used them? Would they be awarded 
and how? What are the rules for that? 
18. SIM: There are legal rules for it. It is not possible to get patents (or similar) for ideas. We look at that for 
exactly the reason you mentioned.   
19. I: This is good to know. Is that because it is so early in the process? 
20. CEO and SIM: Yes.  
21. I: The text length had a negative relationship to the number of solvers. If seekers used long descriptions, the 
number of solvers decreased. Probably because they did not want to spend their time reading. Did you consider 
that?  
22. SIM: Yes, we thought about that. That`s why we used so many different pages. Thus, we split the text to have 
small, on-point texts. 
==PAGE:230==
221 
 
23. I: Ideation phase contests attracted more solvers. Your contest was in that phase, which makes sense. However, 
now you want to do follow-up contests. Are they going further in the innovation process? More in the direction of 
specific applications? 
24. SIM: The Metaverse contest will continue further. We want complete implementation and virtual rooms. Did 
we consider the text length (Referring to S4, AN)? Yes, totally. We sat on the text for months and shortened it. 
Then, the professionals from the crowd improved it. Further, we focused on splitting the text. What did we focus 
on while formulating the task (Referring to S4, AN)? That was the reason why it took so long to formulate the 
text. It should be understandable for everyone. We consulted a colleague for communications and tried to change 
the text from a more technical perspective to one that everyone could understand without a technical background 
to attract enough ideas. There should not be a barrier. It was a big concern for us. Even if solvers did not have any 
touchpoints with the topic, they should be able to contribute. I would say we achieved that goal, as we attracted so 
many contributions.      
25. I:  Seekers that were anonymously attracted fewer solvers. This is partly dependent on the application field. 
Small and middle-sized seeker companies might be better off staying anonymous, and big companies and 
foundations might profit from openness. Did you even consider staying anonymous? Or was that never an option?  
26. CEO: Yes, it was always clear to us that we want to do it openly. It is also marketing for us. 
27. I: Exactly these secondary goals are my next question. How much did marketing, branding, and so on influence 
the decision? 
28. SIM: It was a part of our project plan. What is the primary goal? How do we measure it and what are our 
secondary goals? They were also important, actually, very important. This external reach. A little bit was also the 
strengthening of our brand. When we started the competition, the company was one year old. It is also important 
to signal to our stakeholders that we are doing this. Another secondary goal was to position ourselves on the topic. 
We are a contact partner for electronic ID. Companies approached us after reading about the contest in the 
newspaper and wanted to discuss cooperation.  
29. I: Can you quantify that? How was the balance between the primary goal and the secondary goals? How 
important was the primary compared to the secondary goal?  
30. SIM: I´d say 60/40, is that correct [CEO] (AN)? So, it was important for us.  
31. CEO: I`d say yes. Yes, the secondary goals were important to us. 
32. SIM: However, the main focus was the contest. It was not only about branding. But even though I was not in 
the company at the point of the decision, I think the secondary goals were a reason to stay open.  
33. CEO: In my opinion, I don`t see a reason for how it could help us to stay anonymous.  
34. I: I came up with two reasons. First, if the company has a bad reputation. Second, if the company wants to 
protect secrets.  
35. SIM: Yes, sure. If for example the tobacco industry would participate in crowdsourcing, many solvers might 
not participate because of their principles.  
36. I: I wondered why you were listed in all application fields on the platform. This is highly unusual.  
37. CEO: Because payment is important for everything.  
38. SIM: For us, it was especially important that we receive use cases from different fields. For example, health, 
language science, and whatever. Fields that don`t have any touchpoints, at the moment. After all, it is about finding 
ideas for problems in fields that we did not think about, such as, for example, the health industry.   
39. I: One could say that your task fitted crowdsourcing very well. As you really tried to explore distant and 
different fields.  
40. CEO: Yes and we have the field of values, right? I realized this is a big topic for us. The market might be not 
equipped to address social challenges. Thus, we consciously addressed digitalization processes in social areas. For 
example, the social coin or sustainability topics. It is important to include all people. Otherwise, we can´t pursue 
that brand message.    
==PAGE:231==
222 
 
41. I: I investigated the difference between the number of solvers and the number of contributions as performance 
measures. You mentioned the number of contributions as an important KPI. However, if you receive 20 
contributions from the same person, they might be less creative.  
42. SIM: We definitely would not like 20 contributions by the same person.  
43. CEO: No, that would not be positive. But I think it is highly unlikely to receive that many ideas from one 
person. This could never work without a big loss in quality.  
44. I: I agree. On the other hand, you used the number of contributions as a KPI. The number of solvers might be 
more important. Even though you might receive fewer contributions they might be better.  
45. SIM: Yes, we saw that solvers contributed up to 5 times. There were two categories of solvers. They either 
contributed once or 4/5 times.  
46. CEO: And usually the ones with more contributions often contributed many superficial ones. 
47. SIM: Yes, sometimes you could see that they came up with two ideas and the rest were more or less recycled. 
As you mentioned the KPIs, another KPI for us was the number of solvers that signed in on the platform during 
our contest. We were happy with many new solvers, as this meant that our marketing worked and many people 
were addressed. We could see that it worked. So, this might be a KPI, the number of new users. That we could 
attract users to participate in a contest on a platform.  
48. I: The topic of the Evaluation: As I mentioned, I tried to predict the winning contributions. In the tested contest, 
I managed to exclude 50% of the contributions, while maintaining 90% of the winning ones. The contribution with 
the highest winning chance was also chosen by the jury. Further, one could even improve such models with more 
data.  
49. SIM: I realized that comments like “this was already suggested” or “this is similar to that” helped a lot, as they 
were right. We discussed that in the evaluation and it helped.  
50. I: How was this evaluation process? Were you really alone with the 160 contributions? I saw a long list of jury 
members. That`s another question. Why was the jury so big? 
51. SIM: First Question: I read everything on a weekend. It was a very long one. We wanted to do it fast and with 
the big jury, it was difficult to find a schedule anyways. I went through all of them and gave them points and 
comments. Then, we went through all with the two CEOs and kept around 26. We did not want to limit ourselves 
too much. Our jury was quite big, as we wanted to include all important stakeholders and be diverse. We wanted 
experts from different areas, e.g., technical, political, economic, and legal. We wanted solutions from many fields. 
Thus, we needed to be able to assess solutions from different fields. We had very important professionals in our 
jury and we did not want to waste their time and resources. Thus, we decreased the number beforehand. Then, we 
sent E-Mails to the jury members and presented our 26 favorites. We thought they were exciting, detailed, and 
innovative enough. The members should rank them and declare their favorites; however, they could also go 
through all contributions. Some did. This led to the fact that in the top 10 with the most jury points, which we all 
discussed together, 2 contributions emerged that weren`t in the top 26. Regarding the use of user evaluations 
(Referring to S6, AN): Yes, in general. I think there are better tools that the ones on the platform we used. Then, 
you get better user feedback. 
52. CEO: There are two aspects. The perspective of the user and that of the professional. They might clash at some 
point. However, one definitely needs to consider both.   
53. SIM: I think there are better platforms to profit from user feedback. Only likes are not enough. On some, you 
can let ideas compete against each other, and so on. However, one could imagine awarding the solution with the 
best user feedback. But something we are also looking into. This is what [CEO] (AN) just mentioned. Can it be 
implemented? There are great ideas out there, but they might be unrealistic in the implementation. For example, 
because they don`t comply with data security regulations. We actually received many of such.   
54. I: Coming back to my suggestion. One could use the model to exclude bad contributions and decrease workload 
substantially.  
55. SIM: It is a possibility. But honestly, I am unsure if one can be sure about that. In the end, we miss out on a 
good solution, because the users also did not look at each contribution and, thus, did not give them likes. Then, we 
==PAGE:232==
223 
 
would also not look at them, even though they might be interesting. I would not completely trust it. It is a good 
indicator of where we could find the best ones. However, I believe it was good to look at all the contributions.  
56. I: Finally, I developed an extension to existing frameworks to facilitate decision-making (referring to S7, AN). 
It includes the important influence factors discovered in the thesis. Seekers can use it to decide if crowdsourcing 
is a useful approach. For example, a seeker that is not in the ideation phase should consider, if they have a problem 
focus or strong secondary goals. And so on. The first question is: How was the decision-making in your case? 
How did you decide to do crowdsourcing instead of other approaches?  
57. SIM: I want to start with the last question (referring to S7, AN). Yes, such and similar frameworks would 
totally help us in decision-making, because I think that it was not easy to make that decision. Further, even though 
the decision was already made at the point that I joined, we went back and forth with many decisions afterward. 
Thus, frameworks would definitely help. What do you think [CEO] (AN)? I would think that a framework to map 
problems to appropriate tools would be very helpful for my work. How was the decision made? (Referring to S7, 
AN) This was before my time. Thus, [CEO] (AN) knows that better. But I think the goal was mainly to attract 
many solvers. 
58. CEO: Well, it`s been some time since then. The decision was hugely influenced by me, as I am a big supporter 
of accumulating ideas from crowds. Further, you can find very special use cases. For example, we did it with many 
students from 4 continents and received interesting inputs, as different countries have different problems. They 
might also have similar problems in trade or problems that could be fixed immediately. So, one can have a bit of 
a comparison. Further, people introduce ideas to our ecosystem that we might not have considered. One could also 
use different forms of openness in crowdsourcing it can be completely open or, in this case, it was semi-open.  
59. I: I agree. However, in the end, not every innovation process can be done best using crowdsourcing. You 
showed a clear preference for crowdsourcing in general and I would agree. But seekers should consider different 
options and assess if crowdsourcing is fitting in each case.  
60. CEO: This is true. Yes. I think that one needs to consider if many people independent of their field can 
contribute or if they need much information that they might not have to solve the task.  
61. I: Would you say that the contest was successful? 
62. CEO: Yes, until today it was surprisingly successful.  
63. I: Would you change anything in your next contest and if yes, what?  
64. CEO: As mentioned, we would award the top 3 contributions. What else? It depends on the tasks. But we 
would decrease the size of the jury.  
65. SIM: Yes, a little bit smaller. Further, we would maybe use a platform with more management tools regarding 
the evaluation. The platform had a management tool. However, it was not easy to use. Moreover, having more user 
evaluations. So that you could easily compare contributions on the platform and make a call to the solvers to 
evaluate the ideas and so on.   
66. [Interview Wrap-up]  
67. SIM: Could I get your thesis when it is done? I would be happy if you could send it to us because it might be 
input for us to figure out what we could do differently next time, as its results are, de facto, the success criteria for 
crowdsourcing.  
68. I: I hope it was interesting for you! 
69. CEO: Yes, interesting. 
70. SIM: Yes, thank you for the inputs that you discovered and I am looking forward to your thesis.  
71. [Farewell] 
10.9 Interview OIL at UNIQA (I3)  
05.06.2023, 16:00, 45 MINS, Interviewee: Open Innovation Lead (OIL) UNIQA, Setting: Microsoft Teams 
Meeting 
==PAGE:233==
224 
 
1. [Casual Exchange and Introduction] 
2. OIL: I am two years at UNIQA and I am responsible for the topic of open innovation. I have a marketing 
background and went quite fast in the areas of customer experience and innovation. My work revolves around 
human-centered design in general. It does not matter if it is related to innovation, process optimization, or the way 
of working to get there. Thus, I work in a more holistic field. Crowdsourcing itself is obviously very important in 
the open innovation area. I think the most valuable assets a company has are its humans. Thus, we aim for 
crowdsourcing internally and very importantly externally. Honestly, we don`t have much experience with it. I was 
responsible before for a platform of another company that sought ideas from customers. I have experience in that 
field and liked it very much. However, beyond that, I don`t have much experience and I am not the biggest fan of 
crowdsourcing in general. I think one should learn from the customers and designers should make the product.  
3. [Sharing slides, they can be found in Appendix 10] 
4. I: [Reading out loud S1-3] Finally, non-monetary rewards attract fewer solvers. Why did you choose non-
monetary rewards? 
5. OIL: Because of my master thesis. I think, one needs to always differentiate between quantity and quality. What 
I found and experienced is that a monetary reward might not lead to higher quality, but only quantity. We don`t 
want to attract all users, because we can get feedback from average users through market research. We want to 
attract the lead users. Even if one wants to receive innovative ideas from customers or other stakeholders, maybe 
even solvers that don`t know the company, the solvers need a level of expertise and depth. Seekers can achieve 
that by attracting solvers that can identify with the company or the industry. Thus, our award was a UNIQA 
experience, because we wanted solvers that deal with UNIQA.  
6. I: So, the reason for non-monetary awards was mostly the focus on lead users? Should seekers offer different 
awards if they have a different focus? 
7. OIL: Yes. However, I question a different focus. I am convinced that one can get superficial ideas internally or 
you would ask a random person on the street. It is unlikely that seekers receive very unique ideas. Thus, the focus 
on depth is important. The question is: Why does one crowdsourcing? Is it only branding and the goal is o have 
the highest reach to connect them with the brand and do marketing? Then, I follow different goals, but I am 
responsible for innovation. My goal is always to generate input that we did not consider before or to get a new 
perspective that we did not have internally. I need someone that knows the industry or the topic to achieve that. 
Thus, we focus on lead users.  
8. I: Why did you reward 4 contributions? 
9. OIL: Because we had 4 evaluation categories. We rewarded each to increase the chance of receiving something 
useful for each. Thus, we also did not award one as the best.  
10. I: [Reading out loud S4] You formulated a long task description and provided a lot of information. Did you 
consider the text length? 
11. OIL: We did not consider the text length. We formulated the task consciously with a lot of information to 
provide a basis to build on. However, it is again more in the Lead User’s direction. It was worth a try, but I would 
not have decided to do the crowdsourcing. It was chosen as part of a cooperation with a university. I hoped to 
increase the quality of contributions by providing data. They should be better equipped to solve the task that way 
and provide input that is actually useful to us.   
12. I: [Reading out loud S5] You acted openly. Was the main reason to attract the mentioned lead users? Did you 
consider different reasons?  
13. OIL: The main reason was that it was also marketing to students and creating positive touchpoints with them. 
Thus, it was not anonymous for branding reasons, but you are also right. I am a big supporter of transparency and 
if ideas are really good they should get the platform. Thus, principles and values are another reason.   
14. I: Marketing is also a common secondary goal in crowdsourcing. How important were secondary goals in 
comparison?  
==PAGE:234==
225 
 
15. OIL: Approximately equally important. Maybe not for UNIQA as a company but for UNIQA as an employer. 
We also considered the contest as research. We read between the lines to receive information and discover 
underlying needs.   
16. I: There is a difference between the number of solvers and the number of contributions as KPIs for 
participation. How much did you think about that? What were your KPIs? 
17. OIL: We did not think about it in that way. But I guess we considered the number of solvers for marketing 
reasons. From an innovation perspective, it does not matter to me. If I knew that a correlation exists between the 
number of ideas and the quality of ideas, I would consider it. I could imagine that solvers that contribute many 
ideas go more in-depth than others and their later ideas might be better. However, I did not look at that and we 
also did not have any goals for either contributions or solvers.  
18. I: [Reading out loud S6] Let`s start off very general: How was your evaluation process?  
19. OIL: Students from the university chose somewhere between 10 and 20 contributions. I and a few jury members 
looked at them and chose their favorites. Finally, in a jury session, we evaluated them based on a few criteria.  
20. I: How would you describe the resource investment for the evaluation? 
21. OIL: The students took over most of the (annoying) work, which made it quite easy for us. I totally agree with 
your approach. In this case, they were not crowd members but students, because they fitted our target group quite 
well. Thus, we let the target group pre-select the contributions.   
22. I: Did you have the fear of losing good contributions in the process?  
23. OIL: No. But it depends on how much weight you put on the output of the contest. For us, crowdsourcing was 
only one of many tools that we used to generate input and inspiration for our innovation process. It is different if 
you put all eggs in one basket. I am a big fan of very much considering the costs against the value. It would have 
been unproportionate for me to go through all 55 contributions. I had a very practical approach.   
24. I: Meaning, you would consider solver evaluations? 
25. OIL: Yes, but it obviously depends on the task. One needs to consider who has the ability to appropriately 
assess the contributions based on the evaluation criteria. If one wants to evaluate market fit or market need, the 
target group is the best judge. If it is rather technical one needs at least lead users or experts for the evaluation. If 
the solvers are also future users it is one of the best ways to include them in the evaluation.  
26. I: [Introducing the Framework Slide] Let`s start general again: How did you decide on using crowdsourcing?  
27. OIL: In this case, it was part of the cooperation with the university. Otherwise, I would always do it when one 
wants to address a crowd and generate some insights from it. Especially from the target group. Thus, I think it is 
useful as a research tool.  
28. I: Which other decision factors would you consider?  
29. OIL: Superficiality. Crowdsourcing in its classical sense on a platform does not directly address lead users. 
They are just people that like to solve tasks. There are better ways to reach lead users. If you want ideas without 
depth from many average people, basically just to gather topics.   
30. I: In general, would you consider using frameworks or do you decide based on gut feeling?  
31. OIL: No, I would not say gut feeling. I think it is quite logical. I consider the output goal and the innovation 
stage. There might be an underlying framework but it is just based on knowledge for me. I also consider who has 
the ability to innovate at that stage. If the crowd does not have the capabilities, it is better to gather information 
from potential users and let designers work based on that. I`d call it common sense. I would always consider where 
I could find the capabilities. Lead users are somewhere in between market research and crowdsourcing. They have 
an outside perspective, but they also have skills comparable to professionals.  
32. I: Thus, you did not perform crowdsourcing as broadly and also not in the usual sense (trying to get solutions 
from distant fields)? 
33. OIL: Yes 
==PAGE:235==
226 
 
34. I: Lead users are very close to the ecosystem and the focus might be quite narrow compared to crowdsourcing 
in its original sense.  
35. OIL: Yes. Well, actually, I don`t agree, because the focus is on competencies not on the industry. Basic 
competencies like visualization. People from different industries have acquired such skills. The distance to the 
task`s industry is very important for innovation. However, the solvers need specific capabilities. Not everyone can 
develop drugs if they don`t have any chemical or lab training. And even if it is less complex, some capabilities are 
always required. Everyone can talk about such things but not everyone has the expertise to be innovative. Radical 
innovation always needs expertise in my opinion. Only they can see new ways.  
36. I: In my opinion, seekers find such solvers by trying to reach as many as possible and increasing the chance 
to attract useful ones. It is basically about luck, but seekers can increase their chances. Thus, an indirect way of 
contacting capable solvers.   
37. OIL: Yes, that is true. The seeker is only to some degree in control of attracting the right people. However, we 
did not want to attract many more people (e.g., with monetary rewards). We specifically aimed at solvers that have 
intrinsic motivation and a connection to the topic. They have a higher chance of knowing the topic out of interest. 
This is more in the direction of profiling. Thus, figuring out what an ideal solver would look like and considering 
possibilities to attract such solvers. I would be very interested to know how many truly radical innovations stem 
from broad crowdsourcing. Not just the ones that have been marketed as such by companies, even though they 
worked on similar things for years. I think that this is the norm. But how many led to true radical innovations?  
38. I: Probably not many. Especially as crowdsourcing is usually done in early innovation stages and finding 
radical innovations on the basis of innovative processes might be difficult in general.  
39. OIL: Yes, definitely.  
40.  I: I argue that your focus on lead users undermines the benefits of distant fields, as lead users are usually very 
close to the ecosystem.  
41. OIL: Yes that is true. However, I think you have to suffer too much in crowdsourcing either way. Thus, it was 
also never my favorite choice, because, in the end, you are right. It can be very much questioned if that is more 
efficient than just asking employees, which have probably more competencies and are closer to the company. So, 
yes, I completely agree with you. Well, I never found a use case where crowdsourcing was the best option. Most 
ideas have already internally occurred before.  
42. I: You could have also asked the wrong questions.  
43. OIL: Yes, it could be. If I experience a successful crowdsourcing campaign from an innovation standpoint by 
anyone (us or competition) and they attract truly innovative contributions, I would probably also try it again. 
However, I won`t force it and, as said before, we don`t have much experience with it.  
44. I: Was the contest successful? Which goals were reached?  
45. OIL: Yes, there was actually one interesting food for thought among the winning contributions. We follow 
that actually to some degree. It is an interesting food for thought. Thus, it was successful for me. However, I 
could not say if this is only an effect of the contest or of the cooperation with the university, which was 
refreshing. We worked one semester with young students and looked at the topic. It obviously brought up new 
perspectives different from the internal ones. It was successful to generate a picture of the general mood and to 
get new impulses. That are the two success criteria. What would we change next time? [Referring to the slides, 
AN] I don`t know. We had low expectations. Thus, also no clear improvement points.  
46. I: Do you think that the input would have been better if you had attracted more contributions/solvers?  
47. OIM: No, I don`t think so. It would be interesting to map the contributions somehow to topics like a heat 
map to discover which topics had many contributions because we got many similar solutions. If you increase the 
number of contributions significantly, you obviously also increase the chance for a lucky punch, a really creative 
idea. In general, I think that the ideation phase is overrated in innovation. And, as you said, that ist he phase in 
which crowdsourcing thrives. I think, it is way more important how one combines the ideas and thoughts, how 
one tests the app, how you develop it further, and how one includes different perspectives. The idea is definitely 
important and starts the process, but I think that anything that happens afterward is even more important. But this 
==PAGE:236==
227 
 
is an almost philosophical question. The work starts after the ideation. I think that the focus and weight of ideas 
can be too much.  
48. I: Would you say that your task was not 100% suited for crowdsourcing?  
49. OIL: I don`t know. I did not want to do it. As I said, I did not yet come up with a task that is more suited for 
crowdsourcing than for any other innovation method. Thus, it is difficult. It might be a good method if one 
invests the time and effort to really reach the appropriate solvers. Then, it might work. However, the common 
approaches are in the end just random. 
50. [Clarifying how the Interviewee wants to be cited] 
51. OIL: I think the whole innovation scene focuses too much on methods and too little on content. Everyone 
discusses the methods. However, it is way too little to discuss which competencies one needs, which 
environment, and how to create a human-centered approach. These are the topics that are often disregarded. 
Everyone focuses on the methods, as they are structured and tangible. They are sort of a comfort zone. However, 
innovation is the exact opposite: leaving the comfort zone and trying things in a new way. Well, I´d be also 
really interested to read your thesis after you finished it.    
52. [Thank You and Farewell]
